# 吕祖全传

*The Complete Biography of Patriarch Lü*
*Revised by Wang Xiangxu, Disciple of the Dao*

*The Complete Biography of Tang Hongren’s Universally Beneficent, Trustworthy, and Protective Emperor Lord Pure Yang Lü the Immortal*
Compiled by Lü the Immortal of Pure Yang,
Revised by Wang Xiangxu, styled Danyi Zi, a devoted disciple of the Dao,
Collated by fellow Daoists He Yingchun, Wu Daolong, Fei Qin, Zheng Rucheng, Zhong Shan, and Zha Zongqi

My surname is Lü, my given name Yan, my courtesy name Dongbin, and my sobriquet Pure Yang. Originally, I hailed from Luoxia in Henan. My great-grandfather Yi, fleeing a feud, settled in the old Huoshui Village of central Guangdong, where he fathered Xian and Zhuo. Xian begot me, Yan, while Zhuo died young.

My father, in his youth, studied for the imperial examinations but met with no success. Turning to household affairs, he instructed his son in the classics. On the night before my birth, a Daoist—clad in a yellow turban and black robe, his beard and hair coiled like dragons, his temples streaked with white—leaned upon an iron-tipped staff, a gourd slung at his side, and chanted as he wandered the streets:

*The clear wind drifts and drifts,*
*Brushing my robes.*
*The white clouds rise and rise,*
*Following me in flight.*
*The jade pendants chime and chime,*
*Descending from heaven’s thoroughfare.*
*They bear this soul away,*
*To the land of mugwort and reeds.*
*One day, returning,*
*We shall meet in the realm of nothingness.*

My father, recognizing him as a man of the Dao, invited him home and offered a vegetarian feast. The Daoist drew from his sleeve a medicine and said, “Your wife, née Wang, will go into labor at the hour of the rat tomorrow. Give her this medicine, and she will bear a son.” My father bowed in gratitude. Suddenly, the Daoist dissolved into a gust of pure wind and vanished, leaving behind a poem upon the table.

The poem read:

*A minor Daoist from Zhongnan,*
*Brings you immortal spirits—*
*His name is Yan, of the mountain crags.*
*A guest in the grotto’s halls.*

At midnight the following day, the child was indeed born, filling the air with rare fragrance for ten *li* around, while a long rainbow descended and purple light swirled about the door. The hour of his birth was the fourth day of the eighth month in the second year of the Zhenguan reign of the Tang, during the hour of the rat. From infancy, he was keen and quick-witted; by his first year, he could recite the *Book of Songs* and read the *Book of Documents*, understanding filial piety and brotherly respect. He never ate before his elders, never walked ahead of his seniors, never spoke before being addressed, never laughed before others, never displayed anger before his parents. His parents cherished him. At the age of five, his father, recalling the Daoist’s words, named him Yan.

For three years, he studied by lamplight, mastering the *Three Ritual Classics*, the *Five Canons*, and the works of the Hundred Schools without omission. His teacher marveled at him. One day, during a gathering of scholars, the teacher tested them with the theme *"The Beauty of the East."* No sooner had the topic been announced than I took up my brush and composed the following on the spot:

*The fine night trims the silver lamp bright,*
*A precious mirror hangs high, ten thousand *li* clear.*
*Why need the flute to summon the phoenix?*
*Purple void drifts down with the chime of pendants.*

*In the chime of pendants, her song is artful,*
*Mid-heaven’s steps tread on golden hooks so slight.*
*Fusang steals forth from the crystal palace,*
*Guanghan pacts in secret to wander quiet.*

*That year, they spoke not of Wu’s bestowal,*
*Today, cease boasting of Chu’s fair maid.*
*Her heart yields not to the dream of Wu Mountain,*
*Her grace still mirrors Guye’s screen.*

*From a kingdom that topples cities, the beauty comes,*
*Yet the beauty does not topple—men are ensnared.*
*Let others’ passionate eyes stir emotion,*
*I have an iron heart, firmer than stone.*

*All her thousand graces are but demons’ guile,*
*Her laughing mouth, her delicate limbs—all ravagers.*
*Daji and Bao Si ruined the Nine Lands,*
*Lust for beauty is truly to be loathed.*

*I shall nurture my true qi, self-contained,*
*Through all the springs and autumns, joy unbounded.*

My teacher fell silent, already sensing my aspirations beyond the clouds, though he concealed it. The following year, he arranged a marriage for me from among the candidates on the screen. I showed reluctance, and my parents were displeased, so I reluctantly agreed. I was thus betrothed to the daughter of Eunuch Liu, the Commandant, and though the marriage was consummated, we did not become intimate. Three years later, she bore a child.

It was the day my parents celebrated their seventieth birthdays, and guests were invited to offer toasts. As the third round of drinks was served, three beggars entered the hall—wearing bamboo-leaf hats, clad in straw robes, barefoot, and carrying baskets. I angrily ordered them driven away, but they remained unmoved. My parents offered them food and drink, yet the beggars paid no heed. When asked their purpose, they replied, “We wish to beg alongside your son, Yan.” My father laughed, thinking them mad. My mother, growing rude, ordered the servants to beat and expel them. The beggars remained unperturbed. A burly, bearded man glared at them, and the crowd shrank back, none daring to approach. The beggars then clapped their hands and laughed loudly, singing a song:

*We’re roving men of the clouds,*
*Seeking a few bowls of Magu’s wine and rice.*
*We’ll not take your leftover cups or scraps,*
*Nor force your heart to comply.*
*We’ve learned to roam free, unbound,*
*Far better than your days of toil and strife.*

Then a broad-eyed, iron-faced man with a thick beard raised his chin and joined in:

*Laugh if you will at our beggar’s guise,*
*But know—we brothers are wild men of Zhongnan.*
*To enlighten the foolish, we evade fate’s net,*
*Descend from the clouds to cast aside a guest.*
*You call us drifting duckweed, rootless reeds—*
*Yet who rules the floating world?*

Then an elderly man, white-haired and gaunt, holding a pair of clappers, struck the bamboo and sang:

Alas, we’ve cast aside the snares of gain,
adrift upon the Weak Water’s span.
We’ve placed the cosmos, sun, and moon within a reed’s embrace,
all for the sake of that young man of grace.
Thus do we seek to lift and guide,
to conjure wine and food beside.
Yet fear we parting’s bitter sting—
to meet again would cost remembering.

At that moment, I knew they were no common sort.
I stepped forward and bowed, offering a meal, which they declined.
The bearded one said, “One day on the road to Handan, we shall meet again.
Bring forth a dou of wine for us three then.”
I assented. Turning my head, they suddenly became three birds,
soaring southward and gone.

The feast ended, the guests dispersed,
yet I, filled with longing, could not rest.
Sleepless, I took up Wu paper and dipped my brush of Guan,
composing a *ci* titled *“Gazing at the Cloud Lords.”* The lyrics read:

*In the bright hall, spring’s long day,
twin stars appear in radiant display.
As songs and flutes resound the feast,
auspicious mist swirls in the east.
Sunlight through parasol leaves and daylilies gleams,
wind toys with dancing sleeves and flying sashes’ seams.
We raise jade cups to Mount Song’s height,
wishing boundless years of endless light.*

*From whence came these divine beings,
transformed as beggars on the way?*
*Their pure voices worthy of praise,*
*their gaunt forms lovelier each day.*
*Like the nine-jointed atractylis, tall and bright,*
*their noble spirits soar in flight.*
*I long to follow them away—*
*cross Wu Gorge, sail Penglai’s bay.*

*Yet who knows the dusty ties not yet undone,*
*the debts of past lives still to run?*
*I remain at my desk, my heart in vain,*
*my dreaming soul wearied again.*

Unsated still, I then composed a regulated verse. The poem reads:

The peacock screen unfolds—daylight lingers long,
a jeweled censer sends coiled incense, rare fragrance strong.
The east wind lifts the curtain, Jade Pool’s auspice bright,
from the Southern Pole, cups pass the Sea-House’s brew in light.
A pair of cranes, unhurried, bear ripe peaches in their beaks,
the Three Stars from afar herald chrysanthemums’ first peaks.
When men return beyond the ninefold heavens’ clouded height,
how many geese in misty haze stretch into fading light?

The moon sank past the eastern peaks, stars streamed o’er the briny deep,
magpies startled into flight, village cocks their dawn-song keep.
Just then, my noble friend in sable cloak sent word to roam
the Han River’s waves—my carefree lad readied me to come.
Our boat moored midstream, my golden friend raised his cup in toast.
Among the guests sat Li Yuanhan, a man of old renown, who hailed:
“Next year’s grand examination—I’ve seen an omen in the night:
Brother Lü, you’ll surely mount the dragon’s head in flight.”
I smiled and said, “Your heart outstrips mine, thus the omen’s true.
If I indeed succeed, I’ll hook a fish when we cast through.”
We three in jest cast lines—mine snared a golden carp that day,
while neither of my friends drew aught from the water’s sway.

My golden friend then composed a lyric, offering wine in celebration:
*Spring’s examination hall, orioles urge in hushed refrain,
fish and dragons from the four seas return in turn again.
All vie to leap the Dragon Gate, transformed by fate’s decree,
awaiting the light wave’s thunderous roar to set them free.*
*Warm peach waves flow, green ripples glide,*
*golden scales and fins drift far and wide.*
*Seizing the dragon’s head, soaring the azure height,*
*all will hail the returned top scholar’s light.*

I drained my cup and matched his verse in reply:
*The midday clepsydra fades, red headscarf urges near,*
*idly donning court robes, listening for phoenixes’ return to hear.*
*My talents, long prepared, now test their mettle true,*
*once roused the spring river’s thunder, as in days of yore I knew.*
*Heroes arise, all follow in train,*
*phoenix towers and golden chariots their paths sustain.*
*Let deeds of merit grace the annals’ page,*
*with purple sashes woven through embroidered robes of state’s sage.*

We exchanged more toasts and jests, when suddenly my carefree lad cried out:
“A green bird comes from the west—its hue like kingfisher’s dye,
its form vast as a chariot wheel, its cry deep as a bronze bell’s chime.
It soared to mid-heaven, then transformed into a green-robed maid,
holding a waist drum, singing Daoist songs unafraid.”
We three at first disbelieved, but listened in quietude.
Soon, its sound rang clear as chimes, its rhythm slow and subdued—
pure as a light breeze stirring a delicate spring’s flow,
distant as a fisherman’s song fading where the deep waters go,
resonant as an empty valley answering the phoenix’s call.

The music swelled—now joyous enough to stir laughter, now sorrowful enough to draw tears. We lifted the boat’s canopy and gazed upward: the maiden rose and descended thrice, approached and retreated twice, while clouds now gathered, now parted, now soared, now stilled. The three of us knelt and beckoned her. After a brief pause, she struck her waist drum and sang:

*"Drifting free, unbound, beyond the red dust’s snare—*
*no worldly cares to bind or bear.*
*Magu’s rice in one bowl, mugwort and lotus my sash and gown.*
*On the long road, astride my blue ox, I roam—*
*all I know is ease, untroubled, alone.*
*Laugh at you, chasing fame and gain in vain,*
*scheming, grasping, never content—*
*hoard a thousand taels of gold, yet still you crave more.*
*Toiling, fretting, gray-haired at last—*
*what was it all for?"*

I offered her fine wine. With a sweep of her sleeve, she soared into the air and sang again:

*"Rolling waves, surging dust—*
*laugh at your little boat tossed and thrust.*
*How can one pole withstand the river’s gale?*
*Soon the wind will seize your sail,*
*and though you long to turn to shore,*
*you’ll share the gulls’ fate no more.*
*What choice have you?*
*No escape from the dream of Huainan’s court!*
*Cast off the cage, I say,*
*kick away the hollow play.*
*Set your heart on the Elixir’s round—*
*only when you reach the Dao’s shore*
*will this vessel be yours forevermore."*

Her voice faded into the distance, her form vanished without trace. She left behind a table, on which lay a single gatha:

*"Listen well to my words,*
*turn not toward the imperial court.*
*Hasten back to Handan’s crossroads,*
*and feast with me on mist and cloud."*

I performed the esoteric mudra she had shown me, yet failed to grasp her meaning. My companions, now deep in their cups, remained oblivious. As dusk fell, we turned the boat homeward. My parents stood at the gate, watching as I entered with joy. I recounted the tale, but my mother did not believe it.

The following winter, my mother fell ill with a fever. I forsook food and sleep, day and night, praying to the mountain spirit to take her place—yet she did not recover. I offered sacrifices to the Dipper—still no cure. I cut flesh from my thigh, burned incense, and her condition briefly improved. But the fever lodged in her chest and belly, and she longed for water. The local springs were parched, their waters foul; only the Han River ran clear, though its distant, silt-choked banks made drawing water arduous. I fetched it myself, nearly swept away by the waves several times, yet still it could not quench her thirst. That night, I prayed at the Dragon King’s shrine. Suddenly, a dry well in the temple courtyard gushed forth sweet spring water. Each day, I drew from it for my mother, and within ten days, her illness was gone. To this day, the Spring of Master Lü remains, and people call it the fruit of filial devotion.

During the years after the Zhenguan era, our prefecture suffered poor harvests. The people reaped nothing, yet tax collectors pressed urgently, leaving the poor in dire straits. My family had stored ten thousand *hu* of grain. My father and I deliberated and resolved: all those unable to meet the tax were to be supplied by us. We exhausted our stores to aid them, saving tens of thousands of lives. The authorities, learning of this, bestowed upon our village the banner of "Righteousness."

The next year, in the *dingmao* cycle, the imperial examination approached. The prefecture nominated me. My parents urged me to prepare, and the carefree lad was tasked with carrying my luggage. I bade farewell to my lofty curtains and fragrant chamber, then set forth. My young wife, well-versed in classics and history, composed a poem to send me off:

*"Spare not the roadside blossoms—*
*one glance back, and they lie at heaven’s edge.*
*The east wind, cruel, scatters the wandering rider,*
*staining him with mad fragrance, leaving only sighs.*
*Only sighs—*
*how vainly your soul will wander,*
*circling the Milky Way’s raft.*
*When you stand on the Dragon’s Roll, boast if you must,*
*with golden saddle, jade bridle, and black gauze cap.*
*Once the emperor’s favor graces you,*
*return home swiftly—*
*do not drift like a reed on the waves,*
*lest I gaze in vain toward the horizon’s end."*

I took my leave and journeyed on. At Hengpu, I hired a boat. There, I encountered an old fisherman in a small skiff, singing the *"Song of Canglang"*:

*"Clad in green straw cloak, bamboo hat upon my brow,*
*at dawn I pole into the willow-flower isle.*
*In the misty river, my long rod I cast—*
*only fear the fish won’t take the hook.*
*Won’t take the hook, oh won’t take the hook,*
*leaving me to drown in endless sorrow.*
*Not that I crave wealth or gain,*
*but dread the fate that may be yours in the end."*

I called to him, but he did not draw near. Instead, he dipped his oars and drifted toward the depths of the cloud-wreathed waves, singing again:

*"Misty waters stretch endlessly, the wind so clear,*
*a single boat holds all my joy.*
*Look at those chasing fame and rank,*
*clutching at wealth and power, hearts never full.*
*Some beg in rags for kindness,*
*some seek high office with flattery,*
*some speak bluntly to the lord,*
*some fawn on rulers through their maids.*
*They dream of glory for a thousand ages—*
*yet know this: after death, only an empty name remains.*
*Only an empty name, oh only an empty name!*
*Better to cast off this red dust,*
*and spend my days by rivers and lakes,*
*hooking fish and crabs, drinking village wine till I’m drunk,*
*never once looking back."*

He vanished into the distance. I crossed the river in another boat.

As spring’s first light grew radiant, jade split the indigo fields; willows unfurled along the banks, orioles trilled their clever tunes, and swallows darted like swift shears. Fragrant carriages set noblewomen’s wheels in motion, while fine steeds bore the reins of young lords. Suddenly, thoughts of my homeland surged within me, and gazing at the white clouds, I wept until tears streamed down.

A carefree youth approached and said: *"Master, you grieve—why not rest? Why do you wander now? Why have you left your parents, abandoned your wife and children? This journey of yours brings honor to your kin, brings honor to your family—yet with one honor, a thousand humiliations fade. What cause for sorrow remains? Master, rest!"*

Between my sobs, the heaviness in my heart would not relent. Was I moved by mere self-love? No—my parents were aged, their warmth and care now sparse. Still, the youth’s words held merit, and my spirit felt a fleeting ease.

As I walked through the greenwood path, I encountered a youth like the rogue of Huaiyin’s marketplace, blocking my way with no passage allowed. My servant and I pleaded endlessly, emptying our purses to him, keeping only our zither and sword. The rest he seized in one sweep, sparing us only our breath. With our traveling funds exhausted, how could we continue our journey?

The youth begged for food while I carried my zither and books. Along the road, we met the same beggar from my home—a burly, whiskered man—who laughed and said: *"Scholar, scholar! Once I begged at your door, and you drove me away; your parents drove me away too. Now you are driven by others as well. Back then, the three of us wanted to beg with you, but you, a rich young master, scoffed: ‘What business have the wealthy with begging?’ Today, why not stay home and enjoy your fortune? Why beg like us? Hah!"*

Silent, I felt a deep shame, having forgotten my past deeds.

The burly man drew from his basket a bowl of rice, its stench unbearable—maggots writhing in the thin gruel. He said to me, *"Eat this, and now you and I are of the same company."* I knit my brows, furrowing my forehead, refusing to look. The man gathered it up and left.

My hunger gnawed fiercely, but the youth returned with a bowl of parched grain, which I ate to fill my stomach, thinking no more of food. When I asked where he had found it, he told me it was from the same burly man.

We pressed onward with all haste, our suffering beyond words. Arriving at Puyin Village, we found three diverging paths, no human trace in sight—only the crisscrossing tracks of beasts and birds, leaving us at a loss for direction. We rested beneath an ancient locust tree, glad for the gentle breeze that revived our spirits. In the distance, we heard a herdsman’s song:

*The mountain flowers bloom,
The oriole’s cry is drawn out.
Blowing a short flute, he steps over the layered ridges,
Riding a young calf, he roams the hidden valleys,
Seeing the four wilds so still and quiet.
Still and quiet—
No cares, no troubles,
No black, no white.
The heart wanders free,
Singing a song of the lotus fall,
Forgetting the world is small.*

*The pine shade thickens,
The fiery clouds fade.
He taps out the last moves on a stone chessboard,
Plays a bamboo flute with no fixed tune—
What care has he for the world’s cruel turns?
The cool wind stirs,
The bamboo rustles.
Watching the kites and fish fill his gaze,
Heaven’s work is revealed,
The mystery at every turn.
The golden breeze fills the ridge,
Maple leaves blush red.
A tung leaf drifts lightly down,
The autumn cricket’s lonely cry—
How pitiable is man, a drifting twig.*

*A drifting twig—
When will the dream end?
One must look within.
Why waste the spirit,
Draining life’s essence,
Like a withered leaf’s root?*

*Crimson clouds fill the sky,
Plum blossoms shatter jade.
How many warm chambers with red braziers?
How many wives wail, children weep?
Laugh at the futile bustle of the human world.
The human world’s bustle—
When will it be enough?
Impermanence hurries near.
Slowly, slowly,
The temples whiten—
Can gold and silver buy them back?*

I listened to the voice but saw no figure. I sent the youth to follow the sound, but after a long while, he did not return.

Suddenly, a perfected one appeared—clad in a patchwork robe of a hundred hues, his hair coiled in twin topknots, his beard thick upon his cheeks, his eyes like an ancient dragon’s, his earlobes hanging to his shoulders. His feet were shod in hempen sandals with many loops, a yellow sash bound his waist, and from it hung a gourd and an indigo pouch. In his hand, he held a palm-fiber whisk, its heart long gone. Grinning, he strode before me, then fixed me with a glare and barked: *"Scholar, why do you tarry? The sky darkens, and I know well this place—by day, it teems with brigands; by night, with tigers and wolves. This is no safe haven for rest."*

I rose and bowed deeply. The man at once sat down, facing me knee-to-knee, silent as if in dhyāna. After a long while, I respectfully addressed him, but the master kept his gaze steady upon me and asked: *"Where are you bound?"*

I answered that I journeyed to take the imperial examinations.

The master said: *"Young and learned—just the sort to serve the Son of Heaven above and succor the common folk below, that we humble ones might bask in your beneficence. A fortunate encounter indeed! Yet I have one word for you—remember it well."*

I nodded, ready to receive his teaching.

The master said:

"The winds and waves are cruel, oh so cruel,
In the arena of fame and gain, stand firm of foot.
Beware missteps on the path ahead—
Once you lose this root-sprout,
Ten thousand turns, a thousand days will fail to grasp it.

Go home, go home—take heed in earnest!
Come, come—like a cicada shedding its husk.
Break through the maze of delusion and leap beyond,
By the Golden Weighted Mountain, you’ll find your descent.

I then inquired of my future path: *'Master, do you know?'*
He only shook his head and said:
*The road ahead, the road ahead—
Ten thousand miles of soaring flight, no delay.
A life entrusted to the royal house,
Through life and death, no thought for self.
Since ancient times, loyalty has perished in the cauldron’s depths—
Heroes are but hares in snares.
What use are posthumous inscriptions to the world?
Wealth and rank are but dew on the mugwort.
The eastern wilds’ mounds rise steep and high,
How many pearl-adorned scholars lie within?
I wearied of his words, but the master said: *'Why not wander the clouds with me?
So much joy to be had!'*

I laughed: *'Master, you err.
You dine on coarse grains,
Feast on wild mountain mugwort,
Drink from stone-chiseled streams,
Clothe yourself in tattered hemp,
Have neither parents nor wife,
No lofty halls nor grand estates,
No friends nor companions,
No kin nor visitors,
No servants to attend you—
Where is the joy in that?'*

The master covered his mouth and chuckled: *'Let me tell you of joy.'*
In an instant, he drew a *yugu* drum from his sack and sang a *daoqing* ballad. The lyrics went:"

"Our coarse grains outshine lamb and mutton,
Our ferns and mugwort surpass a hundred fragrant flavors.
The stone-stream’s spring we drink—
No need for grape wine here.
Our patchwork robes suffice—
No silks or brocades for us.
A single hut of stone walls and thatched eaves
Outdoes those towering halls a hundred *zhang* long.
We read not a few lines of books,
Nor know of emperors or kings.
What care we for Qin’s strength or Chu’s weakness, their struggles for supremacy?
What care we for Han’s rise and fall, its glory and ruin?
We delight in deer and cranes,
At times spreading blue clouds as our hundred passes.
Sometimes we ride a light boat to roam the seas,
Sometimes mount a small crane for leisurely flights,
Sometimes take the form of an ordinary man,
Sometimes conceal ourselves as things unseen.
The cosmos traversed without restraint—
Who governs this carefree wandering?
No yin or yang,
No short or long.
No turning of heaven or overturning of earth to mark growth and decline,
No past or present to distinguish soft and hard.
We’ve refined a single golden elixir—
Though rocks crumble and rivers run dry, our nature endures forever."

I deemed it idle talk and laughed, turning to leave.
Just then, a carefree youth arrived, urging us onward.

The master said, “Why do you travel with an empty satchel? I have a pillow here—when folded, it measures no more than an inch, yet when unfurled, it stretches nearly three feet. Most convenient for the road. Would you like it?” I declined, saying we had only just met; how could I accept such a gift? The master replied, “Even strangers who meet by chance may share a moment’s intimacy, as the ancients did. Why refuse?” He reached into his bag and drew it out—a shape like folded bamboo, barely an inch long—and handed it to me. I accepted with thanks.

The master said, “The three-forked path made Mozi weep. Now you, too, are nearly lost. Shall I guide you?” He led me to a carriage, and we traveled a little over a *li*. Suddenly, the master vanished. Panicked, I pressed on another *li* or so, hunger and thirst gnawing at me. The carefree youth lagged behind, struggling to keep pace.

In the distance, a blue banner fluttered above a thatched eave, and a yellow bird sang near an apricot shop—this was an inn run by a woman of Wu. I wished to eat, but what of the coins at the end of my staff? I consulted the youth, who produced something he had found along the way. I said, “The Way does not tolerate theft; the Greedy Spring is shunned by the upright; a filial son avoids the village named ‘Unfilial.’ How could I, for the sake of my belly, commit such an unrighteous act?” The youth laughed and replied, “Refusing a coat is a trivial matter, but distrust is no virtue in an elder. Besides, in matters of life and death, to harm oneself over such scruples is to fail in filial piety toward one’s parents and loyalty to one’s lord. Loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and righteousness—these are all one and the same.” I heeded his words and, with reluctance, entered the shop.

I ordered a meal, but there was no rice left. I asked for it to be cooked, and the innkeeper soaked some millet to prepare it. Exhaustion weighed on my mind and body; I sought a pillow. The innkeeper apologized, saying none were available. I tossed and turned, restless, unable to settle. Then I remembered the pillow the Quanzhen master had given me. I took it out and unfolded it—over three feet long, soft and fragrant, perfectly comforting. With one pillow, I found peace.

I arrived directly in the capital and submitted my name at the prime minister’s gate. The next day, I entered the examination. The first session’s topic was *The Parrot’s Song*. My composition read:

In the second month, holding the essence of yang,
The chariot of Yifan appears in auspicious splendor,
Mountains and rivers nurture its spirit.
Emerald feathers drift across the azure sky,
Crimson mane stirs propitious clouds.
From distant lands it comes,
Reaching the maple palace of the court.
Eloquent and wise, it surpasses all winged creatures,
No envy for the phoenix of Zhou’s sacred ridge,
Freely soaring beyond the orioles of lofty trees.
It feasts on the celestial kitchen’s delicacies,
Sated with the emperor’s finest treasures.
Freed from its cage, it roams the four seas,
Soaring wherever it pleases.

The second session’s topic was a regulated verse on *The Peony*. My composition read:

The gentle breeze first stirs the yellow bird’s song,
Beneath the brocade curtains, its hues abound.
Dancing in the wind, its grace rivals Jin Gu’s gardens,
Dew-kissed and delicate, its emerald branches gleam.
White as dawn’s moon, it sheds its light powder,
Red as spring’s rosy clouds, veiled in sheer gauze.
Wealth and splendor it monopolizes—
Do not refuse to face it, drunk with flushed cheeks.

The third session’s topic was *Rhapsody on the Autumn Toad’s Shadow and Osmanthus*. My composition read:

The time is the eighth month, the season of metal’s softness.
Zhu Rong unyokes his chariot, the Quail Fire flows.
The qi of autumn presses upon the isles,
The White Emperor mounts his steed in the western realm.
The Water Sovereign responds to the tides,
The metal’s sound stirs the barren mounds.
The Silver River pours high,
The jade pestle’s notes linger long.
Its pure light illumines the nine wilds,
The clear hue fills the imperial tower.
It mirrors the mother-of-pearl gates of the limpid river,
Pierces the pearl-screened hooks of the shrimp.
Born of the night’s brightness,
It shatters the darkness of ten thousand ages.
Behold that vast orb,
Gazing back through a thousand autumns.
Its shadow sways in uneven grace,
Now vast and drifting, now sinking and floating.
Some cling to mud for sustenance,
Others wander with the pale moon goddess.
Then comes the *ding-ding* of axes felling trunks,
Suddenly the sound drifts downstream.
The steps ripple with light,
The jade palace coils like a dragon.
Now radiant, now dimming,
It gathers and withdraws.
Fragrance scatters, blossoms burst,
Like plucking clouds’ crowns.
A single branch, loftily grasped,
Soars the roc’s path at will.
Entering the pure void of Guanghan,
Lingering with Heng’e.
Jade pendants chime in pursuit,
Drawing the celestial winds of the nine provinces.
Facing the honored lord in toasts,
Clad in court robes and sable fur.
The examination concluded, the Minister recorded me as first,
Summoning me for an imperial audience.
I was bestowed palace flowers and a crimson robe,
Feasted in the Apricot Grove.
After passing, I strolled the grand avenues,
Where I encountered the daughter of Prime Minister Wen,
Who betrothed me to her household.
I declined repeatedly,
But the emperor transmitted his command—I dared not refuse.
Thus, I reluctantly complied.
Soon after the wedding, I was appointed Prefect of Yuzhou.
Accompanied by my wife’s family, I traveled by single carriage,
The road lined with well-wishers.
I upheld justice with unwavering resolve,
Never bending for personal gain,
Uprooting the powerful and ruthless without fear of the privileged.
Noble kinsmen withdrew their hands.
There was one notorious villain, long engaged in land seizures,
A leader of the *xia* underworld.
His gang of ten terrorized the prefecture.
Upon my arrival, they offered ten thousand gold as tribute.
I rebuked them, refusing even a thread.
Realizing they were outlaws, I summoned them for interrogation.
Within days, reports of their crimes numbered in the hundreds.
I ordered their arrest, but none dared act.
I secretly shelved the matter,
While the gang watched for an opening, attempting bribes.
I feigned friendship.
One day, I hosted a banquet for the leader,
Who arrived gleefully with his ten followers.
Before the wine had circulated,
I shouted—my attendants, a hundred strong, seized them all,
And they were silently executed.
Thus, the entire prefecture trembled.
After five years in office, I fathered two sons.
Upon returning to the capital for evaluation, I was ranked highest,
Promoted to Surveillance Commissioner,
Wielding the executioner’s axe without seeking approval.
From then on, I lived in luxury,
Adorning myself in fine robes,
Wearing the *zhiguan* cap,
My bearing surpassing all at court.
Onlookers dared not lift their gaze,
And I was called "Iron-Faced Lord Li."
While touring Xu and Yang provinces,
I captured thirteen thousand bandits,
Dismissed one hundred thirty corrupt officials,
And demolished over six thousand illicit shrines.
Wherever I passed, none failed to tremble in fear.
After another five years of inspections,
My eldest son, Yinlang, was nine,
And my second son, Chelang, was seven.
Upon returning to the capital,
The emperor’s countenance shone with delight.
He praised me with kind words,
Bestowed gold and silk beyond measure,
Hosted a banquet in the Wenyuan Pavilion,
And promoted me to Military Commissioner of Henan Circuit,
Ennobling me as Duke of Jing.
My wife became the Lady of Jing,
And my son was named Prefect of Yuzhou in title only.
After receiving my titles and passing the examination,
I feasted with the Wen family.
After three rounds of wine,
I could not help but think of my parents and Liu Shi,
Tears streaming down my face, unable to eat.
My wife inquired,
And I told her the truth.
She said, "What difficulty is there in this?"

The next day, I paid my respects to the emperor and presented my plea with sincerity, requesting a brief return to my hometown to visit my parents and bring them back to the capital to share in this prosperity. I also asked that Liu Shi be recognized as my primary wife. The emperor granted my request and bestowed upon me the privilege of swift travel by imperial post.

Returning to my homeland, I ascended the hall to pay respects to my parents and saw Liu Shi. Before I could even express my thoughts, local officials, old friends, and acquaintances filled the roads, their carriages and hats stretching as far as the eye could see. For over a month, there was no respite. Afterward, I tended to the ancestral graves, held feasts for all my relatives, and spent another month before formally requesting my parents to accompany me to the capital.

Thereupon, my entire family set out together. The journey took over half a month before we arrived at the capital and reached our residence. Lady Wen, leading our two sons, welcomed us joyfully. Upon entering the main hall, she first honored my parents with the highest seat and paid her respects. Next, Liu Shi was seated in the place of honor, receiving the full rites due to a primary wife. Then, my two sons were instructed to pay their respects to their grandparents and their legitimate mother.

A grand banquet was held, with music filling the hall and well-wishers arriving in succession. I shared a feast with Liu Shi to welcome her, and we rejoiced together in the same hall. With golden seals and purple sashes, we enjoyed all the wealth and honor the world could offer.

The emperor specially summoned my parents and elevated them to the titles of Duke and Duchess of Jing. He then urged me to assume my post. I departed the very next day.

A hundred *li* from my post, those who came to welcome me formed a forest of swords and halberds, clouds of banners, lightning-like armor, and ranks of soldiers and horses like scales. After residing at my post for over a month, just as I rejoiced in the peace and stability, urgent dispatches suddenly arrived: the Türks had invaded.

I hastily assembled thirty thousand troops, dividing them into two divisions, and marched out of Yu Pass, arranging them in the Nine Palaces Dark Lady formation. Li Ming served as the left vanguard, Shi Si as the right vanguard, Jia Chong as the scout general, Yuan Hongda as the left rear guard, Zhao Bi as the right rear guard, and Zu Sizeng as the central commander—all formidable generals.

Yet the Türks’ forces were overwhelmingly fierce. Li Ming advanced and said, "Commander, you must not underestimate the enemy. The nomadic cavalry excels in three strengths, while our forces excel in five. However, our five strengths are no match for their three. In the past, Emperor Taizong’s peril at the Muddy River could only be avenged by the courage of three arrows. Today, General, you should defeat them with strategy, as Jiezi captured Loulan or Ban Chao subdued the Western Regions. Victory can be achieved. If you rely on reckless bravery, I fear you will place a thousand *jun* upon a bird’s egg or drive a flock of sheep to chase a tiger—disaster is certain! I beg you to consider this."

I replied, "Have you not heard the maxim of military strategy? Know yourself and know your enemy, and you will never be defeated. Know neither, and you will be defeated in every battle. The extraordinary becomes the orthodox, and the orthodox becomes the extraordinary. First ensure you cannot be defeated, then wait for the enemy’s vulnerability. To seize the initiative with a display of might—this is the mark of a great general. Do you truly believe I do not heed your words?"

Thereupon, I positioned the left flank in ambush in the southeast corner, with soldiers holding wooden bits in their mouths and horses with reins in theirs. I gave the command: "Hide at the sound of the gong, advance at the sound of the fire signal. Li Ming shall lead this."

"One flank shall form a battle array in the shape of a Black Dragon’s Swirling Tail, with Shi Si at the head and Yuan Hongda at the tail. If the head is struck, the tail shall turn to aid it; if the tail is struck, the head shall turn to aid it. They are permitted only to defend each other, not to pursue victory. When the Hu cavalry breaks camp, upon the first blast of the horn, retreat a hundred paces; upon the second, retreat another hundred; at the third, turn and engage in full force, then flee swiftly toward the southeast."

"When the central army fires three cannon shots, the ambush troops shall rise as one. Those who have retreated shall turn back, closing the encirclement to strike. Any who disobey this command shall be beheaded. The order was given."

First, a single weary soldier was sent bearing a letter to the Türks, setting the time of engagement for the hour of *si* on the day *ding-si*—the third day after the camp was established. On the second day, the flags at the rear camp suddenly fluttered northward, indicating spies had scouted the encampment. I secretly went to investigate and indeed found them among the undergrowth—thieves stealing horse fodder. They were immediately executed, their heads impaled on poles.

On the third day, *ding-si*, the battle lasted nearly an hour. As previously ordered, the Hu cavalry broke camp and pursued our retreat, falling into our trap. Five of their chieftains were captured, ten thousand armored soldiers slain, and their supplies and baggage heaped like hills. We returned in triumph, singing victory songs. The entire campaign lasted little more than ten days.

The report was submitted to the sovereign, who bestowed upon me a thousand-mile steed, a ceremonial robe with jade fastenings, a cart of gold and pearls, ten thousand *jin* of silver, a thousand bolts of brocade, and elevated me to the rank of Prince, granting me a fiefdom. After five years in office, my son succeeded me in the post. I then resigned my title to retire in leisure, sending my son to present a memorial to the throne. The memorial read:

The humble and unworthy Minister Lü Yan, Military Commissioner of Henan Circuit, respectfully submits this petition to beg Your Majesty’s grace for leave to retire and care for my parents:

I prostrate myself in awe and reverence, kowtowing to submit this plea. The boundless grace of Heaven blankets all things, and the sagely virtue of the Emperor nurtures and sustains all life. At the dawn of this propitious era, the Son of Heaven, responding to the mandate of the age, governs the world, while wise ministers assist in perfecting the Dao of governance. Yet it is said that loyal ministers are found in the households of filial sons, and those who serve their lord must first fulfill their duty to their parents. The Wei founder acted without principle, leaving Xu You to hold empty worth; the Han emperor, though benevolent, left Wang Mang with lingering regret. Such failures bring no solace to a minister’s heart and only diminish the ruler’s virtue. Let the past serve as a warning, and may the future take heed.

Now, by the grace of Your Majesty, who unites Heaven and inaugurates the era, upholding the world with compassion, teaching filial piety and loyalty, the proper order of human relations is restored. You perfect one’s role as parent and as child, and all virtues are thereby fulfilled. The ancestral temple receives the utmost sincerity, and the memory of the late sovereign is honored with lifelong devotion. The eight directions rejoice in reverence, and the nine provinces exult in harmony.

I, your humble servant, am but a lowly rustic, a mere blade of grass at a humble gate. In youth, I received my father’s teachings and secretly emulated the rules of Confucius; in adulthood, though unworthy, I was entrusted with military command and had the fortune to witness the virtue of Emperor Shun. My labors in service, like those of a warhorse, have been exhausted, yet the rites of caring for my parents remain unfulfilled. In my official duties, I have striven to devote myself entirely—how dare I claim my loyalty is complete? Yet the grace bestowed upon me is boundless, and I long to fulfill my filial devotion. I humbly beg Your Majesty to extend Your vast benevolence, which honors the aged and cherishes the young, and grant me the means to provide for my parents in their final years, like the lamb kneeling to feed or the crow returning to feed its parent. Thus, my sovereign’s expectation of service will not be hindered by my foolish heart, and the twilight years of my parents will have a place of refuge.

I am overwhelmed with gratitude beyond measure. I have instructed my son, Yin, to present this petition for Your Majesty’s consideration. The memorial is respectfully submitted.

May the Emperor not grant my request.

After serving another five years, I was promoted to Military Commissioner of Heyang, where I achieved repeated military successes. My son Yin was appointed to succeed me in Hedao, while I was transferred to serve as Prefect of Yuzhou. I remained in Heyang for eight years, during which both my parents passed away. Though the post was a critical frontier defense, the Emperor did not permit me to observe mourning. One day, urgent reports arrived that the Western Qiang had invaded, allied with the Nüzhen, their combined forces numbering eight hundred thousand. I was in the midst of my parents’ funeral rites when, after a single moment’s delay, the enemy reached the pass. I hastily dispatched He Yan to meet them, but he was captured and beheaded, and twenty thousand of our soldiers were lost. By the time I rushed to the front, the raiders had already fled. The court dispatched five Commandants of the Imperial Guard to bind and escort me to the capital, my wife and children unaware of my fate. I prostrated myself before the Emperor and recounted the circumstances in full. Moved by my past service, the Emperor inclined toward leniency—until slanderous ministers spoke against me, and my execution was ordered. As the blade fell, my spirit scattered ten thousand li, and my entire household was slaughtered to the last soul. I heard my family’s cries, and suddenly awoke—it had all been a dream. The millet was just done cooking. A carefree boy called me to eat, and so I woke.

Alas! A single pot of millet had simmered through thirty years of life—wealth and honor, sorrow and joy, life and death—all no more than this. After the meal, the boy urged me to depart. My heart wavered; I recalled the Quanzhen master’s words and wished to see him once more, yet knew not where he dwelled. I asked the innkeeper, “Have you ever seen a Quanzhen master with a full beard around here?” The innkeeper replied, “Not far from here. Take the forked road, turn at the grove of scholar trees, and walk half a li north—you’ll find a small hermitage. He never strays far.”

I turned back, but the boy resisted. I deceived him by saying I had left my pillow behind, and together we returned to the original spot. As we reached the scholar trees, the Quanzhen master was already seated beneath them; it was the hour of *shen*. He smiled and asked, “Why has the scholar returned?” I answered that I had come to return the pillow. The master said, “If you still wish to sleep, why return it so soon?” Just as I reached to give it back, he turned the pillow aside and fixed his gaze on me: “Do you wish to sleep beneath this tree?” The boy at my side did not want me to rest. The master pointed and said, “Why not take a brief nap while you wait for your master?” The boy promptly fell asleep.

Exhausted, I lay down and slept. Two youths in green robes called my name: “Come, come! Let us take you on a journey!” Unsure who they were, I looked closely and saw they resembled my childhood friend Jin You, and Zhu Jialang, with whom I had shared a bench in youth. I followed them into a pine forest, then through a cypress grove, where the rustling wind sounded like autumn leaves falling, and the stillness was as profound as a midnight road devoid of travelers. Towering bamboo and murmuring springs led us through winding paths into layered passes, up towering cliffs, and down to level ground, until we approached an official residence. Seated within was an official in black robes and a black gauze cap, flanked by attendants who summoned me forward. The official descended the steps to greet me and asked the youths, “Where have you come from?”

The youths whispered into the official’s ear, to which he replied, “Very well, very well.” Suddenly, he ordered a soldier—his body like Vajra, his eyes like those of a tiger or leopard, his voice like that of a wolf, clad in a dark green straight robe, a red sash at his waist, and a three-peaked hat upon his head—to lead me onward.

We arrived at a place where an old woman, her white hair cascading, held a porcelain bowl and offered tea to passersby. Parched, I wished to drink, but the youth in green stopped me. We continued on, traversing the River of Desire, its bridge no wider than a foot yet rising a thousand yards high, its waves surging, fish and dragons gaping as if to devour all. Some who crossed walked steadily upon its beams, while others were shoved or toppled into the torrent, where the creatures vied to swallow them. I turned away, unable to bear the sight.

Near the River of Desire lay the Lake of Blood, its stench so foul it could not be endured. Those who drowned here were all women—some with only their faces visible, others with breasts or bellies exposed, in countless grotesque forms. Some clutched young children, others cradled infants, their bodies suffused with crimson light, making them untouchable.

Beside the Lake of Blood stood the Knife and Block, where navels were slit, bellies split open, entrails torn out, and the victims pounded with pestles and mallets. I grieved and averted my eyes, but the green-clad youth laughed and forced me to look.

Nearby hung the Gallows, slightly less severe than the Knife and Block. Some were suspended by their hands, others by their feet, still others by both, their tongues lolling from the weight of stones, their eyes bursting from their sockets. They were flogged with thorned rods and lashed with whips, their cries of agony resounding.

The executioners took it as sport, like puppeteers at play. Near the Gallows stood the Mountain of Knives, its jagged peaks and sheer ridges bristling with blades and halberds, dense as spring bamboo shoots, their silver edges glinting. The guards drove the condemned up the mountain, stripped bare; those who resisted were beaten from behind with great yellow rattan cudgels, forced to crawl upward until their limbs split open and blood gushed forth like springs. I pitied them and begged for mercy, but the guards paid no heed.

Beside the Mountain of Knives lay the Grinding Stone, even more gruesome. The sinners were bound, the upper millstone lifted, and the condemned placed within; the stone was lowered, weighted with boulders, and the victims’ screams shook the earth. Two demons turned the mill while another prodded the flesh and bones with a fork, reducing them to pulp. Blood and pus flowed like slurry beneath the stone.

Nearby stood the Ox-Plow, where sinners were bound with their hands behind their backs. One demon seized their feet, two others pressed a great wooden beam against their backs, forcing out their tongues. Another demon hooked the tongue with an iron curtain rod and stretched it to over ten feet in length, then drove a calf back and forth across it. The tongue, two yards long and three feet wide, was plowed until it turned to mud. Those awaiting their turn were made to watch, their souls shattered in terror.

Beside the Ox-Plow bubbled the Cauldron of Oil, an iron vat like a great urn, filled with boiling oil and stoked with firewood. The sinners were bound only by their feet and lowered into the cauldron; they thrashed with their hands until a demon forced them under with an iron fork. In an instant, flesh and bone dissolved.

Near the Cauldron of Oil lay the Realm of the Hungry Ghosts, a pit holding thousands of sinners, their emaciated bodies no thicker than a span, their throats no wider than a needle. Their heads swelled like bushel baskets, smoke issuing from their mouths, their voices thin as mosquitoes, their forms like turtles or alligators submerged in water—one could not fathom what sins they had committed. The green-clad youth said, “These wretches craved the five pungent foods and the three forbidden meats, never satisfied in life, and so they suffer this retribution. Soon they will be reborn as maggots.”

Beside the Hungry Ghosts burned the Pit of Flames, a trench filled with glowing coals. The sinners’ clothes were stripped away, and they were hurled onto the embers. As they struggled to rise, demons pinned them down with iron rakes, searing their flesh until the stench carried for miles, lingering even a hundred li away.

Near the Pit of Flames stretched the Realm of Darkness, where even in daylight one needed a torch to see. Only the sound of weeping could be heard—nothing more.

Beside the Darkness lay the Realm of Wrongful Death, its walls enclosing countless souls bound by ropes, some missing hands, others feet, some headless or faceless, their forms grotesque beyond measure. All had died unjustly.

Beside the Realm of Wrongful Death stood Avīci Hell, the most dreadful of all. Its gates were barred by tigers, and ranks of ox-headed and horse-faced demons stood guard, allowing no glimpse within. The green-clad youth said, “By the command of the Lord of True Yang, I shall open it for you to see.” The ox-headed demon cracked the gate slightly, and from within came a surge of sinners, wailing and shrieking for help. My heart turned cold, and I turned away.

The youth led me to a hall where I first met the official. He tried to detain me, but I refused. The green-clad youth gave me a push, and I awoke. The master and the carefree youths were gone. The sun had set, and with no place to lodge, I searched for the original inn, but it had vanished without a trace.

The golden wheel of the sun’s chariot had sunk in the western valley, and the jade mirror of the moon hung over the eastern marsh. Before me lay no hostel like Mengchang’s, behind me no estate like Pingyuan’s. Caught between advance and retreat, I wandered, lost in thought. By the roadside stood an ancient pine, its roots gnarled, its branches spreading like a canopy. I leaned against it and sat.

I saw scattered stars like pieces on a half-finished chessboard, the Milky Way like a silken ribbon dipped in a river. A startled magpie cried from the nearby woods, and a few crows winged their way home over the high ridge. From a distant inlet came the creak of a fisherman’s boat returning; from a familiar village, the mournful notes of a shepherd’s flute. I looked around—desolate and alone, my shadow my only companion.

Suddenly, I thought of my parents, who had raised me with such care, always by my side. Now I was lost in this forsaken place, and they knew nothing of my fate. Tears streamed down my face, and I composed a song:

*I gaze at the distant mountains veiled in white clouds,*
*The winding waters of home cut deep into my heart.*
*In the high hall, white-haired, who will tend to them?*
*Neglecting my filial duties, my soul takes flight—*
*O clouds, O clouds, measure my longing across a thousand li.*

Then I thought of my wife, Liu, who had married me still a maiden. She served my parents above and lacked the comfort of children below. Her bed was cold and lonely—all my fault. I composed another song:

*I gaze at the empty valley where a fair one dwells,*
*Spring’s beauty in full bloom, our hearts entwined.*
*Parted like birds with severed wings,*
*I long to soar to the azure skies.*
*Yet in her youth, her mirror gathers dust.*
*Far from home, I shatter her spirit—*
*O song, O song, may the apes not hear my sorrow.*

As I finished singing, my grief deepened. Dark clouds gathered, and the bright moon lost its luster. After a long while, the mist rolled back, and the sky cleared as bright as day. From the southern peak, a white crane descended, its form vast as a wheel. Its cry was almost human as it alighted on the pine, bending its neck to peer at me—seeming to pity me, or perhaps to mock. Several more cranes circled and landed before me, dancing, their feet tracing patterns in the air. With a piercing cry, they joined my song:

*On the southern mountain, white clouds drift,*
*On the northern mountain, white clouds drift.*
*Gazing between them, the heart labors in vain.*
*When clouds gather, heaven seals itself;*
*When clouds scatter, heaven splits apart.*
*Gathering and scattering, parting and uniting—*
*All lies with the heart of heaven.*
*Solid or dissolved, what can man do?*
*Fix your eyes on these clouds,*
*Close them, and they vanish.*
*Parents and children are like these clouds—*
*Though you long to cling to them, you cannot.*

The crane nodded at me: *“Scholar, scholar, which parent does not wish it? But when impermanence comes, they cannot care for you, nor you for them.”*

The crane shook its head and sang again:

*Take that scorched paulownia, craft it into my zither,*
*Pluck its strings once—joy fills my heart.*
*Morning and evening, harmonious union,*
*Play but one tune—the phoenix’s cry.*
*By lamplight and moonlight, side by side we walk.*
*Vowing to grow old together, a hundred years as one.*
*Suddenly the strings snap—silence, soundless.*
*Though I long to play again, the music is not what it was.*
*The phoenix alights on the high ridge,*
*The mirror of the *luan*-bird shatters its water-chestnut frame.*
*Mortal couples are like this zither—*
*Heaven will seize what it will, not to be ransomed even with a thousand gold.*

Again it nodded at me: *“Scholar, scholar, which couple does not wish it? But when the day of fate arrives, each goes their own way. You will not see them, nor they you—where then to seek your spouse? Cut this binding cord at once, and seek for yourself the path of eternal life. When you reach that place, naturally parents and children, husband and wife will know no separation.”*

I was about to question its meaning when the crane stretched its wings, soared into the sky, and returned to the southern peak. The stars shifted beneath the moon, the cock crowed in Fusang, and dawn’s light stirred. My heart suddenly awakened—I no longer wished to press forward. The thought of fame and rank turned to ash, longing for home was severed, and I drifted with thoughts beyond the world, desiring to seek the Perfect Realization as my teacher. Retracing my steps along the old path, I came to the locust grove and sat for a while. There I met a wandering Daoist, his age nearing a hundred years, with snowy hair and bushy brows, clad in a tattered robe, straw sandals on his feet, and a staff in hand. I stepped forward to bow, and we sat together. He asked me: *“From what realm have you come?”*

Amidst the verdant willow grove ahead, a young servant hangs by his life from a high branch.” Startled, my face paled at these words—I knew it must be my runaway page. I immediately wished to go and see, but the Daoist halted, saying: *“Stay, sir. I observe your bones like a solitary crane, your face like spring blossoms, your beard like fresh willow shoots. A dark mole marks your brow, your mountain bones rise firm, your twin orbs gaze toward the sea, your five peaks stand full and proud. Your spirit is clear, your qi fresh—you are surely destined for immortality. Why not follow a master, receive the secret formulas, and seek liberation?”* I bowed deeply to receive his teaching.

The wandering Daoist smiled and said: *“For over thirty years I have roamed, yet never encountered a single true aperture. You should seek elsewhere—do not let this delay your own affairs.”* With that, he turned and departed westward, and though I called, he did not return.

I searched the willow grove and indeed found the boy hanging dead beneath a tree. It seems that while I slept beneath the locust, the master had already left. The boy called to me but I did not wake, so he kept watch through the night. When he realized I would not rise, driven by despair, he wept for me once, then hung my zither and sword in the grove before following me in death.

Alas! Before I could attain the Dao, I first brought ruin to a boy’s life—how can I bear this?

I gathered earth to form incense, bowed toward the empty sky, and swore an oath: *“If I should indeed meet an enlightened master who points out the path of delusion and confirms the mysterious Dao, I shall first deliver this boy. May the gods of heaven and earth witness my sincerity—let not this lonely soul drift far from its root. If I break this vow, may heaven and earth cut short my years.”*

When the oath was complete, I lowered the boy’s body, gathered mugwort from beside the willow roots to cover him, and placed the zither and sword at his side. With earth, I wrote upon the zither: *“To any traveler or dweller who buries this boy, these shall be your reward.”* Then I wept bitterly before taking my leave.

I returned to the locust tree to await the Perfected One’s return, but after two days, no word came. Each day I ate wild fruits to stave off hunger and drank from the stone spring to quench my thirst. My body grew gaunt, my form like withered wood; my feet could no longer walk, my eyes could no longer see. I lay prostrate at the root of the locust tree.

Suddenly, I heard the faint strains of a woodcutter’s song. I strained to listen—its melody was like a tune, the rhythm marked by the strike of an axe handle. The song went:

*“My home lies deep in the mountain wilds,
Axe in hand, I enter the forest thick.
I’ll fell a cypress a thousand years old,
I’ll hew a pine that’s stood ten thousand years.
I care not if he’s a hero or a lord,
I care not if he feasts on golden bowls.
Sometimes I gaze at the moon at zenith bright,
Sometimes I watch the clear wind on the ridge.
Sometimes I dig the white roots of poria,
Sometimes I pluck the red blooms of magnolia.
At dusk, I sleep on the mountainside—
All I gain is a carefree, easy mind.”*

The song faded into the distance. With great effort, I pursued the woodcutter to pay my respects. He asked, *“What brings you, sir?”* I inquired after the Perfected One’s dwelling. The woodcutter replied, *“None here, none at all. Beyond this mountain’s peak, down the other side, stands a dense bamboo grove. Within it is a small thatched hut, where a Daoist lives—jowly, bearded, wild-eyed, barefoot, with a great belly. We call him Master Jin. He’s a Perfected One who’s left the world.”*

I thought to myself: *“When I was still at home, that beggar spoke of ‘a hermitage in Mount Jinzhong.’ Could this be the same man?”* I begged the woodcutter to guide me. He exclaimed, *“Sir, you’re courting death! Why seek that rogue Daoist? Though he’s left the world, he’s fiercer than any layman. When merchants pass by with goods, he doesn’t need to do much—just a single shout, and they’re so terrified they look like little ghosts. The folk in these mountain villages must invite him to eat and drink at

The woodcutter spends his days gathering firewood among the mountain ridges,
Too lazy even to share a pillow with the white clouds.
He cares not for right or wrong, for all their topsy-turvy ways,
Nor for the thousand wiles of men,
Nor for the perils of wind and wave,
Nor for the hardships of the world’s long road.
When the mood strikes, he simply goes home—content—
While you fret and fuss, restless and unquiet.

The woodcutter was already gone. Exhausted, I sat upon a rock.
From afar, I saw a Perfected One approaching. Drawing nearer, I looked closely—it was not him, but the great bearded beggar from my hometown, now disguised in the robes of a Perfected One.
Sensing he was no ordinary man, I prostrated myself and bowed.

The man said, *“Ah, scholar, you’ve come this far—how bitter it must be! Why not stay at home? Above you, parents; below, wife, children, and servants. By day, fine meats and grains; by night, silken quilts. Why not live in ease? What possessed you to endure such hardship—no kin in sight, no food by day, no bed by night, soon to be devoured by tigers and wolves? Let me guide you back the way you came. What say you?”*

I bowed again and swore I would not return.

The Perfected One said, *“If not home—then what do you seek?”*
*“I wish to see my master in the Golden Heaviness Mountains.”*
The Perfected One pointed with his hand: *“Here he comes, here he comes.”*
Before my eyes appeared the potbellied, bearded beggar who had once come to my home, now also clad in the guise of a Perfected One, drifting toward us as if borne on the wind.
I prostrated myself upon the ground, but the Perfected One raised me with his hand. The two Perfected Ones exchanged words between themselves.
Then the latter one laughed as he looked at me: *“Coming so late as this—you will regret it when it is too late.”*
I swore again as before.
The two said, *“Since this is your resolve, wait here for us. We go but a short distance to seek a fellow Daoist, then return with you to the hermitage. If we do not come back, cross this mountain and look for a thatched hut on the knoll ahead—ask there for Master Golden Heaviness. Simply sit within the hut, and we will return.”*
With a flick of their sleeves, they left me and went on their way.

As dusk deepened, I could walk no farther and took shelter by the roadside. In an instant, fierce beasts roared, circling around me as if to seize and devour me. Collecting my spirit, I prayed in silence: *“Now, in my quest for life and death, I reck not of body or life. If these beasts hunger, let my flesh fill their bellies.”* A beast crouched before me, its eyes flashing like lightning, its voice thundering like a storm—after a long while, it vanished. Just as I closed my eyes, a bear sniffed at me, its fur scraping my face and brow. It seized my arm with its paw, then reached another into my chest, as if to tear out my heart. I prayed as before, and the bear released me and fled. The night passed without a moment’s peace.

At dawn, my stomach gnawed with emptiness, and my legs would not move. I dug up bracken roots to eat, and with some effort, pressed on. By midday, I saw a shepherd boy tending sheep. I asked how far the mountain hermitage lay, and he replied: *“Not less than ten days’ journey—five to the summit, and five more to the hermitage.”* Undeterred by the distance, I walked on. Parched with thirst, I cupped my hands to drink from a spring and accidentally swallowed a small water snake. My belly twisted in pain. Lying on my back by the stream, crows pecked at my eyes, hawks clawed at my chest, and ants gnawed at my feet. With a single unmoving heart, I endured the agony, though it was truly unbearable. Opening my eyes, I saw the shepherd boy laughing beside me: *“Fool, fool! Where do you think you’ll find Master Golden Heaviness? I’ll lead you back down. Your family awaits your return. Suffer like this, and by tomorrow you’ll have no life left.”*

"How could you ever reach that place?" I made no reply. The boy took a wheat cake from his bosom and offered it to me, but I refused. The boy said: *"Master, you still have ten days’ journey ahead. If you do not take sustenance, your life will be in peril—how then will you ever meet your teacher?"* His words rang true, so I accepted the cake and ate it, thanking the boy. As it went down, I felt my spirit clear and my qi swift; the pain in my belly vanished. I spat out the small snake—only to find it was a blade of grass root.

I bowed to the boy, then took my leave and pressed onward. Before I had climbed halfway up the mountain, dusk fell again, and no villagers were to be seen. In the distance, among the dark pines of a secluded ravine, blue smoke curled faintly; a yellow dog barked sharply. A thatched eave rose above a thousand ridges, and a wicker gate leaned crookedly over a deep gorge. Delighted to have found shelter, I went and knocked on the door. After a long while, a maidservant holding a candle emerged and scolded me: *"This is a village household. Our master is away—how dare you be so rude as to knock on our door?"*

I pleaded pitifully for mercy. The maidservant’s anger subsided slightly, and she bade me wait. After a moment, she led me inside. A vermilion gate swung open. Within stretched a vast hall of deep chambers, where prawn-shaped hooks hung high and beaded curtains screened peacock-feathered screens. Brocade cushions were spread in rows; aloeswood and sandalwood smoldered in jeweled censers, silver candles flared upon golden stands. As embroidered drapes parted lightly, a fragrant breeze wafted forth. Several maidservants in black led out a young beauty, nearly twenty years of age. Her willow-leaf brows trailed green; her almond eyes shimmered blue. Raven tresses coiled like the morning sun; her powdered face outshone the loveliness of the Luo River goddess. She bore the grace of the Chu palace and the bearing of the Wu kingdom.

She invited me into the hall. I demurred, reluctant to approach the inner chambers of such a noble house. The woman said: *"Since you have come here, it is a bond of three lifetimes, not something fixed in this one alone. You are a young gentleman; I am in the bloom of my years. Heaven has granted our union. Otherwise, how could I dwell in this secluded valley, neighbor to rocks and herbs, companion to deer and swine, where even woodcutters and herdsmen seldom come—let alone a gentleman like you? This is a gift of Heaven, not the work of man."* She called for her maids to prepare a feast.

I prostrated myself in refusal, repeatedly declining, but the woman’s star-like eyes flashed, her willow brows bristled, and her pomegranate teeth clenched. She ordered the maidservants to drive me out: *"Let this starveling scholar be food for wolves!"* I gladly withdrew, but as I did, her anger turned to insistence, and she commanded the doors be locked—though I wished to leave, I could not.

Soon, the feast was ready. She ordered me to take my place. I lingered outside the hall, refusing to enter. Several attendants lifted and carried me in, seating me to her left while she took the right. Before us, a hundred singing boys and dancing girls assembled. With each round of wine, a dance and song followed; the pipes, strings, and drums clamored in my ears, their sound shaking the hills and valleys.

First, they performed *"Cherishing the Hours,"* singing a measured tune. The lyrics went:
*Soft winds stir the brocade chamber,*
*Crabapple blossoms perfume the air,*
*Silken sleeves of dancing robes sway long.*
*A bird’s cry rings like pipes and strings,*
*Golden shuttles deftly cast among green willows.*
*Young nobles and ladies,*
*Wandering together to seek spring’s fragrance.*
*Where joy is found, there is the play’s stage—*
*Why seek the Way of immortals beyond this world?*

I leaned against the table, feigning indifference, though I committed every note to memory. Forced to rise, they next performed the *"Dance of the Eight Winds."* The melody followed the same form. The song said:

The small pond’s new lotuses, layered like coins, unfurl.
A balmy breeze first stirs the Yu strings,
while fireflies drift into the painted hall.
A single rain cools the bamboo mat for sleep—
I call a young maid to arrange cups and plates,
idly pouring fragrant wine in delicate streams.
Together we admire the scene:
man and moon, both full—
who would know that immortals walk this mortal world?

After three rounds, they danced *"Rainbow Skirts,"* the melody unchanged. The song went:
A crisp, cool breeze flutters lightly,
the well’s pulley creaks at the steps,
and the autumn cricket’s cry disturbs the chill.
Maple leaves on the crag outshine blossoms in red.
Wild geese in slanted formation cross the frontier,
while pure thoughts pair well with a full cup of wine.
The spirit of Red Cliff’s excursion rises yet higher—
a place for joy,
how often can one find it?
Speak not of Peng Zu’s aged frailty.

After four rounds, they danced *"Flying Swallow,"* the melody as before. The song said:
Six-petaled jade flowers swirl wildly through the vast sky,
beast-shaped braziers burn warmly without cease.
Wine is poured low, songs sung softly, lamb simmered—
we laugh to see plum blossoms open as the moon hangs on the branch.
Tapping sandalwood clappers,
dancing slender waists—
the joy of this world is true heroism.
Marten furs and golden belts adorn the mighty,
while we mock those who seek companions beyond the world among the mugwort and wormwood.

The women rose, lifting cups of wine in toast.
I did not respond and lay down.
Forced into marriage, I feigned madness in rage.
The woman said, “If you do not obey, blood may spill within inches.”
I smiled and replied, “One may live or die, but resolve cannot be taken.
Would I endure a fleeting pleasure to ruin great integrity?
I gladly accept the blade without protest!”
I stretched my neck, awaiting the blow.
The woman said, “A mad scholar indeed.”
Knowing my will could not be bent, she retreated to her orchid chamber,
leaving me in the central courtyard.
I still did not relax my guard,
fearing drunken sleep might bring disgrace.
I feigned slumber with closed eyes,
and she did not violate me for even a night.
A dog’s bark startled me awake—
I found myself in a pile of straw.
Was the vision of that strange night a phantom?

I pushed through brambles and thorns, climbing up the mountain stream.
After a mile or so, I saw a boy on a yellow calf descending from the layered peaks.
I asked him.
The boy told me, “Master Jin fell from the cliff in a violent gale ten days ago.
Crows and magpies have picked at him—
little remains of flesh and bone.
Where could he be now?”
Hearing this, I sighed in grief and sorrow,
ready to end my own life.
Yet hesitating, I thought,
“There are others with the same surname.
I cannot take this as certain.
I must go to the hermitage myself.
If he is truly gone, then I will decide what to do.”

Just as the boy turned past the gully, two or three more young lads emerged, each astride a yellow calf, forming a playful procession as they frolicked among the ridges, piping on flutes and singing songs. Their tune went:

*Up the mountain slope we ride,*
*calves our steeds, the wild our guide.*
*Green straw cloaks and turbans tied—*
*laughing blossoms plucked where peaks collide.*
*Here a gull on water glides,*
*there a cuckoo calls the rain.*
*Who would know the world’s cruel tides?*
*Day by day, our joy remains.*
*Carefree hearts forget all pains.*

When the song ended, they clapped their hands and burst into laughter. Another boy struck up a different melody:

*O’er layered peaks and humbled hills we roam,*
*barefoot, tattered robes left home.*
*Through pine shade we drive our calves to roam.*
*Stone springs quench our thirst alone,*
*wild tunes hummed to winds that moan.*
*Tender grass our bed, the sky our dome.*
*No chains, no bounds—*
*let heaven and earth drift as they may.*

Again they laughed. A third boy sang in the same style:

*By the crag, osmanthus blooms turn gold,*
*chrysanthemum scents the peaks unfold.*
*Cool winds stir the drifting clouds untold.*
*Eagles soar on wings so bold,*
*geese in ranks traverse the cold.*
*Moonlight like water washes cares away.*
*See a leaf-like sail on Xiang’s stream glide—*
*how many storms may toss the tide?*

When he finished, the four boys gathered in a circle, playing bamboo flutes together. Pointing at me, they giggled and teased, saying, *"What kind of fool are you? Abandoning your home to wander lost in these wild mountains. You must be a madman, out of your mind!"* With each remark, they laughed anew. Then another boy began to sing:

*Their tune went:*

Behold us delighting in these mountains,
laughing at those who topsy-turvy remain—
clutching wealth and rank as idle burdens,
stirring only vexation and care,
stirring only sorrow and strife.
I urge you: cast them away.
Shatter the snare’s scheme,
and only then may you escape life and death.

The four boys sang as they laughed, walked as they sang, then vanished in an instant. I strained to see, but they were gone. Gathering my robes, I pressed onward—already the sun hung low at dusk. Though I stumbled in pursuit, I could not catch them. Turning in every direction, I searched.

Then, atop a sudden rise, on a level stone sat two village boys. Their yellow hair fell to their shoulders, ragged clothes draped their frames. One cradled a handful of stones and two baskets; the other held a stone board, spinning pieces upon its surface. Startled, they stood and bowed in fear.

By then, the moon crowned the pines, bright as daylight. I crossed my legs beneath a sprawling cypress and sat. Soon, two elders arrived with coughs and sighs, settling beside the stone to arrange their stone pieces for weiqi.

One elder called to the yellow-haired boy: “Child, do you know weiqi? Let me tell you of the game.

Weiqi mirrors heaven and earth,
embodies yin and yang’s mutual conquest,
spins in ceaseless transformation,
completes its measured course.
Wield the spirit’s scheme in profound stillness,
birth cunning and skill among peers.
Seize the corners with four or five stones,
claim the borders with slanted plays.
Cast the heron’s long legs,
trap the turtle’s withdrawn head.
A lone army’s deep thrust—
guard against flank-striking pawns.
Troops arrayed in ranks—
require high ramparts and deep trenches.
Illusory eyes: do not treat as live land.
Severed shapes: abandon as dead.
Do not advance and lose your rear,
do not lean right and drift left.
Do not covet bait and lose the fish,
do not indulge in joy and forget sorrow.
To break a formidable foe,
first settle your own strategy.
Open the gate, charge the formation,
watch for gaps to steal in.
Let divine schemes render you unfathomable,
let hidden wisdom make you hard to fathom.
Pursue the plan of ten thousand certainties,
beware the greed for petty gains.
One move amiss—
the whole board is lost.

Like a person in the world,
like a game of weiqi at play:
spread wide, and black and white stretch in every direction;
gather in, and valor and might hide away.
A hundred years pass no swifter than one game,
ten thousand affairs return to a single scheme.
Gaze upon the board and reflect—
I urge you, sir, turn back soon.”

As he finished, I stepped forward to ask a question. The two elders brushed their robes and strode off; the boys gathered the board and followed, vanishing into the distance.

As I stood there in bewilderment, suddenly a great whistle rang out from the northern hollow—like the howling of wind through trees, like the surging of a mountain stream. Two towering black-clad men came charging toward me, their shouts fiercer than Xiang Yu’s, their presence more fearsome than Chiyou’s. One seized my hair, the other my body. Alas, I had no purse! The robbers laughed: “We’ve lurked here for years, but never crossed paths with such a penniless wretch. No money, no clothes—what a waste of our time!” The shorter one grumbled, “I can’t stand this!” Drawing a hemp rope from his waist, he bound me without a word, trussing me like a dumpling in meat. Then they hoisted me high into an ancient juniper, clapped their hands in a triumphant whistle, and vanished into the dense woods.

At that moment, I had no path to heaven above nor earth below, nor any hope of rescue. My limbs burned with pain, and I fainted twice over. Wailing to the empty air, I composed a poem:

*For immortality I sought the Red Pine’s trail,*
*Through brambles and thorns into the mountains deep.*
*How oft my parched throat found the stream’s cool grace,*
*Yet all day my hollow gut knew no grain’s fill.*
*My shoes, worn thin, met jagged peaks’ cruel bite,*
*My tattered robes could scarce withstand the wind’s embrace.*
*Who knew that after dangers past, new perils would arise—*
*Now dangling from this ancient tree, my life nears its last trace.*

I wailed aloud, then fell silent to recite the verse again. Suddenly, a cough echoed from the boulder below. I looked down to see the old weiqi player, gazing up at me with a smile as he chanted:

*Your frame hangs like a withered branch, your body gaunt and frail,*
*Yet fate still tests you with these brutes so cruel and hale.*
*The ropes entangle you as spiders weave their snare,*
*Your shadow sways like a butterfly lured by scent’s sweet trail.*
*They’d have you curl like hedgehog or mantis in retreat,*
*While their wolfish hearts unleash their savagery complete.*
*The flying teal has yet to master life’s elixir true—*
*Why must they shrink your world before its time is due?*

I pleaded for his help. The old man said, “These bandits are vicious—challenge them, and we’ll both be done for.” I begged again. At last, he lowered me down, untied the ropes, and rubbed my limbs, where the bindings had cut deep. Only after a long while did I revive.

“What brings a scholar like you to such a place?” he asked.

I told him my purpose.

“Ah! Ah!” he exclaimed. “You’ve gone astray!”

That Master Jin bears the name of a renunciate but not its truth—by night he debauches women, by day he plunders travelers. Those two brigands just now were his accomplices. Seeing him come flying after them like that, I knew his wicked impulse had flared, so I followed at a distance to this spot. Sure enough, he was up to his tricks. Had I not intervened, you’d have been left dangling till death. Now you’d best take advantage of the dark to find your way back and save your life—wouldn’t that be best?”

I replied, “My resolve is already firm. Unless the eastern mountains crumble to dust and the southern seas run dry, I will not give up my search for my master. Death comes to all in time—that is why I have come here. If I waver now, Heaven and Earth will surely hold me to account. I would rather die than turn back.”

The old man laughed heartily, left me, and returned the way he came. I sat and waited for dawn, then climbed once more toward the mountain’s peak. Near midday, I saw smoke rising from the treetops and heard the crowing of roosters. In the distance, a low wall of whitewashed plaster enclosed half a cottage, where three boys drew water. I approached to beg for food.

There, along the level terrace, stood several tables in a row, all occupied by scholars. One asked, “From what quarter does this friend hail? Your robes are disheveled—have you suffered the hardships of Chen, or perhaps encountered the blades of Kuang? Please rest a while and take some tea.” They invited me to the seat of honor. I demurred, but at last sat down.

The tea arrived—clear, cold, and fragrant, its savor one I had not tasted in over a month. When I had finished, a young scholar spoke, asking me the reason for my journey. I answered truthfully.
