The others covered their mouths and snickered, saying, “Sir, since you have studied the classics, how can you be ignorant of the great principles? Confucius said, ‘To attack what is heterodox is to invite harm.’ To depart from Confucius and embrace the heterodox is to harm oneself. To spread such teachings to others is to harm the world. Mencius said, ‘Of the three forms of unfilial conduct, the greatest is to leave no descendants.’ To be without father and without lord is to be no better than a beast. You have abandoned what you have learned to follow heterodox ways—forsaking your parents is unfilial, forgetting your lord is disloyal, severing the bond of husband and wife is immoral, casting aside friends is faithless, and disregarding the order between young and old is disorderly. You have corrupted the Five Relationships; how then can you stand in heaven and earth as a member of humankind? Moreover, from Pan Gu to the present, how many have not died? How many have achieved immortality? Consider the flower: if there is glory, there must be withering. Consider the arm: if it stretches, it must also bend. Heaven and earth themselves wax and wane; mountains and rivers themselves change. We are but one thing among many—how can we transcend heaven and earth to exist alone?”

Then all the scholars joined in unison, urging me to stay and pursue the same path: “This place is scarcely a hundred li from the capital. In ten days, the great examination will be held. Go forth and win rank and office—would that not bring greater joy than wandering beyond the world?” I gave no answer but took my leave. Though they pressed me to stay, knowing my resolve could not be shaken, some offered a kerchief, others a pair of shoes, but I accepted nothing. Lightly I departed, and after half a li, I turned to look back—no trace of them remained.

That day I reached the summit’s end. Lifting my gaze, I saw on the opposite peak’s flank a small hermitage, and my face lit with joy. Descending along the mountain’s back, weary from the climb, I rested beside a bamboo grove. There flowed a stream, its jade-green waters carrying the source of Kunlun, its silver springs issuing from the celestial branches. Chrysanthemum fragrance assailed my senses, rivaling the beauty of Nanyang; peach blossoms rode the current, like guests on the banks of Wuyi. Clear enough to wash one’s cap strings, deep enough to feast the eyes—I cupped my hands and drank.

A fisherman rowed toward me in a fragrant orchid boat, singing as he came:

*I steer a light, light skiff,
Push a floating oar so swift,
Don a small, small straw cloak,
Toward a winding, winding brook.
Roaming free, roaming free—
On the sand, mandarin ducks entwine their necks,
Bathe their feathers in the limpid stream,
Call and fly past willow banks so green.*

He sang a verse, struck his oar a few times, and rowed past the western bend. Then another song:

*On both banks, lotus coins lie stacked,
Waves like flying snow attack,
Fish at play in the water’s track,
Butterflies dance round the bank’s slack.
Who cares if the midday sun burns bright?
On the waves, a cool breeze brings delight.
Joy unbound—
Gently I ply my orchid oar,
A marvelous sight!
Wandering, I cannot bear to rest.*

Again he rowed toward the eastern bend. Another song:

*Dense hibiscus lines the shore,
Brilliant knotweed blooms galore,
Hear the lone goose cry on Xiang’s far floor,
Hear the village’s cold mallet’s roar.
Most lovely when moon shines bright,
Moon shines bright—
The flute’s notes chase the clear stream’s flight,
Osmanthus fragrance drifts in the night.
A golden cup raised high,
Drunken eyes in clouds and waters lie.*

He rowed his boat past where I stood. Another song:

*A biting north wind rises,
Willow fluff lightly flies,
Jade petals strew the ground in white surprise.
The Jiang and Han rivers shrouded deep in cloud,
A fisher’s pole feels winter’s chill so proud.
How can one sigh for the lamb’s bold might?
The lamb’s bold might.*

In the warm chamber, fair maidens gather in joy—
I ponder:
How can their brocade robes compare to my tattered garb?
Mine is far finer.

I bowed and begged the boatman to ferry me across the stream.
The boatman asked, “Where are you headed?”
I replied, “To Master Jin’s hermitage.”
The boatman pointed and said, “Follow this creek past the bend, then turn up the hillside. The main path will lead you there.”
With that, the boatman rowed downstream.

Following his directions, I indeed found the main path. After walking about a *li* along the slope, I saw a tousle-haired boy descending the hill. Clad in a black robe, he held a begging bowl and chanted the name of Amitābha. Knowing he was from the hermitage, I bowed and asked after the master’s whereabouts.

The boy said, “The master was bitten by a snake while gathering herbs. He lies bedridden in the hermitage, his life hanging by a thread.”
Hearing this, I felt both joy and sorrow. Taking leave of the boy, I hurried straight to the hermitage.

Suddenly, no one was in sight. Fallen leaves piled beneath the eaves, withered branches stretched across the path. I pushed them aside with my hands, my fingers torn and bleeding, my palms stained crimson. Reaching the hermitage, I found the wicker gate tightly shut. I knocked for a long while, but no answer came. After a pause, I knocked again. From within, a cough sounded:
“Hmph! What rude mountain brute dares disturb my humble abode?”

“I am but a poor Daoist, my clothes barely covering my body, my food lacking in flavor—what could I possibly possess to provoke your intrusion? Be gone at once and seek your livelihood elsewhere!”

I called out, “Master, Master! It is your disciple, Lü Yan.”

“For decades I have lived here alone as a renunciant—where would I have taken a disciple? I do not know you.”

I called again, “Master, Master! On the road to Handan, you once bestowed upon me a bamboo pillow. Among the pines, you made me a promise. I have come especially to seek you out.”

The master let out a roar: “You foolish scholar! Had I made you a promise, why did you delay until now? In searching for you, I was bitten by a snake on the toe. Now the rot has spread nearly to my thigh, the pain unbearable, my life hanging by a thread—all on account of you, you wretch! Now you arrive—what use is it? Go back, go back at once! Your parents are aged, your wife and children still young. The court has just opened the southern selection—begone, begone!”

“Unable to bear my suffering, I fell to my knees outside the gate, wailing and weeping bitterly: ‘Master, if you will not take me as your disciple, I shall hurl myself from the cliff and die. What a pity—after enduring a thousand hardships to reach this place, only to play out a meaningless farce. Master, have mercy!’

The master fell silent for a long while before barking, ‘Are you not feigning sincerity?’

‘If I am false in my heart, may Heaven strike me dead.’

‘Very well. Gently push open the wicker gate.’

Before I could lift my hand, the door swung open. Inside, I saw only a clay bed and a tile pillow, no mat or blanket. The master lay prostrate, the walls bare, with neither table nor stool, nor pot nor stove. He called me forward: ‘What a fine disciple! Just when I had no one to attend me. You’ve come at the right time—come, come, and tend to my wounded foot!’

I lifted the hem of his straw robe to look: the stench was unbearable. One foot had rotted into a pulp of flesh, the leg already festering, maggots writhing thickly. I brushed them away with my sleeve, scooped them into my lap, and scattered them outside the hut hundreds of times before it was somewhat clean. Yet the sight of his rotting foot pained my heart deeply.

That night, the master bade me sleep at his feet. I made no objection, tending to him step by step. He cried out in pain all night, while I sat and soothed him.

The next day, the master said, ‘Disciple, for ten days the pain has robbed me of all appetite, but today I feel hunger gnawing at my belly. Go and find me something to eat.’

I gladly accepted the command.”

I stepped out of the hut but knew not the way. When I first arrived, there was a path, yet now, searching again, I could not find it—all was overgrown with brambles and thatch. I knew not where to turn, nor dared I ask the master, fearing I might rouse his anger. Lifting my head, I prayed to heaven: *"Disciple seeks food for his master—may the mountain spirits guide me to a path!"* Suddenly, I spied a narrow trail, just wide enough to walk where no grass blocked the way. Following it for several *li*, I came upon a small hamlet, where an old woman stood at her door. I begged her for food, and she gave me a bowl of rice.

I hurried back, and by the time I returned, it was already the *shen* hour. From afar, I heard the master shouting and cursing from within the hut, railing about hunger: *"Where did this wild savage come from, with no place to stay, pretending to be a disciple—thinking I have something worth stealing! He left at dawn and has not returned till now, no doubt feasting himself full, with no care for his sick master. When that wretch returns, I’ll drive him out at once!"*

Muttering curses under his breath, I rushed to the bedside, knelt, and explained what had happened. The master fell silent, ignoring me entirely. After kneeling for half the time it takes to cook a meal, he finally snapped, *"Bah! Bring it here for me to eat."* I cupped my hands and offered the rice, but with no chopsticks, I had to pick it up with my fingers to feed him. His eyes flashed with rage as he roared, startling my soul from my body: *"You savage—how dare you be so insolent! A sick man like me, forced to eat such coarse rice, and you don’t even wash your hands before feeding it to me. What kind of reckless fool are you? What sort of ‘home’ did you leave to become this way? Go, go at once—or I’ll beat you with my staff!"*

With tears in my eyes, I pleaded, *"Master, forgive this disciple’s offense. Let me go seek better food. But this place is deep in the mountains, with few households—only a handful of poor villagers within ten *li*, not a single wealthy family. Where could I find white rice?"*

The master said, *"I’m too furious to eat. Just go—don’t make trouble here!"*

I pleaded again and again, *"Allow this disciple to serve until the master’s illness is cured—then I will gladly depart. Let me go once more to seek food to fill the master’s hunger. I beg the master to calm his anger."* The master finally fell silent.

Following the same path, I went to beg for food. By good fortune, I came upon a wealthy household, its gates shut tight. I knocked until they opened, only to be met with a torrent of abuse from the gatekeeper, who threatened to strike me. I pleaded pitifully. After a while, an elder emerged and asked what had happened. I explained the situation. The elder smiled faintly and said, *"You fool—if you’re starving, that’s your own misfortune. Why should your master’s hunger concern you? Are there no other monks in the world? If he scolds you, go elsewhere and find another master. Why persist so stubbornly?"*

I bowed deeply to the elder and replied, *"A loyal minister does not serve two lords; a chaste woman does not take two husbands. In ancient times, Chen Xiang was rebuked by Meng Ke for abandoning his teacher, while Li Si was condemned by Confucians for betraying his roots. Even Chen Ping remembered his debt to an unknown benefactor, and Zengzi could not bear to replace Confucius with Master You. Great righteousness outweighs petty resentment. Moreover, the rites of serving a teacher demand labor and devotion—how could I dare turn away?"*

The elder, finding my words reasonable, gave me a bowl of fragrant white millet rice and told me to eat. He also gave me another portion for my master. I refused to eat first, as the master had not yet taken his meal. The elder insisted, so I gratefully accepted. After bowing in thanks, I returned, only to find the moon already filling the thatched window. The master was still shouting curses at home: *"Leaving the door unlatched at night and still not returning—what a savage heart you have!"*

I entered and first reported to the master, *"This disciple’s hands are now clean, and I have obtained white rice. Please, master, take your meal."* The master opened his eyes, and at last, a hint of pleasure crossed his face. *"Give it to me,"* he said. I fed him with my hands, fearing he was too hungry, and offered it to him repeatedly. With a sweep of his hand, he scattered it all to the ground, cursing, *"You barbarian—do you mean to take my life? I’ve been ill for so long, my throat is parched and narrow. How could I eat so quickly? Yet you keep shoving it at me!"*

I explained, *"I feared the master was too hungry and meant no harm. I beg the master not to be angry. There is still half a bowl left—please, master, eat."* For a long while, he said nothing. Then he finally snapped, *"Bring it to me!"* I fed him slowly. With another shout, he struck the bowl away, sending the rice spilling to the ground, gnashing his teeth in fury: *"I was just about to eat, and you deliberately slow down—you’re clearly trying to torment me!"*

He raged on until midnight, then barked, *"Won’t you pick up that rice from the ground and eat it clean? If you don’t, it will all be your fault!"* I humbly assented, kneeling to gather and eat every grain.

The master, peering through the moonlight, smiled and said, *"Well gathered, well gathered—now that is a true disciple."*

Since eating the scholars’ tea and rice, I had not felt hunger. Arriving at the hermitage, I was famished, but as the master had not yet eaten, I dared not eat first. Now, after gathering the scattered rice from the ground, I found myself strangely full.

The master said, *"Disciple, tomorrow fetch some water to wash my feet—be early."*

At the fifth watch, I took leave to draw water, but after searching the entire mountain, found none. At a ravine’s mouth, I saw a pool, yet had no vessel to carry it. Using my alms bowl, I scooped a bowlful and returned to the hermitage by noon. The master flicked his hand, spilling it to the ground: *"I told you to go early. Now you bring water at midday—how am I to beg for food? Forget washing. Go beg instead."*

Another night passed. He called for water again. I set out at the fourth watch and returned only at dawn. The master scolded, *"You witless, ailing fool! My feet require a whole vat to wash—would you use this eating vessel for such a task?"* Again, he dashed the water to the ground.

I pondered in vain, then said to the master, *"I have no vessel to draw water, nor one to wash your feet. What if I carry you on my back to the ravine and wash you there?"*

The master hesitated, then replied, *"Very well, but carry me carefully—my feet must not be jostled."*

I helped him lean against my back, cradling his legs in my arms. Four times I stopped to rest. At the ravine’s edge, lowering him proved difficult, so I first knelt, then lay down sideways, easing him to the ground. His rotting thigh rested against mine as I scooped water with my hands, gently wiping away the decay. The foul flesh washed clean, the stench lessened. The master sighed in relief, and I, too, felt joy.

After washing, I carried him back. By late afternoon, as we neared the hermitage, a fierce wind arose—sand flew, stones rolled, the pines roared like raging tides, the ravine echoed like a battle cry. The master trembled; so did I. Suddenly, from the bamboo grove, a thunderous crack split the air as a white tiger leapt forth, its form like a water buffalo, charging toward the master.

I quickly set him down and threw myself over him, pleading to the tiger, *"Eat me instead—spare my master!"* My wails shook the heavens. The tiger paused, then slowly retreated. The wind stilled, the sky cleared. I carried the master back to the hermitage and laid him on his bed.

He turned to me and said, *"Now, at last, you understand my heart."*

The next morning, his feet were healed, and he could rise. One day, he led me to Pine-Plum Ridge. There, pines stood evergreen through the seasons, plums bore the grace of a thousand years. White cranes soared in rivalry, colorful *luan* birds danced in pairs. Fragrant breezes stole upon us, radiant beauty unfolded—another world entirely.

We sat cross-legged on the open ground, the master and I. Knowing my familiarity with letters, he asked me to compose a poem on the pines. I answered at once. The poem read:

A steadfast soul has long embraced the cold-season’s resolve,
Through endless frost and ice, unbending, never swayed.
Its verdant needles cluster, nourished by the rain’s caress,
Golden pollen sifts like fragrance, scattered by the wind’s embrace.
Roots twist through hidden earth like creatures in deep hibernation,
Its voice pierces layered clouds, roaring like a raging tide.
I charge the woodcutter’s axe: spare wanton felling—
Wait and see it rise as pillars for the halls of heaven’s court.

The master said, *"What you have composed speaks of its use, its outward form, not its transformation. You have yet to grasp what makes the pine the pine—its unyielding integrity, its steadfast constancy. It may offer warmth, it may offer cool, it may endure the snow, yet its rooted foundation remains unshaken. It seizes the essence of heaven and earth, gathers the radiance of sun and moon. Through ages untold, it yields *fuling* from cinnabar caves, works divine wonders among cliffs and peaks, and endures as long as heaven and earth themselves. With the passing of generations, it thrives in lush verdure, soaring to the skies, piercing the clouds—how could its greatness be confined? This is why the pine is revered above all other trees. Do you understand?"*

I bowed my head in acknowledgment and received his teaching.

To compose on the plum, I wrote this poem:

It does not follow the crowd in warm currents,
But on the ridge, its nature remains serene and secluded.
Its fragrant essence drifts unseen on the wind,
Its jade-like form faintly floats in the moonlight.
Where its scent grows rich, birds steal away to slumber;
As its blossoms seem to take flight, a flute leans against the tower.
Turning back, all other flowers retreat in deference—
Who dares vie with it for precedence or claim kinship?

The master said, "Indeed. Yet the plum’s distinction among common blossoms lies still deeper. Aged upon its branches, it blooms anew with spring’s return. With ice-like flesh and jade-like bones, it endures the cold without fading. Not only can it not be transplanted elsewhere, but its original branches never rot or decay. Through rebirth and renewal, its lineage never perishes. Precisely because it does not flaunt lush beauty nor indulge in extravagant display, but delights in seclusion to nurture its innate nature, it stands as companion to the pine and bamboo, while all other flowers and common blossoms fall short.

You—like the pine’s steadfast strength and uprightness, unstained by dust, bearing *fuling* that forms solid fruit; like the plum’s refined elegance and tranquil seclusion, untempted by crimson and purple, with roots firm and seeds yielding jade censers—will not merely rise above the common, but attain everlasting life, shed your form and husk, and become a perfect essence between heaven and earth."

I heard this and closed my eyes in silent contemplation. After a moment, all cares dissolved, every thought emptied. Inwardly, I beheld a clear vision—spirits manifesting, yet I knew not how. The master called, "Awakened?" I replied, "I have just entered this state, still lingering in imagination, unaware of where it leads." The master gave a faint smile and said, "If you know not where it leads, where will you seek its origin? Recall Confucius’ teaching to Zilu—when you enter a state from without, there is still a point of departure. To seek its origin is to find your own unformed self."

As we spoke, the sun sank behind the western crags, and the moon rose over the eastern ridge. The master said, "Let us return." I followed him.

Returning to the original hermitage, I found the overgrown thickets cleared, the path smooth and unobstructed. Upon arrival, a young attendant opened the door to welcome us. Gazing at him, I recognized the same boy I had met descending the mountain when first entering the valley. Inside the hermitage, all furnishings—utensils, bedding, and mats—were renewed, no longer mere earthenware or tiles.

The master sat cross-legged on the couch, while the boy lit incense on the table and served mountain tea. He bade me sit behind the master, facing him. After tea, the boy took up a jade flute and played, its notes like the phoenix singing in the parasol tree, like jade pendants chiming in the Milky Way. Three melodies later, my spirit felt refreshed, all worldly cares dissolved.

The master instructed me to close my eyes in deep meditation, to harmonize the bellows of the heart, to breathe with the void, to turn the Celestial River within the primordial pivot, to revolve the earth’s axis in yellow mud. He taught me to open the twelve mist-laden gates, to traverse the nine winding paths—three hundred sixty-five degrees and a quarter, not a hairbreadth amiss; the sixty-four hexagrams, each one unfolding into eight, not a single measure awry. When summer came, winter departed; when winter departed, summer came—each rooted in the other. At the extreme of movement, stillness returned; at the extreme of stillness, movement arose—alternating in ceaseless flow.

His guidance now clear, the practice became familiar. For one hundred twenty days in the hermitage, I sat in unbroken meditation, fed only dates and chestnuts, drinking only dew, until I forgot sleep and hunger altogether.

One day, the master said, “Your qi is now full. The turbid has dissipated, the pure has congealed; the human has departed, the celestial has returned. Will you leave this place? Go home to visit your parents, then return.”

I knelt in distress, bowing my head: “Master, why speak thus? Why speak thus? Your disciple harbors no such thoughts. My ties to the world are severed, all worldly affairs resolved—why should I return?” The master only smiled and said no more.

Ten days later, he took me to wander in Emerald Void Cave. We climbed steep slopes and jagged peaks, where perilous cliffs and towering rocks stood amid pines and cypresses, deer and cranes gathered in flocks, and purple mushrooms and jade trees intertwined along the path. A fragrant breeze stirred, and songbirds trilled—another realm entirely. How wondrous to behold!

At the summit, a level terrace appeared, with stone benches and tables, clouds and mist forming its curtains, the surrounding peaks its screens. The master and I sat upon the benches, lingering over the view. He asked, “Have mortals ever seen this?” I replied that few had.

The master said, “Two of my teachers once promised to meet me here, so I have brought you along to wait with me.”

Before long, from the depths of the pines and cypresses to the southeast, white cranes appeared, dancing ahead as guides. Two attendants in blue robes followed, bearing whisk brooms, and behind them, two masters. The first had tousled, unkempt hair, large eyes, and a bushy beard; he wore a black robe tied with a brocade sash, his face dark, his ears large, his body stout and tall. Lame in one leg, he leaned on an iron staff, appearing about fifty. The second was gaunt as a crane’s bones, his hair white, his complexion ruddy; he wore a white robe over a blue gown, an angular cap on his head, and a green silk sash. In his hand he held a ruyi scepter and seemed over seventy. Both wore straw sandals, their forms drifting lightly toward us.

We exchanged bows. The lame one took the seat of honor, the master the second, the elder the third, and I was told to sit beside them. I bowed again, asking their names. The lame one tugged at his beard, laughing loudly: “Do you recall the tale of King Ling of Zhou whipping his mother’s grave?” Then I knew him to be Li, the Grand Historian. Only then did I realize this place was truly a realm of immortals.

A spike struck my crown, and my dreaming soul stirred slightly. I bowed again to the three masters. The elder said, “I was born before the Grand Historian—Zhang Guo of Bohai Prefecture.” I kowtowed once more in deep reverence.

Li said, “Do you know who your master is?”
I replied, “Master Jin Zhong.”
All three burst into laughter, clapping their hands. Li said, “Your master is none other than the Han general Zhongli Mei.”
Only then did I realize that *Jin Zhong* was *Zhong*. Overjoyed beyond measure, I thought: all that I had once heard and recited in tales, I now beheld in person before my very eyes.

The master Li said, “This student is a scholar from Jingzhou. Yet even in the moment of begging alms, he failed to awaken—and now he has endured the toils of years. Have you resolved your mind? I fear that if you falter later, regret will turn to sin.”

I awoke further. The three beggars remained before me, their faces unchanged, though their robes were transformed. Rising, I bowed my head: “With these mortal eyes, how could I recognize the assembled masters? For my past transgressions, I can only beg the heavens’ forgiveness.”

The master smiled: “Now we are one family—what blame remains?” We sat again.
“Li and Zhang wish to discuss the Dao with me. Let us hear your thoughts.”

Li spoke: “We and you, fellow sojourners, lodge our traces in floating dust, our forms in clods of earth—hardness not equal to metal, rootedness not equal to trees, liveliness not equal to rivers and deep pools, steadfastness not equal to Mount Tai. Borne along by the wild horses’ tumult, no more enduring than mayflies in their rising and vanishing: such is our general condition.

As for the stirrings of joy and anger, sorrow and delight, they do not match even a flower’s bloom and withering. The vicissitudes of poverty and success, gain and loss, do not match even a season’s shift from cold to heat. Fame and rank turn like a palm’s reversal—graves and mounds, like a palm’s covering—and all too soon, in a flash, they settle beyond reckoning.

Thus, in terms of form: heaven and earth are my greater, while I am heaven and earth’s lesser. In terms of mechanism: the cosmos is but a single breath, while my body is breath unceasing. To know the body’s unceasing breath is to surpass the cosmos’s ceasing breath. To know what ceases, and to know what does not cease—then my body is heaven and earth, heaven and earth are my body, one becomes three, three becomes one. The great is no longer great, the small no longer small, formless and mechanism-less. Thus it has been for ten thousand ages.”

I rose and inquired into the doctrine of the unceasing. Li requested our master to speak. Master Zhong said, “Listen well, disciple.” With a flick of his palm-leaf whisk, he intoned clearly:

The river’s pulse lies in its source—
no pause in its mechanism,
flowing day and night.
Mighty waves surge from Kunlun’s spring.
No brook is left behind,
flooding all nine provinces.
Bearing the celestial virtue’s grace, no seep nor leak.

This root, discerned in stillness,
is one spring and autumn through ten thousand ages.

Master Zhang struck the fisherman’s drum, rapped the bamboo clappers, and joined in harmony:

Each has its source—
behold it,
flowing on the river’s course.
Luminous pearls roll from Floating Bridge’s spring.
A hundred channels like brooks,
nine bends like provinces.
Stored in the fields, let none be lost.
This root, unblocked, is made alive—
a ceaseless torrent, knowing no autumn.

From his seat, Master Li brandished his ruyi, struck the stone with a resonant *clang*, and joined in:

The ancestral qi is the pass—
issuing from the true source,
flowing day and night.
Pity the long-blocked spring!
Mud clogs these brooks,
filth chokes those provinces.

After several breaches in the unformed vessel’s leaks,
this root, now pierced through from this day forth,
unfolds in one unbroken autumn’s flow.

The Master asked, “Little one, do you grasp this?”
After a meal’s span of insight, I replied, “I have glimpsed it.”

Masters Li and Zhang said, “If so, you may compose in the same rhyme, that we may gauge your depth.”
I bowed deeply, stood in attendance, and joined in harmony:

On the river’s course, turn not back—
passing thus, like this,
an unbroken stream.
Now the mechanism’s aperture is breached.
Dredged channels, cleared brooks,
linked rivers, opened provinces.
The vast, surging torrent—how could it leak?
Having grasped the root,
its limpid depths run clear,
enduring through ten thousand autumns.

Master Li rejoiced greatly, saying, “This child may be taught, may be taught indeed.
Now I ask you—whence have you come?”
I answered, “From the place of coming.”
He asked, “And what is there?”
I said, “Luminous and unceasing.”
Master Li laughed, “Luminous and unceasing—what is it that moves in this?”
I had no reply.
Master Li said, “Listen to my words:”

Where was the trace ever found in the first place?
Two and five congeal to form the harmonious body.
At sunrise, a single red gleam from Fusang’s boughs—
a thousand groves of splendor planted in Shangyuan’s gardens.
The aged clam’s bright pearl—how could it have a seed?
The lofty terrace’s precious mirror—yet holds no form.
How many fair and foul images are cast forth,
while the serene realm remains utterly empty.

What is real belongs to him; what is illusory belongs to me.
What exists belongs to him; what is nonexistent belongs to me.
What is stirred by feeling belongs to him; what is still and unmoved belongs to me.
Yet if there is this “me,” there must be that “him.”
Without “him,” one becomes a mere thing, a dried gourd.
Without “me,” one becomes a planted pattern—where then are all these scattered things?

Thus, he must be himself, and I must be myself;
he cannot be dispensed with, yet he does no harm.
I remain utterly unmoved,
while he devises a thousand schemes and a hundred snares.
Yet I sit in solemn dignity, like the great sovereign enthroned in the ninefold palace—
what comes does not depart; what departs is entirely his.

Recognize the path of what comes,
and the path of what goes will not err:
from the original place it comes, to the original place it returns.
Thus, to come is of me, to go is of me—
who could restrain me?
To ascend to heaven is by me, to enter the earth is by me,
to be small is by me, to be great is by me—
who could bind me fast?

Now, the people of this world come by this very path.
At first, they have not gone far and still recognize some of its signs.
But as they press on into vast realms and crowded places,
they forget entirely.
If they turn back in haste, they have not strayed too far.
Yet they know not that along the way, countless enticements
dazzle the eye and beguile the heart,
luring them by day and drawing them by night.
They rent a dwelling, amass gold and treasures,
and just as they settle into a household,
the six thieves scale walls and burrow through holes,
devising every cunning scheme to steal away their wealth,
not ceasing until their home is stripped bare.
Reduced to destitution, their house in ruins—
by day, the light wind whistles through the door;
by night, the fine rain drifts past the window.
Walls collapse, beams crack, pillars snap.
Those who once beguiled them now abandon them,
leaving them nowhere to rest.
Seeking the original path to return,
they find only boundless, indistinct places—
how could they recognize it?
Helpless, they drift east and west,
settling wherever they may,
heedless of good or ill, comfort or hardship,
content to lay down their burden and be done.
Even then, debts and enmities drag them down,
no one in the east will take them in,
no one in the west will keep them,
while the south reviles them.
Suffering in a thousand ways, utterly wretched,
they can only seek shelter in a haystack or a crumbling wall,
each step leading them further astray,
so far from their origin that it might as well be the Central Plains—
never again will they find their way back.
How pitiful, how pitiful indeed.
All this is the harm he inflicts, the ruin he brings.

Thus, I must take command of myself,
seek out the source of my coming—
this is to return to the root and revert to the origin,
to return to the clan and restore the ancestry.
Heaven and earth are my dwelling,
yin and yang my husband and wife,
sun and moon my lamps,
rivers and lakes my cups,
the eternal mountains my clods of earth,
wind and clouds my unfolding,
rain and dew my saliva—
how did it begin?
How will it end?
From whence did it come?

Whence does it go?
What is life?
What is death?
Surveying Qin and Chu, observing the gains of rivals,
glimpsing the foolishness of Chiyou and Xiang—
all this is but a laughing matter.
What then of gain and loss?"

I asked, "May I dare inquire what method brings one to this state?"
The master replied, "The techniques differ, and so too their returns to the origin.
One must interweave and cross-examine,
seeking the utmost, the most profound, the subtlest, the deepest—
only then is it refined.

Let me explain to you:
Those who dine on pine needles and consume elixirs
cannot swiftly transcend;
life and death are fixed in their breath—
how can they forget form?
Focusing on the hundred meridians,
visualizing the cinnabar field,
regulating breath to a thread—
yet the fetal state remains unformed.
Holding breath and concentrating thought,
perceiving the profound silence of the primordial—
still, in the end, they fail to ascend.
Those who take the mouth and nose as the Mysterious Female
are like wind trapped in a net.
Those who regard the square inch as the heart’s field
see the ancestor but forget the clan.
If one accumulates essence as celestial lead,
the cinnabar and mercury remain incomplete.
Treating spirit and qi as mother and child,
the terrace of immortality lies far beyond.
Opening the crucible for refinement,
labor is spent in vain;
refining mercury and balancing tortoises—
these are but perverse and deluded arts.
Three years, nine cycles—
how can the firing time suffice?
Holding breath to absorb the primordial,
the yin wind howls in response.
Those who recognize mind and see nature
may have a head, yet end as spectral beings.
Sitting at midnight and noon—
though this is effort, it is but rigid and constrained mystery.
Is the tongue truly the Crimson Dragon?
Eyes closed like a fox’s breath—
the crown as the Yellow Court,
the kidneys as creation—
mired and unpenetrated.
The tail palace as life,
the foot apertures as the source—
substance without transformation.

More still: consuming water and fire to return to the origin—
the path of error grows ever wider.
Absorbing essence through dual cultivation,
the road of entry strays further.
Gazing at the heavens to draw in the essence of sun and moon,
disregarding celestial soul and earthly spirit—
a single meal spanning day and night,
only to invite emaciation and frailty.
Curving the body, lying prostrate,
knocking the five gates to sound a long cry—
what benefit is there?
The Way of inner cultivation lies in guarding the primordial and embracing the One.
Turning the twin pupils inward to reflect—
this too is not the near path.
The gate of the oral formula:
raising the shoulders, craning the neck,
circulating the spine’s flow—
the hundred meridians may open, yet one aperture remains dim.
Inscribing talismans, chanting incantations,
pledging contracts in clear words—
the hundred evils may be dispelled, yet one spirit is obscured.
Worshipping the Dipper, gazing at stars—
labor spent in vain, no aid to sighing.
Massaging and pressing, practicing arts—
what merit in such methods?
The talk of exhaling turbid and inhaling pure is crude;
the discourse on eating plain and fasting is base.
Holding registers and performing rites,
setting up altars—
all devoid of virtue.
Using bellows and keys to gather essence and coalesce spirit—
only to fall into the path of transgression.
The ancestral qi of the primordial
does not reside in form, image, or imitation;
the sacred medicine of the Great One
how could it depend on smelting lead or refining mercury?

Transcending the realms of the multitude,
awakening to the formless within the formless;
entering the Mysterious Pass,
discerning the auspicious within the existent.
Unfolding the lotus within fire,
drawing forth the pearl from water.
In the cauldron of dragon and tiger,
no need to seize—naturally subdued;
in the furnace of tortoise and snake,
no need for forging—silently refined.
In movement,
true stillness without motion;
in emptiness,
firmness without void.
There is something prior to heaven and earth,
formless yet within the unshaped embryo.
A voice in the valley echoes through a thousand mountains;
waves on the river stir a hundred seas.
So many afflictions—
unrelated to these myriad endeavors,
heedless as withered wood.
Yet when spring dew touches the withered,
it revives like dead ashes;
when fire and wind scatter the ashes,
one recognizes the magpie’s chariot ascending layered heavens.
The fluid of the jade elixir congeals in the Crimson Palace.
Why must the Yellow Dame mediate marriage?
Why must the Infant be nurtured to completion?
Drawing from Kan to fill Li,
mending the torn patch of the monk’s robe;
Qian revolves, Kun turns—
rebuilding the crumbled walls.
Those who probe the Mysterious Pass of a single aperture
cling to deluded views;
those who point to the nine-fold elixir method
hold shallow discourse.
Riding the golden ox to roam the eight wilds,
reversing the Yellow River through the three barriers—
all are but dust with traces,
not the supreme vehicle of the formless.

Within the twelve hours, it is qi;
within the chest, it is spirit.
If one merely wanders among a hundred paths and thousand byways,
in the end, one flows into the realm of gods, demons, and ghosts—
able to seize a vessel, yet also to be reborn.
Nine revolutions, ten transformations—
if one can transcend the mundane,
then one can enter sagehood.
A single surge spanning a thousand leagues—
know that refining stone can mend heaven,
then believe that ascending clouds enables transformation.
This living, vibrant thing,
like a rolling pearl upon the earth;
this radiant, luminous thing,
outshining the bright moon in the sky.
Unmired in stagnation,
unobscured by dimness—
seeing through the mechanism of the aperture,
leaping beyond the gate of life and death.
Heaven and earth share my rise and fall,
the cosmos my duration and transience.
Unbreakable, unseverable,
unscorched by fire, undampened by water.
Though the seas overturn and rivers surge,
the heart does not dance, the spirit does not stir.
This is the most profound mystery,
yet not a matter of many techniques.
One’s own affair must be sought by oneself—
to ask another is already error.
Coming by one path,
going by one path—
to stray elsewhere is to lose the way.

You have affinity; how could I remain silent?"
Having spoken, two youths bore platters of fruit, nuts, and wine cups,
arranging them upon a flat stone.
I bowed in gratitude for the instruction and bade him sit again.
Gazing upon the spread:
peaches like great bowls,
lotus roots like flat boats;
the pears of Hebei surpassing,
the jujubes of Jiaozhou exceptional;
melons large as cauldrons,
oranges brimming with chess pieces.
As if simmering dragon’s liver—yet the flavor more exquisite;
as if stewing phoenix’s wing—yet the form unlike.

The crane’s breast needs no preparation,
the phoenix’s embryo forms not in smoke;
the ape’s lips require no ape,
the leopard’s marrow no leopard.
Jade nectar, liquid gem—
seeping into amber and glass;
mushrooms of hemp, essence of greens—
their savor brushing the azure vault and jade firmament.

After long toasts, white cranes and green phoenixes danced in flocks,
their rises and falls keeping time,
their bows and lifts measured.
Gracefully they passed beyond the eight ridges,
their harmony surpassing the seven virtues.
When the dance ended, they stood in ranks to left and right.
Several green-robed youths arranged themselves below,
beating time as they sang.

The first song:
The two qi breathe in and out—
the valley echoes with sound.
Sweeping a thousand mountains’ fallen leaves,
parting the nine deserts’ gloom.
Seek it—no shadow;
observe it—no form.
Suddenly still,
suddenly present.
Not to be bound by traces,
nor easily companioned on the way.
Not swift yet swift,
not present yet numinous.
From the dense grove it lifts its head;
from the roaring tiger it springs to life.
Opening my robe in one sweep,
I feel my thoughts refreshed, my spirit clear.

The second song:
Receiving the radiant qi of Heaven and Earth,
it flourishes in spring’s fullness.
Receiving the Craftsman’s artful touch,
it charms in bright clarity.
Adorning a thousand reds and a hundred purples,
it vies with the branches’ rare blossoms;
nurturing pure fragrance and graceful essence,
it exhales a heart of rich perfume.
Though wild bees madly dart,
its essence remains unbroken;
though wanton butterflies swarm,
its truth is not lost.
Its root and stem stand firm,
bearing fruit again and again.
Through years and months it endures,
lifting high through frost and dew.

The third song:
Like the two qi’s subtle blending,
the myriad things transform in harmony.
Pervading all with clarity and peace,
above and below in profusion.
Spanning the six directions in flight,
unlike wild horses’ headlong rush.
Covering the nine lands in shelter,
unmoved by carved jade’s splendor.
Scattered, it needs no restraint;
gathered, it takes form.
Refining yin’s spirit to fill the space between,
transforming yang’s spirit—none know where it abides.
Wondrously shifting, never fixed,
delighting in temple halls and tavern signs alike.
What makes this flesh and bone?
Only silver pheasant’s plumes and crane’s feathers.

The fourth song:
Holding this yin essence,
it reflects the great yang.
Bright and unwavering,
radiant with light.
Like a dragon spitting a jewel,
rolling forth from the Milky Way.
Adorning the mid-heaven with constant brilliance,
its pure glow alone shines.
No thing remains unillumined—
like a treasury of wisdom to discern right and wrong.
No place is left untouched—
like a sage’s jade dispensing kindness.
Enviable, that single spark in the spirit’s terrace,
through ten thousand ages unchanged.
Though waxing and waning, it never ceases,
awakening this benighted world.

When the youths’ songs ended, they played the *Xiaoyaoyue*.
Golden sheng, jade pipes, phoenix flutes, dragon xiao,
silk se zither harmonizing with ice strings,
jade clappers keeping time with flower drums.
The high notes soared like cranes crying over the nine marshes,
piercing the layered clouds;
the low notes flowed like ten thousand ravines’ fine streams,
cascading through deep gorges.
At times slow—like Yao pacing, Shun stepping,
bowing and yielding in the ancestral hall;
at times swift—like a single ox or feathered rider,
racing through the ranks.
Sometimes swelling in unison—
like officials arrayed at court,
daring not overstep before the Son of Heaven.
Sometimes restrained, stepping in measure—
like a vast herd at pasture,
when all things revive yet scales and tails still pause.

Then the cranes stretched their necks to join,
the phoenixes flapped their wings and sang,
the phoenix tuned its tongue to sound,
the parrot cleared its throat to follow the rhythm.
Listening intently, one felt the spirit soar and the heart sway,
drunk with joy, forgetting the vastness of heaven and earth,
blurring self and other in shared delight—
beyond words.

When the music ended, the two cranes stepped forward, raised their heads,
and spoke in song, voicing Daoist sentiments.
The lyrics said:

The Azure Cloud Hermitage, far from the bustle of towns and courts,
where fine dust never rises—truly secluded and serene.
A thousand emerald cypresses stand among golden osmanthus,
a few jade-flowered trees interspersed with crimson peaches,
while fragrant breezes swirl around the lotus pool.

Behold the ancient steps layered in tiers,
phoenix-tail ferns entwined with sacred lingzhi,
and jade-like herbs carpeting every path.
Flowers bloom in all four seasons, never fading,
while thousand-year pines stand firm and proud.
Amid such scenes, countless vistas unfold,
and joy overflows like wine.

Again they sang:
A shallow stream, a humble bridge,
where jade-like pearls tumble from cliffs into the ravine,
crimson clouds dazzle, adorning the mountains in splendor,
a bright moon, full and round, hangs at the ridge’s peak,
while birds sing in the branches and human traces vanish.

Behold the stone benches beneath pine shade,
where pebbles are tapped in rhythm,
oblivious to thirst or hunger.
In the empty sky, kites soar in formation,
on calm waters, dragons coil and glide.
Here, boundless delight unfolds—
true freedom at last.

The song ended, and with several cranes, they flapped their wings,
leapt and stretched their necks, circling left and right,
bending low and rising tall—
a thousand postures, ten thousand forms.
After their dance, they let out a piercing cry,
their voices shaking the nine marshes,
halting the flight of clouds,
resounding through the heavens,
leaving one’s heart unbound and spirit soaring,
mind clear and qi flowing.

The Three Masters drank deeply from great goblets,
their hearts open in mutual toast,
as the hours passed—
unaware how many days and months of the world had slipped by.
When cups and plates lay empty,
and dishes were nearly bare,
Li and Zhang spoke:
“Today’s gathering was to open a passage for Master Lü.
You may transcend, you may transform—
come with us now,
do not linger through the years,
lest you miss the appointed time.”

Master Zhong assented in agreement.

Li and Zhang turned to me and said, “Hold firm, hold firm—awaken swiftly, awaken swiftly. We await you.” Then they mounted their cranes and departed westward. I gazed after them in longing, heart gladdened that they had joined the immortals’ journey. Yet my master had not taken me along, and this weighed heavily on my mind.

The master said, “The two gentlemen have gone. Why not return to the hermitage with me? Was today’s gathering joyful? Can you find joy without clinging to joy, and ease without being troubled by sorrow? Can you meet without attachment to meeting, and part without resistance to parting? Bless them as yourself, bless yourself as them—then their joy is your serenity, their sorrow your repose. Those who gather are bound, yet you remain free; those who scatter are lost, yet you stay unburdened. This is the solid within the empty, the empty within the solid. In every word and deed, you must not let a single moment slip by.”

I received his teaching and returned to the hermitage, where I served him with unwavering devotion. My reverence was single-minded, my devotion earnest, ever renewed and flourishing. By day, we discussed secrets beneath the shade of pines and bamboo; by night, we delved into the profound through silent contemplation, glimpsing the true face of our original nature and shattering the illusions of the Three Mysteries and Seven Thieves. From the depths of a ten-thousand-foot abyss, a pair of eyes and five tigers leapt forth; within a pit of fire, nine golden lotuses bloomed. A pearl rolled across the plate, six swords unsheathed from their caskets. Chaos gave way to the first stirrings of life—budding, restless, then settling into clarity, the muddy waves stilled. Neither being nor non-being, neither emptiness nor non-emptiness—truly, all actions are but phantoms, while radiant truth lies in stillness. Before the puppet stage, strings are tugged in vain; in the foundry’s hands, iron and bronze are melted to no purpose. The tangible is false, the formless is real. Here, a flake of snow flies in the furnace’s fierce blaze, a hair of ice descends into the boiling cauldron. The house is left behind, empty—several times I roamed the six directions; walls stood in vain—countless somersaults carried me to the Three Terraces. To exist is to be spirit; to think is to transform. No need for search, no labor for conjecture.

Knowing my practice had reached fulfillment, the master called to me one day: “The formless has already revealed itself. The formed is yet to be known. Return along the ancient Way to the old homeland. Once, in Puyin Village, you met my master and vowed to become a carefree youth. Now he has fallen into an inauspicious path—you must teach him the secret method, guide him back to the mountain, then swiftly return to the hermitage. Do not let worldly dust entangle you or obscure the road ahead.”

That day, he entrusted me with a whisk and a begging bowl. I received them, bowed in farewell, and rode a pure wind straight to Huoshui Village. There, I transformed into a mendicant Daoist, wandering the roads. After a day of searching, I learned my parents had passed. Only Liu-shi remained, having kept her chastity as a nun; the family home had become a Daoist abbey, where she and a few elderly nuns chanted sutras before the bodhisattva’s image. I entered directly, and the nun scolded me: “This is a convent, a pure and precept-bound sanctuary. How can a male Daoist simply barge in?”

I bowed my head in apology and addressed the assembly: “This poor Daoist comes from a distant mountain wilderness, ignorant of proper conduct, and has trespassed upon your sacred abbey. Yet as fellow renunciates, when a monk comes, you see the Buddha’s face. I have no clothes to cover my body, no food to fill my stomach, and few acquaintances in this world. Seeking refuge in your abbey, I humbly beg for alms of clothing and food. Within ten days, should I encounter a generous patron, I will repay your kindness and return to my mountain. I implore you to open the gate of universal salvation, to magnify the virtue of delivering others—do not turn away this mountain dweller. Your compassion will be deeply felt.”

The chief seat glanced at me and ordered the manager: “Give this Daoist a liter of alms-grain and send him on his way to find lodging elsewhere. Though this is but a small abbey, it is a private residence, and moreover filled entirely with women—how can we accommodate a man? It would not be proper.”

I refused the alms-grain and made a bow to the chief seat: “Venerable one, venerable one, I came to this sacred abbey for a reason. If you will not permit me to stay, and I am given alms-grain, who will cook it for me? If you would but lend me a young brother to keep me company, I will leave at once. If you refuse, I will remain here and never step outside for a thousand years.”

Hearing this, the chief seat flushed crimson and flew into a rage, cursing: “This wild Daoist is utterly without manners! This is a sanctuary of the Buddha’s disciples, a pure Dharma-gate—how dare you spout such nonsense here! You are no madman who has lost his senses, yet you utter such reckless words. If you do not leave at once, we shall deal with you by the Dharma’s principles.”

I laughed and said: “Fine nuns, fine nuns—you speak of leaving the household and purity, but where is your purity to be seen? You have not even renounced the south, yet you presume to become Buddhas? Listen to what I tell you: that Amitābha is no trifling secret.”

"To begin the work, one must first extinguish the nameless. Hold fast with a heart as unyielding as iron; let not even a trace of male or female stir warmth in it. Gather and extinguish all joy, anger, sorrow, and delight of the mortal world; sever all ties to clothing, food, and bodily needs in this dusty realm. Then, even if right and wrong, strength and weakness contend and trample, or blades and cauldrons of boiling oil assail you, my wisdom-eye and Chan heart shall remain unmoved—this is the supreme secret of attaining Buddhahood and ascending beyond."

Hearing this, the chief seat bowed her head for a moment, then stepped down from her seat and prostrated before me, saying, "This humble nun’s fleshly eyes failed to recognize your loftiness. I beg for your instruction."

I asked, "Venerable sister, in what year did you leave the household? How many years have passed since?"

The nun replied, "I was once the wife of a scholar. When my husband left to seek official rank and never returned, my parents-in-law and parents passed away, leaving me alone to keep my chastity. With a few like-minded companions, I have practiced the Way at home for several years now."

I said, "Would you, venerable sister, leave the household under my guidance?"

She answered, "How could a woman follow a man? Such conduct would invite reproach. My heart is as steadfast as stone—do not take this as jest, noble one, but instruct me in the Chan principle. If you insist on such stingy teaching, I shall take my leave at once, lest you sow doubt and disrupt my pure precepts."

I said, "Venerable sister, since ancient times, who has truly attained Buddhahood? Among women of the world, the fortunate ones marry a husband—some ride in grand carriages, wear purple robes and golden belts, dwell in perfumed chambers and embroidered pavilions, delighting in morning joy and evening pleasure, honored with golden seals and purple decrees, living as ladies of rank and enjoying glory; others amass gold and jade, adorn themselves with pearls and treasures, feast on rare delicacies, celebrate every dawn and festival, called *yuànjūn* or *māma*. What could be more blissful than this? Instead, you forsake a fine match, squander your youth, eat plain gruel and tasteless rice, wear coarse robes and tattered garments. In the desolate gate of emptiness, you dread the howling wind and rain; in the bleak, cold chamber, you sorrow at the dripping water-clock. In the tender beauty of spring, you weep as you watch others break hairpins and play games; in the scorching heat of summer, your heart aches as you listen to songs in the cool pavilion. The autumn wind soughs, and you resent the migrating geese; the winter snow drifts, and you grieve over your iron-hard quilt. After enduring such hardship, in old age, you will have no children to see you off in death. Of the countless who leave the household, not one has become a Buddha. Why not return to the world? I shall not leave it—how does that strike you? How does that strike you?"

The nun, having heard these words, flared into a great rage, and barked at the attendant to drive the Daoist out and bolt the convent gate. I thought to myself: *My words have angered her—how then shall I help her transcend the mortal coil?* So I transformed myself, entered the sutra hall directly, and with a flick of my whisk, incense smoke curled and celestial blossoms rained down. The attendant hastened to report to the nun: “That Daoist was just shut out, yet now he stands in the sutra hall, whisk in hand, sending incense aloft and flowers cascading.”

Startled, the nun emerged and said: “Immortal brother, pray do not display your numinous powers to disturb this disciple. This disciple is resolved unto death, with no other thoughts. I beg you to take your leave elsewhere.”

Perceiving the sincerity of her heart, I said: “Liu Gu, would you like to see your husband?”

The nun replied: “My husband has been gone so long—whether he lives or has died, I know not. Even if he were to return now, I would never again consort with him as a mortal. If he comes, I shall have him choose another fine match; I vow never to meet him again.”

I laughed and said: “This poor Daoist and your husband both left the household under Master Jin Zhong. He has already attained the right fruit and descended the mountain with me. If I bid him come to see you, you may look—he approaches from behind.” The nun turned her head. I then revealed my true form. When the nun looked at me again, she stood in silent loss, her numinous radiance now visible, and bowed to me, saying: “My husband has already attained the Dao—why not deliver me as well?”

I reached into my pouch and took out a single elixir, bidding her to swallow it. Then, with the tip of her tin staff, I traced a crane and commanded her to mount it. Suddenly, Master Zhong’s voice rang out from midair, halting us to await his decree, and the crane reverted to a staff.

I imparted a gatha:
*At midnight, the heavens raise a gleaming moon;
In the third winter’s furnace, jade snow takes flight.
The finest nectar fills ten thousand acres of fields—
No mundane flame need melt the śarīra.*

Hark!
*Three lives were not the Husband-Watching Stone—
One kick sends you soaring past Heaven’s Gate.*

Having inscribed the gatha, I brushed my robes and departed the nunnery. Arriving at the ancestral burial grounds, I inquired of the mountain spirit. The spirit reported that my grandfather, grandmother, and mother had all ascended to the immortal realm and were no longer recorded among the ghosts. Delighted, I returned to Pu Yin Village.

When I was still more than ten *li* away, I sought alms among the village households, inquiring after their traces. An old man with flowing white hair leaned on his staff by the door, sighing ceaselessly, his sorrowful countenance plain to see. I clasped my hands in greeting and begged for alms. The elder asked: “Where does the master come from?” I told him of my quest for alms.

The elder pointed toward the village ahead: “Master must not take that shady, overgrown path—there is something strange there, so terrifying to speak of. At times it takes human form, at others the shape of tree branches, or of tigers and wolves, or of ghosts and specters. Sometimes it appears as a woman to lure young men, sometimes as a tavern to entice travelers to drink, sometimes roaring like thunder shaking the valleys, sometimes leaping like a dragon surging from the deep. Its transformations are endless, its malevolent forms myriad. Those who encounter it are ground to powder; those who meet it are shattered to pieces. Roughly like the willow twig of Liu Wo, it has claimed no fewer than a hundred lives. If no passersby are to be found, it seizes men and women from nearby villages, young and old alike, throwing households into panic and disarray. No law can restrain it, no talisman subdue it—who would not fear it? Before, my youngest son was herding sheep when it ensnared and devoured him whole. Master, you must be on guard. Should you meet this demon, your life will be no more, your body obliterated, no different than if you had ascended in feathered transformation. What can you do, Master?”

Upon hearing this, I reproached myself deeply. This was my fault—I had brought this sorrow upon them, this harm upon all. My Dao remained unfulfilled, yet I had first wrought calamity; my merit remained unaccumulated, yet my sins were already complete. What words could I offer in my defense? Fortunately, my master had guided me here, and there was still time to avert future disaster. Otherwise, the harm would only grow more severe.

I bid the old man farewell and set off toward the dense forest. He called after me several times, but I did not respond and pressed onward. After roughly a *li*, a sudden gale erupted, whipping up sand and dust for ten thousand miles, uprooting towering trees, with the force of overturning mountains and upending seas. My feet could not stand firm, my body could not remain upright—I knew the demon had begun its work. I swept my whisk several times, and the wind subsided. Soon after, a black mist enveloped all, connecting heaven and earth, blotting out sun and moon, obscuring mountains and rivers. Day became as dark as the deepest night; even faces before me were unrecognizable. A thunderous roar grew louder, closing in upon me. I swept my whisk again, focusing my spirit with full intent. The mist dispersed into the empty mountains, the clouds returned to the vast sea, and clarity shone once more.

After another *li* or so, a pack of jackals and wolves suddenly charged forth, jaws gaping and fangs bared, intent on devouring me. I raised a finger and shouted, *“Cease! Do not be so insolent!”* The pack of jackals and wolves turned out to be but a few willow branches. As I examined them, a deafening crash resounded—the southern mountain split at its peak, the northern ridge shattered the layered heavens, the sound so terrifying it struck fear into the heart and shattered the gall. A creature resembling a *yaksha* leapt forth: its eyes blazed like lanterns, its mouth bristled with blades, its hair tangled like wild foliage, its body gnarled like twisted deadwood. Naked in form, unclothed in body, it brandished a wolf-toothed cudgel, leaping and bounding toward me, intent on devouring me. Overpowered, I cried out, *“Master! Master!”* I sat cross-legged, allowing it to flail its limbs, yet it dared not approach me. After concentrating my spirit for a moment, I exhaled a single breath, and it turned and bounded away.

I rose again and walked another *li* or so, only to find the path ahead blocked by a towering branch, as high as a hill, with not a single gap to pass through. Turning back, I saw the way behind choked with brambles, thorns, and trifoliate orange, trapping me in between. Unable to advance or retreat, my heart filled with dread. I cried out urgently, *“Yitong! Yitong! How have you lost your way to this? Have you so soon forgotten the bond between master and disciple? I came here for your sake, to save you from this harm—how could you now turn and harm me?”* As I finished speaking, with a sudden crack, a willow tree dropped from the sky, ensnaring me. Yet it dared not harm me outright, for a flicker of spiritual light still remained unextinguished within me. I called out again, *“Master! Master!”* With a wave of my hand, the tree dispersed.

After another *li*, I neared the place where he had hanged himself. The ancient trees stood desolate, no trace of human habitation, not even a bird in flight. The scene was far more desolate than when I had passed through before. In the distance, I saw a Daoist, his form ethereal as if beyond the material world, his bearing majestic with the aura of one who had attained the Dao. Clad in a green robe and blue sash, a yellow cap and jade-green shoes, he held a pure broom. Smiling, he greeted me: *“Fellow Daoist, where have you come from? Would you care to rest awhile in my humble abode?”* Knowing this was Yitong, I followed him to a thatched hut. Its courtyard, shaded by verdant trees, was serene and lovely.

I sat, and he took a seat beside me. Suddenly, he began discoursing on the art of the external elixir, speaking also of absorbing yin to supplement yang, of harming others to benefit oneself. I merely assented. After a short while, he rose and served tea and fruit.

I partook of the refreshments, showing no sign of surprise. Pleased, he smiled, believing he had won me over to his methods. Little did he know I had secretly gathered them into my gourd.

Then I enticed him, saying: *“Brother Daoist, dwelling here, your neighbors are trees and stones, your companions deer and swine. At dawn, the wind sighs through ten thousand hollows; at dusk, your solitary form lingers in stillness. With so little seen or heard, how can you unlock the mysterious gate? Without knowledge or perception, how can you achieve the nine-fold elixir? A secluded realm seals the dust from the sun’s gaze; a clouded mind obscures the path to heaven. Once ensnared in the web of desire, you will never escape the cage. If you plant the roots of sin, you must tend to them yourself; if you cultivate good deeds, you will never be at a loss. Heaven admits no wicked souls, while hell spares no virtuous ones. The nine-tailed fox devours men—how cruel!—yet cannot escape the bears of the Wei River; Yulei consumes ghosts—how mighty!—yet still falls to the scholar of Zhongnan Mountain. Since ancient times, those who accumulate goodness suffer no calamity—when have the violent ever avoided disaster? For your present course, it would be better to abandon this shady retreat and follow me along the lesser path to famed mountains and sacred realms. Seek out an enlightened master, pay homage to the true sovereign, and enjoy the boundless bliss of freedom, leaving behind the torments of the fiery pit. By shrinking the earth, you may ascend to heaven; through longevity, you may attain deathlessness. Weighing the two, which is the better choice?”*

He only laughed without answering, turning away to hem and haw for a long while. The moon shone white on the eastern ridge; in the dim window, he held a candle. His heart remained obstinate, showing not the slightest sign of repentance—instead, he mocked me with jesting words. Suddenly, he rose as if to relieve himself. I sat in silence, watching for any movement.

Then, all at once, a sharp *clang*—like a blade or axe hurled against bare stone—made me shudder and tremble, my heart pounding with dread. Before I could discern its cause, a piercing whistle cut the air. With a fierce bound, he leapt before me: his face livid, his eyes blood-red, fangs bared, a willow leaf sprouting from his skull like disheveled hair. His feet were gnarled as willow roots, his hands like withered branches, his body knotted and twisted. He needed not unleash his malice to strike terror—his very form and visage were enough to shatter a man’s courage. Even the hellish minions I had witnessed in the Yellow Millet Dream’s underworld—those grotesque, laughable, shocking, and fearsome specters—paled beside this apparition.

He wiped his hands and feet, bared his fangs, and glared, advancing and retreating with a menacing, hesitant air. I sat unmoving, eyes closed, preserving the primordial *qi*, imagining my master at my side. At the sound of a single cough, the demon vanished, leaving only the empty space beside me. The moon sank, stars faded, clouds dispersed, and the sun rose. Crows broke the dawn’s stillness; dogs barked in alarm. All around, the world was silent, devoid of any trace.

Brushing off my robes, I strode toward the shade of the green locust tree and wandered the mountain paths, lost in thought: *"If I depart in haste, the wayward youth will surely fall into wickedness, betraying my vow to deliver him. To break my oath to heaven would be a failing in my Daoist practice—how could I bear it? Yet if I linger here and the demon vanishes without a trace, where shall I seek him again?"* Caught between these two dilemmas, I hesitated as the sun dipped westward, shifting into the hour of *You*.

Suddenly, a fragrant breeze stirred, carrying an unusual scent. From the east appeared a young beauty, barely eighteen, her brows delicate as fresh willow shoots, her star-like eyes clear as still water. Her hair was coiled like the raven clouds of Wu Gorge, her cheeks rivaled the red apricots of Shanglin Park. No Chu maiden could match her radiance; no Wu beauty dare vie with her grace. Her jade hairpins were exquisitely small, her golden bracelets finely wrought. Her crow-black robe fluttered lightly, her moon-white skirt swayed gently. Her shoes outshone Pan Feiyan’s; her smile surpassed Bao Si’s—no need for the rending of silk to captivate. Truly, she stirred the heart without a hint of suspicion.

She carried a basket of bamboo shoots, passing by me before turning back, her gaze lingering. After half a *li*, she faced me again, set down her basket, and bowed deeply, saying: *"This humble woman is Yang of the Eastern Mountain. My husband passed away just a month after our wedding. With no parents-in-law above nor children below, and my own parents long gone, my brothers few, I vowed to remain chaste in his memory. For a full year, I have lived in solitude among the thatched huts. Today marks the anniversary of his death, and I gathered these shoots in the mountains to offer as a memorial. Yet, poor and alone, I lack the means to invite monks or Daoists to pray for his soul’s ascent to the celestial registers. My fidelity may endure, but how can his spirit find peace? Fortune has brought me this chance meeting with an immortal master. Though I dared not impose earlier, I feared losing this opportunity forever. Thus, I swallow my shame and beg your indulgence. If you possess even a shred of compassion, bestow your mercy upon my humble home and perform a simple rite for my late husband. Both the living and the dead would be forever grateful."*

I observed her composed demeanor and refined speech, her grasp of the Three Bonds and Five Constants, her adherence to the Three Obediences and Four Virtues. Convinced of her sincerity—though unversed in scripture—I initially refused. Yet her pleas grew so earnest that I reluctantly followed her.

We arrived at a modest but elegant dwelling, its walls adorned with ink-wash landscapes, a spirit altar set within. Entering the hall, she urged me to take the seat of honor, but I declined. *"I bow to my husband, not to you, immortal master—why refuse?"* After the bow, she took the bamboo shoots inside. The sun had set.

I thought of the adage: *"Tying one’s shoe in a melon field invites suspicion of theft; adjusting one’s hat beneath a plum tree risks false accusation."* Thus, I excused myself to leave. She replied: *"Immortal master, you are mistaken."*

Here stood no village ahead, no inn behind, nor any nunnery or temple—where could one find shelter? My dwelling is pure and clean, fit for your stay. Though storms may rage, they never cross my threshold, and my chaste resolve remains unstained. Would I rather couple wantonly with a stranger than unite through proper matchmakers with a worthy mate? Would I cast aside a thousand years of fragrant reputation in a single day—something even wood and earth would scorn, yet I would do?

Convinced by her earnest discourse, each word ringing true, I settled my heart and listened. Soon, she placed the bamboo shoots upon the table, and we sat facing each other. She made no further mention of the ritual for her husband, only urging me to eat, her eyes and brows beckoning. I sensed her ill intent, yet the night was still, and escape impossible—moreover, the gates were bolted fast. I steadfastly held to my resolve, unmoved.

She deployed a thousand wiles, a hundred charms, saying: *"Immortal master, this meeting today is heaven’s ordained union, not of mortal making. You are a husband long parted from his wife, and I, a woman dwelling in resentment. Dry wood meets raging fire, a parched fish finds living water—we must not let this moment slip."*

I remained silent, sitting still. She rose, lifting her chopsticks, and said: *"I have composed a new lyric; pray, lend your ears."*

The verse went:
Swift flies the time,
years hasten unseen—
years hasten unseen,
fair scenes and fine hours
must be enjoyed to the full.

Golden cups invite the host’s delight,
the bridal chamber loves the lotus candle’s light—
lotus candle’s light,
neck-entwined mandarin ducks,
all men envy such delight.

*"How do you find this?"*

I gave no answer.

She said: *"I have composed a new melody; pray, lend your ears once more."*

The song went:
Loose raven tresses half unbound,
a beauty’s grace—
jade bones, skin like ice.
Distant mountains, brows of sparse green,
autumn waves in limpid eyes, brimming with passion.
More lovely still, a single point of cherry lips,
peach cheeks half unfurled.
Fine silk veils tender bamboo,
light robes reveal soft flesh,
a waist so slender, so supple.

She continued:
A fair-faced beauty to share,
in the bloom of her nineteenth year, all the more enchanting.
Her bearing so winsome, so bewitching,
in plain adornment or rich, ever radiant.
Spring’s allure stirs the soul to melt;
silken curtains, brocade hangings,
phoenixes paired, lovebirds entwined.
The savor within—you must know it!

*"Immortal master, do not squander this hour. Join with me in the joy of marriage, tie this red thread of fate, bear sons and daughters, build a household, uphold your ancestors’ century-old line, and extend your descendants’ thousand generations of lineage. Is this not far better than a solitary form, a lone shadow, drifting east at dawn and west at dusk like a stray dog, a masterless spirit, a floating duckweed or drifting twig? In life, lacking sustenance; in death, no foundation for offerings—do you wish to be but chaff to fill the gullies?"*

Only then, hearing her wanton words, did I realize she was a wayward spirit in disguise.

I said, "Lady, do not let delusion plunge you into the Three Wretched Realms! Hear now the perils of love and desire between man and woman."

With the handle of my whisk, I struck the table to mark the rhythm, and sang:

The human body’s essence and qi flow like water,
all a hundred meridians rely on their fullness.
That single spark of true yang—cherish it well,
for lusting after flowers and willows brings only ruin.
Like withered wood or dried roots in the northern wind,
when moisture dries and waters run dry, years cannot last.
Fair maidens are axes that hew the body,
beauty is truly the worm that gnaws the tree.
Bewitching demons of passion toy with men—
though not sharp blades or gleaming swords,
they have slain every hero.
Let others adore the bloom of rosy cheeks,
I keep company with mist and clouds, rivers and peaks.
The resolve of Mount Tai cannot be shaken;
treasure essence, nurture qi to sustain the body.
Do not fall into the snare of fleeting pleasures,
for even joy is but a springtime dream.
The clear wind is my companion,
the bright moon my silken-canopied friend.
When husband and wife are content as mandarin ducks,
that is the true delight of heaven and earth—
what need for powdered faces and rouged lips?

"In our Daoist way, yin and yang are husband and wife,
wind and moon are friends,
flowers and birds bring joy,
mountains and waters stir the spirit.
Fruits and berries fill the belly,
spring streams quench thirst,
grass makes clothing, hemp makes sandals,
deer and cranes serve as attendants,
clouds and mist are our chariots,
heaven and earth our home,
the four seas our livelihood.
What greater bliss could there be?
What need to build estates or beget descendants?
We endure while heaven and earth endure,
remain ever-new through the ages—
what need for ancestral rites or grave offerings?"

At these words, the woman flushed with shame and dared not draw near.

Then I recalled, "My master once gave me a small gourd containing several pellets of elixir, saying they could be taken to perceive the mind’s true nature. This spirit has lost its original bearings and forgotten its roots—perhaps this elixir will help."

I reached into my sash, drew out the gourd, and poured the pills onto the table. Pointing at them, I said to her, "Eat these, and you will never hunger again. Their fragrance is sweet, their taste exquisite."

She smiled but paid no heed. I pressed her several times, yet she refused to look. As I returned the gourd to my sash, the woman transformed into a willow spirit—fangs bared, claws outstretched, ready to seize me.

I placed the gourd back on the table, leaned against the armrest, and feigned sleep, watching from the corner of my eye to see what she would do.

Seeing me lie down, the willow spirit crept near the table and snatched the gourd, playing with it. She poured the pills into her palm and swallowed them. No sooner had she done so than she stood frozen in place. After a moment, she leaned over the table and called out to wake me:

"Master, master! Long ago, beneath the locust tree, I served as your attendant. When all other plans failed, I hanged myself from a willow, becoming the tree’s spirit and turning demonic here. Little did I know my master would arrive—repeatedly, I have offended you. I beg your forgiveness and ask that you take me home."

The master’s virtue was vast indeed." With these words, she bowed deeply, weeping bitterly. Moved, I too felt a pang of sorrow, my heart heavy with grief. Yet I rejoiced that she had recognized her true nature and not remained forever on the demonic path, fulfilling my hopes. I recounted the full story to her, and through the night, we poured out our hearts to one another. By then, dawn had broken.

We stood beneath the weeping willow. The attendant said, "Since parting from you, master, I have relied on these demonic ways. Now, saved by your grace, I am a thousand times fortunate. This tree cannot remain—lest it bring harm again. Please step back, master, and I will be gone from here." With that, she grasped the tree and uprooted it with all her might, roots and all—truly a feat of divine strength. She then gathered the bones of all her past victims, buried them in a pit, and prayed to heaven in repentance. Afterward, she followed me on our journey.

As we neared the green locust tree, we saw Abbot Zhong seated beneath it. From afar, the attendant pointed and said, "That treacherous Daoist is the one who deceived you years ago, master, leading to my hanging and years of wandering. Now, by fortune, master and servant are reunited—like a withered tree blooming anew, a dried fish returned to water—yet here he comes again. Let me drag him down and cast him into a deep ravine, shattering his bones to dust. Only then will this day’s resentment be eased." She wiped her hands, gnashed her teeth, and glared, ready to charge. I shouted to stop her.

Approaching, I paid my respects to the abbot. The attendant’s anger still simmered. The abbot raised his left hand in the *mudra* of wish-fulfillment, and she immediately bowed. Turning to me, he said, "This attendant’s karmic debts are too deep, and her merits too few, to bring her back to the temple just yet. Your cultivation is nearly complete—what you lack is good affinity. I give you these elixir pills, several talismans, and a demon-slaying sword. Travel first to Handan, settle where you may, and wherever you find peril, aid those in need. Rescue the suffering, guide them toward goodness. When your merits are full, I will seek you out, and only then may you return with her."

He called the attendant and instructed, "You lost your root and fell into evil, causing much harm and accumulating deep karmic debt. Without your master’s salvation, you would have remained a monstrous thing, forever sunk in darkness. Now, your spirit is not yet dimmed, and your true nature awakened—this is your fortune. Since you took form from a willow, let ‘Willow’ be your surname. You shall be called Willow Attendant. Follow your master, bearing this sword. From now on, reject demons, take refuge in the true Dharma, cast aside the wolf’s cruelty, and nurture a heart of compassion. Turn the impulse to kill into the virtue of cherishing life, replace ruthlessness with mercy. Extend your love from the great to the small, from the desire to harm none to the resolve to harm nothing. When the human heart stirs, let the Dao-heart quietly endure. If wicked desires arise, suppress them at once. Thus may you atone for past wrongs, renew your karma, and truly become your master’s follower."

With that, he vanished in a gust of pure wind. Willow Attendant bowed again toward the sky. From then on, her former thoughts were gone, her mind utterly empty. She followed me toward Handan.

As we rested by the roadside, a scholar approached with a graceful air. He glanced at me and Willow Attendant with a faint smile and asked our purpose. I replied that I sold medicine. He inquired about the elixir of immortality, though in jest. I answered, "Is there no elixir of immortality? The ceaseless reverence of the august heavens, the unceasing purity of the sky—King Wen’s harmony with heaven, the sage’s unity with it. To be one with heaven is to be without end; without end is to be without beginning or end. Thus, is it not true that the elixir of immortality exists?" He then asked, "Are the teachings of heaven and hell real or not?"
