I answered, "Heaven, earth, and humanity form the three powers of principle. The manifest and the hidden are the divisions of these three powers. The manifest has its overseers, while the hidden has its spirits and gods—this is an immutable principle. When one’s virtue is good, the mind’s essence is bright and clear, radiant and upright, aligning with yang in its power. When one’s nature is evil, it becomes dark and crooked, murky and obscured, aligning with yin in its power. Yang follows its kind to enter heaven, while yin follows its kind to enter earth. Things gather by kind, and beings separate by group—this is an immutable tendency. How could such distinctions exist only in the unfathomable and obscure?

For instance, when a person suffers illness in sleep, this is clear proof of experiencing hell. If the heart and intestines fester and rot, this is the hell of carving and flaying. If the throat and tongue ulcerate and decay, this is the hell of plowing and harrowing. If scorching flames and billowing smoke afflict, this is the hell of fiery pits. If boiling water and searing oil torment, this is the hell of cauldrons. The sores on the crown of the head are like the hidden placement of fire basins. The abscesses on the waist are like the dark suspension of rolling stones. When the whole body is wracked with pus and blood, the pain unbearable—what is this if not the hell of knife mountains? When the entire frame is seized by cramps, the agony unendurable—what is this if not the hell of hanging poles? When the body feels pressed by a thousand *jun* and cannot turn, this is the hell of grinding wheels. When every bone feels broken, the pain piercing flesh and sinew, this is the hell of pounding and beating. When sores and scabs cover the body, leaving no patch of unbroken skin, this is the hell of flaying. When stabbing pain racks the limbs as if they were being torn apart, this is the hell of sawing. When one smells fragrant food but cannot swallow it, is this not the hell of hungry ghosts? When one knows what one desires but cannot see it, is this not the hell of darkness? When blood is vomited by the basinful and discharged by the barrelful, is this not the hell of the blood pool? When pain strikes like snakebites in the east and thorn pricks in the west, is this not the hell of the village of evil dogs? When one shivers with alternating heat and cold, when thick furs bring no warmth and boiling water no relief, this is the suffering of the hell of ice mountains. When one cannot eat a full meal or wear proper clothes, lying ill for months without seeing the sun or sky, when fetters and shackles bind or cudgels and rods strike, this is entry into the Avīci hell. These are the manifest signs of the hidden in the world above and below—why doubt, sir?

As for those who ride in grand carriages with outriders shouting before and behind, who wear gold and purple and feast on rich delicacies, whose desires are fulfilled without seeking and whose wills are satisfied without striving, who roam in realms of carefree ease and delight in lands of joy—these dwell in heaven. Once, my master gave me a pillow, and by resting on it, one can awaken to the future and witness all things between heaven and earth. Would you like to try it?"

The scholar fell silent for a long while, then asked my surname. I replied with [redacted], and in turn, I asked his. He answered, "Lu." I gave him the pillow. He took it and lay down on my straw mat. Before the hour was out, he awoke from his dream and told me what he had seen, which matched exactly what I had said. Sighing deeply, he said, "Wealth and rank are but passing clouds before the eyes; fame and achievement are but inns on the road of life. Life is a sojourn, death a return—all is but a dream in a single sleep. May I now abandon my home and follow you as your disciple?"

I replied that though Lu had good intentions, his resolve was not yet firm. I did not accept him as a disciple but instead taught him the art of the Ninefold Returning Elixir, a method to prolong life and preserve destiny. We then parted ways, and this became the tale of *The Handan Dream*.

Leaving that place, I traveled to the cities of You, Yan, Liao, and Ji, reviving the weary and ailing from their sickbeds and relieving the distressed and destitute from their hardships. When I reached the Si River, I ascended the platform of Confucius, changed my attire to that of a village tutor, and sought lodging by carriage. There was a family of the Ji clan, a great household devoted to the study of life and destiny. All three of their sons studied the *Book of Changes*. Upon meeting me, I discussed with them the principles of the prior and posterior heavens. Ji Si was delighted and invited me to instruct his sons.

The eldest, Tong, the second, Da, and the youngest, Shu, were all earnest and sincere scholars. They treated me with the rites of disciples. At that time, those who followed me included Shen Yuan, Kong Da, Zhou Yijing, Bai Liang, Gongsun Yu, Chen Kun, Wang Zixian, Wang Zizong, Meng Ji, Kong Yinzhe, Kong Cheng, and Kong Ji—comrades of shared purpose and lofty aspiration, who stood out in their understanding of the Way, much like the disciples of the ancient Master Xuan Ni.

Only Lu Shaoji and Wang Zongsheng, though learned in letters, behaved crudely and at times showed disrespect. One day, I recited to the students the passage: *'Even if one possesses the talent and beauty of the Duke of Zhou, if he is arrogant and stingy, the rest is not worth observing.'* Then I expounded its meaning:

'The Duke of Zhou was endowed by heaven with extraordinary gifts, and through his diligence, he perfected the virtues of the Three Kings and embodied the four excellences. He governed the state and assisted in its administration, dispelling slander to restore the house of Ji, wielding the axe to suppress treacherous hearts. He was guided by the loftiest of virtues and expanded them with inexhaustible wisdom—his talent was truly beautiful. Yet he humbled himself before scholars, extended his concern to others, received the words of herdsmen with open mouth, and listened to the strategies of artisans with hair undone. Never complacent, he eagerly sought goodness, desiring to benefit all under heaven and bring them to the realm of brilliance and acuity. Only one with talent as beautiful as the Duke of Zhou would suffice. This is why the Duke of Zhou’s talent was beautiful, and why his beauty was worthy of praise.

If, however, he had flaunted his beauty, cherished his talent and feared others possessing it, prided himself on his excellence and feared others sharing it—then his talent would have been but a trivial matter, his beauty but an empty pursuit.'"

This is but a trifling matter, not worthy of esteem. How could one still cling to it, having nothing to observe? Even one as talented and virtuous as the Duke of Zhou must not be arrogant or stingy—how much less those beneath him, or those with but a single talent or skill? If the Duke himself, in his arrogance and stinginess, would render his talent unworthy of regard, how much more so those beneath him in arrogance and stinginess—what would there be to observe?

Extending this principle further: he who rules all under heaven, if arrogant and stingy, will lose his realm; he who governs a state, if arrogant and stingy, will lose his state; he who heads a household, if arrogant and stingy, will lose his household; he who possesses a self, if arrogant and stingy, will lose his self. How grave indeed is the harm of arrogance and stinginess!

You, my students, study to become sages and worthies, aspiring to assist the Son of Heaven above and the state below, and to establish yourselves and glorify your names to honor your parents. How could you tread this path of error? Where correction is due, correct; where effort is needed, strive. I share this with you all."

The two disciples, hearing this admonition, blushed with shame, sweat streaming down their backs. They bowed their heads and received the teaching. Before a season had passed, their reservations melted away like ice, and they embraced humility and modesty all the more.

On another evening, he expounded to them: *"When the crane calls in the shade, its young respond in harmony. If there were a fine office, I would share it with you."* All things between heaven and earth—those of the same sound resonate, those of the same qi seek one another, like kinds gather and unlike kinds separate, not even a hair’s breadth apart. Thus, feet tread the same path, mouths share the same tastes, ears delight in the same sounds, eyes behold the same sights. All of like kind are alike—why should the heart alone be doubted?

What hearts share in common is virtue. Virtue is heaven’s true nobility, the heart’s fine office. When hearts and virtue align in this, heavenly office is complete, and human desires and aspirations converge. All who possess the innate goodness of constant norms will arise with the resolve to respond and follow, unbound by borders or boundaries, unrestricted by youth or age, lowliness or eminence. Those who delight in it share the same response; those who admire it harbor no divergent thoughts.

Without this, heaven and earth’s processes of generation, growth, harvest, and storage would be nothing but virtue; our own lives and destinies are likewise rooted in this virtue. Preserve this virtue wholly, unstained by even a speck of dust, all thoughts clarified. Unmuddied by the tumult of distracting desires, immersed in the profound tranquility of heaven’s depths—then what heaven and earth possess, you possess; what heaven and earth govern, you govern. Thus, you penetrate the heavenly mechanism, pierce the earth’s axis, and this resonance elicits that response: the crane calls, and its young answer in harmony.

You may thus take your place between heaven and earth, preceding heaven while the world follows, following heaven while adhering to its timing—yet all the while, you know not that heaven and earth are for me, but that I am for heaven and earth.

After residing there for a year, whenever I expounded the teachings, I illuminated profound principles with clear analogies, guiding the disciples step by step into the mysteries. Zhou was the first to awaken. Fearing my traces might be exposed, on the first day of the month, at dawn, I departed without a word.

Arriving at Huai, I encountered a great outbreak of pestilence. I halted at a thatched hermitage, distributing talismanic water; all who drank were completely cured. For those of ample means, I offered a talisman for a single coin; from the poor, I accepted nothing. Thus, I preserved the lives of nearly ten thousand in the prefecture, and amassed over ten million coins, which I stored in a treasury. After the epidemic, when famine struck and corpses lined the roads, I distributed the coins in relief, saving hundreds more. For I had foreseen this and accepted payment for the talismans in advance. Once the funds were exhausted, I departed.

Proceeding to the Three Wu regions of Yangzhou, I visited the Terrace of Xishi and the Stone Chamber. The land of Wu revered spirits, trusted talismans, and favored shamanic practices, yet its customs were frivolous and decadent, its mores profoundly shallow. There, I transformed into the Mad Daoist, while Willow Wanderer became my young attendant. We sang as we walked along the roads:

*I recall descending the mountain, riding the pure wind,
Head crowned with white clouds, feet treading dragons.
Drinking from stone springs, feasting on pine—
Singing a hymn of the Dao, chanting a ha-ha-li-yao-hong, ha-ha-li-yao-hong.*

Willow Wanderer harmonized with his own song:

*Following my master, transformed into a gust of wind,
Clad in straw, staffed by a dragon.
My companion is the bright moon, my friend the Red Pine—
Singing a hymn of the Dao, chanting a ha-ha-li-yao-hong, ha-ha-li-yao-hong.*

We sang wildly along the way, stirring the entire town. Young and old, men and women, thronged the streets to watch. Seeing the crowd, I composed myself with solemn dignity and called out, *"This poor Daoist seeks a most generous benefactor, a venerable elder of karmic affinity!"* After each proclamation, I would burst into mad song, yet by day’s end, none appeared. The next day, the same. On the third day, an elder of sixty, his hair half-black, half-white, bearing a robust and imposing presence, stood firm, fixed his gaze, watched for a while, then slowly inquired, *"What does this Daoist seek from such a benefactor?"* Feigning ignorance, I continued my cries. The elder seized my arm.

With a swift hook of his foot, Liu Xing sent the elder tumbling head over heels, rolling twice before he sprawled on the ground. The crowd erupted in laughter. The elder scrambled up at once, turned, and hurried home, barking orders to four or five sturdy servants to seize us both and haul us away. The onlookers gasped, certain we were doomed. This elder was none other than the local despot of Gusu—Yuan Bojī, the Winged Tiger—his wealth beyond measure, his retinue of servants and attendants numbering in the hundreds, his concubines and maids uncounted. Those who crossed him were beaten to death with his staff, and none in the prefecture dared challenge his arrogance.

Dragged into the central hall, Bojī gnashed his teeth in fury. *"You wild Daoists—how dare you be so insolent! I am the foremost magnate of this prefecture; who does not defer to me? I inquired after your purpose in good faith—why then did you, like thieves and rogues, mock me before the crowd, making a spectacle of my headwrap and inviting public ridicule?"* He roared at his servants, *"First, give them thirty strokes of the great cudgel! Then send them to the authorities to have their crimes properly punished!"*

The servants moved to strike Liu Xing first. Before the rod could touch him, Liu transformed, revealing his true form. The servants shrieked, their souls scattering in terror, and fled in panic, clutching their heads. Bojī collapsed to the ground in shock, lying motionless for some time before he revived. When he rose and saw only our familiar faces, he asked in bewilderment, *"You Daoists are most strange. Where did that monstrous apparition come from just now?"*

I smiled and replied, *"Elder, your eyes fail to discern good from ill, to distinguish virtue from malice. Here stand only my master and disciple—where do you see any monster? Have you not heard the *Scripture of the Supreme*? ‘When a man harbors good intentions, though he has not yet acted, auspicious spirits already attend him. When a man harbors evil intentions, though he has not yet acted, malevolent spirits already follow him.’ My disciple and I are but humble men of the Dao, begging alms in the streets, never having trespassed against you. Yet you, elder, brought this humiliation upon yourself—your stumble on the road was but an accident, yet you sought to harm my master and me. Such was your evil intent, and so the evil spirits took shape, visible to you though unseen by us. How many years remain to you in this life? Does time linger for any man?"*

Is spirit like flowing water? Is power like the Eastern Ocean? There is trust and betrayal, success and failure. Take, for example, a vessel—those sturdy endure longer, while the frail break early. Elder, your wealth and status, your arrogance toward the world and disdain for others, all stem from roots planted in past lives. If in this life you cultivate good deeds, preserving the integrity of your soul, you may be reborn in another fine place, much like this one. But if you lose your way, acting recklessly by your own nature, your spirit-light will scatter and flee—how then could you retain such power? To know the causes of past lives, observe the blessings of this one; to foresee the fate of lives to come, examine your actions now.

While this breath remains, you have gold and silver, fine food and clothing, wife and children, and mighty influence. But when this three-part breath is gone, gold and silver are emptiness, food and clothing are emptiness, wife and children are emptiness, and power is emptiness. Reflect, ponder—it is not too late to turn back. Strike a blow, kick away desire, and only then will you awaken. Recall how, moments ago, you saw a phantom and collapsed in terror—had you not revived, who knows what would have become of you? How then could you have spoken with this poor Daoist?

Your servants and retainers, long nourished by your generosity, your maids and concubines, your wife and children, all cherished by your care—when this crisis struck, each fled for their own lives, hiding or running away. Not one remained at your side. Yet I, this poor Daoist, though we have just met and you never granted me the slightest kindness—instead seeking to take my master’s and my life—chose to stay and tend to you rather than flee. Elder, let this moment give you pause for deep reflection.

In my hermitage, I composed a song to pass the time, and now I bid this boy sing it for you to hear.

The boy, obeying his master’s command, sang:

*(Song lyrics to follow)*

Life drifts like duckweed or willow floss—rootless, unmoored.
Why cling so desperately, refusing to turn back?
Gold and silver piled higher than mountains,
Crimson cheeks and painted brows in endless revelry.
Spirit drained, body spent in ceaseless passion,
Dawn’s joy, dusk’s delight—never a moment’s rest.
A thousand delicacies fill your mouth,
Silken robes and embroidered gowns adorn your frame.
In and out, you ride fine carriages and four-horse teams,
Ever drawn to the pleasures of flowers and willows.

Then consider:
All you possessed in this world—how swiftly it all came to nothing.
Wealth like hills could not be guarded,
Fair faces and tender beauty were cast aside.
Feast and finery turned to emptiness,
Jade bridles and golden saddles could not be mounted again.
Now you lie beneath a mound of green grass,
Foxes and pines your only companions.
Even if you had worthy sons and filial grandsons,
Could they bear your sorrows in your stead?

The boy finished singing and burst into laughter: *"Foolish man! Seeing only what lies ahead, never what comes after. Who lies beneath the northern ridge? Who rests in the eastern fields? That grassy mound once held a hero; that lofty knoll once cradled a man of wealth. The white-haired elder on the high mountain? A man who spurned fame and fortune. The rosy-cheeked scholar in Penglai? One who never chased flowers or willows. Foolish man! Foolish man! You sought to harm us—why then did you see spirits and ghosts?"*

He spoke on, laughing again. The elder sat motionless as a clay statue, struck dumb by the words. After a long silence, he bowed deeply before me and sighed: *"Alas, this old body never recognized true worth. Master, every word you speak is truth, every line rings with reason. But I am old, my days waning like the setting sun, my essence depleted by the past—how can I cultivate now to complete what I once began? I beg you, take the high seat. I wish to become your disciple and receive your teachings."*

I did not refuse his bow and immediately imparted to him the secret of *"turning the light inward to return to the root."* After ten days, he had already entered the first stage of the Way. I entrusted him with the task of seeking herbs and departed. Not only had I transformed his stubborn nature, but I had guided him into the mysteries of the profound. This was the first great achievement of the boy, Liu Xing.

Along the way, I dispensed medicine: in a single day, I cured ten cases of carbuncles, five of parasitic afflictions, and eighteen of consumptive wasting. I initiated one monk and three Daoists. Then, taking Liu Xing with me, I traveled to Jiangyou, entering Yushan, where no human traces remained. We sat beneath a poplar tree, and Liu Xing said: *"Master, this is my tree. I detest it—we cannot stay here."* So we moved to a catalpa tree.

After a moment, the poplar tree rumbled like thunder, then creaked like a turning cartwheel, then crowed like a rooster. Liu Xing exclaimed: *"Strange! Strange indeed!"* In an instant, he drew his sword, intent on hunting the strange presence, following the sound as it wound through the mountains. I rose to follow, but before I had taken a hundred steps, another Liu Xing appeared, saying: *"Master, where are you going? The boy who follows you is your disciple—what is this thing?"* I was startled, unable to tell them apart.

The first Liu Xing, who had urged me to leave, glared and said: *"This is my master, Master Lü. What kind of spirit are you?"* Liu Xing replied: *"This is my master. I am Liu Xing, named by Ancestor Zhong."* The spirit said: *"I am a willow spirit. Years ago, I followed my master, Lü Yan, to the capital. I died as a willow tree and became a willow spirit. Where did this Liu Xing come from?"*

Liu Xing turned to me: *"Master, please sit again. Let me slay this spirit before we go."* Then he said to the spirit: *"If you are real, let us test our skills—what say you?"* The spirit agreed.

Both displayed their powers. Liu Xing let out a great shout—wild winds uprooted trees, sand and stones flew in chaos, battering the spirit. The spirit responded with a roar, summoning a black mist and countless jagged staves raining down from the sky. Liu Xing blocked and parried, sending the staves scattering to the ground. With a fierce cry—*"Begone!"*—beasts roared, and packs of jackals emerged. The spirit remained unruffled, countering with a shout that summoned lions and elephants, dragons thrashing in the waves.

They clashed, one wielding the might to topple mountains and shatter cliffs, the other unleashing the force to overturn seas and upend rivers. A thousand paths diverged, a thousand transformations unfolded. I watched for a long time and saw that the spirit’s power was no less than Liu Xing’s. With a flick of my whisk and a point of my sword, the spirit vanished without a trace. The poplar tree trembled, its roar like fury—only then did I realize it was the spirit of the poplar.

I handed my sword to Liu Xing and ordered him to fell the tree. With one mighty swing, he split it in two. Blood flowed into the mountain stream, and the spirit was vanquished.

We passed through Rao, circled Jiujian and Hongdu, and visited the mountains of Dragon and Tiger, seeking the company of the profound and true. Disguised as a Perfect Realized One, I expounded the mysteries of the One Aperture, but none understood. So I journeyed to Min. The customs of the southern barbarians were even more resistant to awakening. Thus, I sang in the marketplace, weaving profound principles into common speech, concealing true subtleties within vulgar words—hoping the world might find it easier to follow and perhaps be transformed.

*(Song to follow)*

Upon the lofty terrace, a mirror—pure and bright,
Discerning beauty from ugliness, dividing fair from foul.
In its wholeness and truth, it receives the empty and numinous,
Embracing all principles, responding to the spirits’ call.

Yet suddenly defiled by grime and dust,
Its reflection sullied, its truth obscured.
The clear turns turbid, the bright grows dim,
Heaven retreats, and man encroaches in.
The moon veiled by clouds, the water drowning duckweed,
Its nature ravaged, its order undone.

I urge you: scrape away the filth, scour clean the dust,
Restore this emptiness and numinosity, reclaim your original truth.
The One Aperture embraces all, never sinking into the depths—
Thus may you endure, no need for recasting.
Let your heart be like water, ever clear,
Like the moon, ever bright.
Through heaven and earth, across all time,
From bustling thoroughfares to winding alleys,
From crowded markets to desolate paths,
I passed through them all.

Listeners pressed shoulder to shoulder, garment to garment,
Old and young, men and women, all strained to hear,
Yet not one stirred in heart to inquire.
Thus I sang for ten days, but my art found no purchase.

Then at the crossroads, I set up an incense table,
Wrote talismans and practiced healing arts to cure the sick.
Those who sought the talismans found divine response,
And so some came to my door.
They arrived in succession, numbering over fifteen hundred.
Gradually awakening, their numbers swelled day by day.

Harassed by the authorities as a follower of Zhang Jiao’s ilk, the two of us were seized and imprisoned for a full hundred days—such was fate, so I did not flee. When the term expired, both Liu Xing and I emerged from prison and fled to the eastern and western Guang provinces, traversing even the most barren lands. Through wind and rain, enduring the ravages of cold and scorching heat, we tasted every hardship to its dregs, suffering toils and exhaustion beyond what others could bear.

By my reckoning, those who entered the Mystic Gate numbered in the millions, and those I saved and aided totaled fifteen million seven hundred thousand and more.

At that time, I passed through Huoshui Village once again. Within, I had already attained true enlightenment, and the hermitage remained as before. I dwelled beside my ancestral graves, awaiting communion with the divine. After more than ten days, I beheld my parents both residing in the Land of Ultimate Bliss, dwelling in the Pure Land as lords of that radiant realm.

Yet perils were beyond counting. As I prepared to return to the hermitage, my master arrived and declared: “Your merit and virtue are now complete, vast and boundless—heavenly records have been made of them. Yet the vital essences of mountains and rivers must not go ungathered. You must wander further, surveying the finest landscapes, seeking the Dao and the Mystic, to refine the essence and strengthen your foundation. I shall come to guide you at Zhongnan; there is no need to return to the old hermitage.” Turning to Liu Xing, he added, “You must follow with utmost care, for your merit is no small matter.” We both received his decree. Then, in an instant, my master ascended upon the clouds and departed.

Liu Xing and I first journeyed to the Iron Gate Pass, then sailed a boat upon Dongting Lake. We paid homage at the Shrine of the Xiang Consorts and left a poem in mourning:

Nine banners and imperial regalia rest upon Jun Mountain,
The Emperor’s daughters, full of feeling, linger here.
Who knows how much blood and tears have flowed?
Ten thousand pearls of jade are all stained with their grief.

We turned toward the Mute Spring to pay respects at the Temple of Ma Yuan, where I inscribed another poem:

A blazing heroic spirit, a minister unmatched in his age,
He seized the first victory over the southern tribes.
A loyal soul, through a thousand ages, retains its vital qi,
Even in death, his martial might aided Kongming.

Next, we came to Mount Wudang, where I sought the secrets of the Tortoise and Snake, Water and Fire. That very night, I faced the North Star and discoursed on the principles of the Great Monad, obtaining the sublime and wondrous teachings of the highest order. Then I composed a regulated verse:

Within the cavity, a perfect pearl nestles close to the jade hairpin,
Jade nectar soaks the golden robe upon the crown.
The spring breeze stirs not the bamboo before the window,
Yet night rain nourishes the peaches beyond the sea.

Tigers roar and dragons chant as lead and mercury unite,
Snakes coil and tortoises breathe where Kan and Li intersect.
The vow fulfilled, the Three Lights shine undimmed,
Illuminating the spirit’s rhinoceros horn to towering heights.

Again it is said:
Before the cliff, an iron pestle was ground,
Success fears not the labor’s weight.
Through three thousand six hundred kalpas, the practice complete,
Forged is the demon-subduing Tai’e sword.

Turning toward Plum Mountain, I composed a verse on Hanging Arrow Peak:
Deceiving Mei, the jade sap first flowed,
Startled by barbarian riders in the thinning mist.
Had not the peak still borne its arrow,
Who would recall the general’s might of years gone by?

Gazing toward Wuling Stream, I reached the summit of Mount Wuyi.
For ten days, I surveyed the scenery,
Awaiting the arrival of Daoist friends,
Hoping to engage them in discourse.

I composed an ancient-style poem:
South of the Jian River, the land teems with spirit,
Lush and verdant with pine and bamboo.
Ages pass, unknowing of Yao’s reign,
Yet in the still night, the flute of Prince Wang is heard.

Peach blossoms drift upon the winding waters,
Waves surge through stone ravines, casting cold jade.
Does the azure phoenix cry as a common bird?
What wild beast pursues the dark deer?

I watch the lads plucking magic mushrooms,
Clad in lotus and water-chestnut robes, swaying in the breeze.
From what era hails the crimson-cheeked elder?
He speaks of life since Pangu’s time.

The mountain gate knows neither cold nor attachment,
Peaches red, roses purple.
I would take white stones and green essence,
Casually kindling dragon bamboo to boil them.

Mount Wuyi, fair and lofty,
Where one may grasp the profound and escape life and death.
Here, the days and months remain ever thus,
A game of chess consumes the bright daylight.

Ascending Sleeping Dragon Ridge, I surveyed its summit’s sights,
Beholding relics that stirred deep feeling—truly a hero’s mark.

I composed a poem:
Often did Liangfu chant, embracing his knees,
The hidden dragon stirs, leaping to meet the dragon.
His strategies surpassed the scholars of his age,
Even now, the Eight Gates of Dunjia evoke admiration.

Again at Dragon Tiger Mountain, I sought the remnants of the Daoist legacy,
Staying ten days, I left a poem:
Dragon Tiger Mountain divides in dragon and tiger form,
The nine provinces’ map finds truth here.
You, sir, are no mere mortal—meeting a hero,
Revealing heaven and earth nurturing the land’s spirit.

Red talismans guard the thousand-year records of the forbidden court,
A white crane rests amid the pines like a wisp of cloud.
I ask: where has the master gone?
In the azure mist’s embrace, seeking ginseng and poria.

From Tiantai, I stepped across the Stone Beam,
Seeking the abode of arhats,
Tracing the old veins of Peach Blossom Spring,
Straight to the summit, deep into perilous passes,
I sat cross-legged at the root of an ancient cypress.

The night was still, the moon bright,
All sounds hushed.
Then, faintly, the murmur of flutes and pipes arose,
Music blending in harmonious chorus.

From a crevice in the ridge emerged dozens of youths,
Clad in lotus robes, barefoot and tousled,
Marching in ranks, vanishing southward.

Again, clear tones of sheng and se lingered,
A group of white-haired and rosy-cheeked elders passed before me.
I bowed, but they vanished like a dream.

Then, joyous voices and laughter resounded,
Dozens of celestial maidens faded into the eastern mist.

In a single night, a thousand visions unfolded.
I composed a five-character ancient-style poem:
Tiantai’s peaks are most wondrous,
Steep cliffs, sheer as carved jade.
A thousand summits cluster like feathers of green,
A single stream encircles jade’s luster.

The Stone Beam Bridge arches across,
Like a dragon spanning the nine heavens.
Waves still as a dragon’s sword scales,
Pines steadfast as cranes returning to their nests.

I hear the women who fled Qin
Do not become the rain of King Xiang.
Moonlight graces their cloud-like skin,
Rosy pendants rise with the wind.

Old monks gather ferns for sustenance,
Entering samadhi upon the celestial altar.
Peaches follow warm ravine streams,
A hundred locks chill the ancient crags.

Stone gates close from time to time,
Years and months unrecorded.
I would knock upon the mystery within mystery,
Yet refine the qi within qi.

Suddenly, from the empty valley,
Music resounds like the wind of heaven.
Ten thousand ravines devoid of human trace,
Flowers bloom red through all four seasons.

I boarded a boat for the Southern Sea to pay homage to the Great Being.
Ascending Mount Luojia, gazing upon the tides,
I awakened to the wondrous, perfect, and luminous Dao,
Delved into the Mahayana’s great Dharma,
Stood firm to behold the Tathagata,
My wisdom eye meeting the secret of Dharma.

I composed a gatha:
Bright and clear, the moon at the sea’s heart,
Perfect and harmonious, lacking nothing.
One day, lifted to the center of the sky,
Its pure radiance illumines the darkest night.

Hark!
Shattering the relic within,
Why rest when one may wander freely?

I composed another poem:
The mountain floats like a wave-crested eggplant,
My southern journey to India halts here, mooring my raft.
At dawn, I dine on jade dew, dragons offering the alms bowl,
By night, golden lotuses boil, deer adding blossoms.

The meditation cell, pure and seething, seals the clouded cliffs,
Pearl and angelica gleam, vying with the moon’s splendor.
Behold the eastern lands beyond the seas,
Where sky and ocean blur, free of perching crows.

I traveled to the Southern Seas, reaching Feilai Peak in Lin’an,
Meditating in the Call-to-Apes Cave, unseen by any.
I said to Liu Xing: “Marvelous is this peak!
Peaks pierce the heavens, sheer cliffs hang inverted,
The cave wrought by heaven and earth—
Such is the land’s beauty!”

I composed a poem:
A wing of the sacred vulture unfurls,
Exquisite as if self-soaring.
Within the cave, a monk enters samadhi,
The mountain still, a crane comes to roost.

When the meal is ready, apes are called to eat,
As scriptures are chanted, birds awaken to understanding.
I urge you to stay long,
Never again to take flight.

There was a monk named Huiyi, who knew of my arrival,
Most kind, he exchanged secrets with me.
Thus, he abandoned Chan to return,
From stillness perceiving nature,
From emptiness recognizing being.

He led me to observe Dongxiao.
Together, we composed and responded in verse.

I composed a poem:
Step by step I enter the sacred peaks,
With each stride ascending higher.
Nine summits encircle emerald cliffs,
A single path winds through azure skies.
Flying squirrels like gray cranes,
Mountain sweetleaf blooms like peach blossoms.
The cave’s gate stands ever open,
Inviting one to follow the path of Youchao.

The monk responded in verse:
Nine emerald screens rise in layered arches,
Green peaks tower in myriad folds.
The lofty summit brushes the sun,
Two caves stretch dimly into the heavens.
A lone village’s fire flickers in the distance,
Ancient trees bear springtime peaches.
The Chan mechanism rests in solitude,
No need to learn the ways of climbing nests.

From Dongxiao to Tianmu, we linked verses.
I said:
Heavenly pillars stand east and west,
Divine eyes hang left and right.

(The monk:)
Mists and clouds fly beneath our feet,
(I:)
Stars and constellations array before my chest.

(The monk:)
All the young return to stillness,
Every emptiness enters Chan.
(I:)
Above, golden bells resound,
(The monk:)
In the monastery, jeweled lamps burn bright.

Strange rocks crouch like tigers,
Creeping fig carpets the earth like coins.
(I:)
Waterfalls pierce the peaks like needles,
Rainbow-hued cliffs join like woven silk.
(The monk:)
When the ephemeral flower blooms, pluck it,
Snail-script characters form a bound volume.
(I:)
Moths return, orioles remain undisturbed,
Spring dew adorns the lush grass.
(The monk:)
Sheer cliffs soar into the azure,
(I:)
Jagged peaks meet the blue heavens.
Our laughter and discourse startle the unenlightened,
(The monk:)
Yet this world’s fate remains boundless.

We completed ten linked couplets; the rest of the poems are not recorded, for they cannot all be recounted.

After lingering for over a month, one evening the monk said to me: “Gathering is easy, parting is hard. I am fortunate to have encountered a crane-like companion—how could I bear to separate? Yet Nirvana calls me first; I must depart to prepare for our next meeting. Tomorrow at noon, I ask that you point the way for my return.”

Thus passed over a month of friendship. At noon the next day, the monk bathed, sat upright, and passed away. I prepared a coffin and placed it on the mountain, gathering the assembly below. As the flames rose, I held the torch and recited a gatha:

Do not wander in straw sandals,
Cherish your cloud-woven robe for self-cultivation.
On Mount Sumeru, the scenery is fine—
Look back, and the ephemeral flower silently unfurls its fragrance.

Cease your restless wandering, abandon haste,
From this day shed your foul skin-bag.
Leave true traces across the great thousand worlds,
In the Land of Ultimate Bliss, sun and moon endure long.

Ah!
Leap from the river of desire—spring days are fair,
On the Anavatapta Lake, drink the purest ghee.

In that moment, amid the flames, his true form appeared, laughing as it ascended straight westward. The monks gathered the remaining ashes, and I then took my leave.

I sailed to Jinshan, gazing across the vast ocean, and stayed at the mountain temple for over ten days. I composed a poem:

Boundless are the azure waves, vast and deep,
The mountain within seems a green conch shell.
Monks return, washing their bowls as dragons devour rice,
Guests sit watching the tide, dining on alligator soup.
A perilous peak looms beneath the moonlit tower,
Scriptures recited from the Dharma seat drown in surging waves.
I rinse my dust-laden sleeves in the clear current,
Delighting in the celestial wind’s harmonious dance.

Following the current, I pursued my journey, savoring the scenery along the way. I inquired after the wonders of Jiuhua and traced the relics of the Six Dynasties, then lingered at Jiuhua. The weather was clear and mild, the mountain’s beauty radiant. I composed a quatrain:

The sky clears, revealing Jiuhua’s splendor,
Mountains like layered screens draped in green gauze.
Once the path to bodhi was pointed out,
The way to Pāramitā lies not far ahead.

At the mountain’s foot lived a Daoist named Jin Shouyi, who had built a hut for alchemy. Upon meeting me, he inquired about the arts of transmutation. I replied: “The pursuit of elixirs is but a delusion for the greedy. Have you not observed the fox? It deludes itself with sunlight and moonlight, stealing human souls and spirits, then transforming—now a man, now a woman—to bewitch others, none the wiser. Yet when it encounters a master of the Dao, its true form is revealed, and the fox cannot hide. Thus, one knows that one’s original nature cannot be feigned. If human effort could alter it, heaven would not produce treasures, nor earth nurture rare jewels.

An ancient saying goes:
‘Truth and falsehood are hard to confuse,
In the end, one must return to one’s original form.
The greedy may scheme with all their cunning,
Only to trap themselves in cold poverty.’
Now heed my words!”

“He chanted:
‘I urge you, cease your firing of lead and mercury,
For time and years are but a vain burden.
The yellow sprout may never take root,
And what use is the white snow?
Companion to paper ash—
As if you’ve dreamed the butterfly’s dream.
Companion to paper ash—
As if you’ve dreamed the butterfly’s dream!’

He continued:
‘Three-sided crucibles and tripods, fires fed in vain,
Foolishly clinging to civil and martial arts.
Seeking rebirth to nurture the sands,
Drawing qi yet never leaving the mother.
All day long refining celestial sulfur—
Have you not borne enough toil?
All day long refining celestial sulfur—
Have you not borne enough toil?’

He added:
‘Seeking the elixir through dead arsenic, lead, and mercury,
Boasting of a method to reach the heavens.
The five metals yield no favor,
The eight minerals never truly unite.
Entering the bright furnace,
Passing through the lead pool—
All is but riding the raft upon the waves.
Entering the bright furnace,
Passing through the lead pool—
All is but riding the raft upon the waves.’

He declared further:
‘People of the world speak of firing the elixir,
Claiming one must first perfect the inner.
Yet who knows the inner, once achieved, bears no burden?
The myriad mirrors need not trouble the heart,
Nor the wooden raft be prized.
Even if you turn stone to gold,
In the end, it crumbles to brick.
Even if you turn stone to gold,
In the end, it crumbles to brick.’

Jin Shouyi, upon hearing my words, immediately smashed his elixir furnace and alchemical vessels to pieces. He sought the secret of a single aperture, tireless in his pursuit. I, too, revealed the full truth without reservation, holding nothing back. Having guided him onto the path, I brushed my sleeves and departed like a swallow in flight.”

Perched atop the peaks of Five Clouds, I gazed toward the desert wastes. Long I stood in melancholy contemplation, and so composed the *"Rhapsody on Yearning for the March."*

The rhapsody reads:
Misty, misty—Yan Clouds stretch north to the Wolf’s Watch,
Where frontier geese cry and frontier steeds race.
Ascending the heights, my thoughts surge unbounded—
Ten thousand li of mist and glow veil distant sight.
Fine sands drift, swirling and rising,
Blanketing the earth in golden reed-grass.
Where, oh where, lie the passes and mountains?
Even without sorrow, my heart would break.

In the still night, beneath the bright moon, a Qiang flute plays,
Startling me from a soldier’s dream upon my frontier cot.
Just now, in a perfumed chamber, we spoke of parting—
Who could know I would not share my days with wife and child?

I recall leaving home in the bloom of youth;
Now frost has settled upon my temples.
At the gate, my kind mother strains her eyes in vain,
While my wife’s heart must be heavy with longing.
In her boudoir, silk sleeves grow damp, layer upon layer,
Stained by the blood-tears of one gone to war.
Look—upon the sands, bones pile mountain-high,
All the longing of those left behind.

At my waist, the bronze seal of command has yet to turn to gold,
Yet beneath the starry skies of the frontier, my soul is already spent.
Though my noble name may be inscribed on bamboo annals,
How can filial piety and virtue hallow a barren grave?
My gaze strains until my nose stings with sorrow—
For the bitter toil of those conscripted to the march.

Life and death have ever been inescapable;
Why do later generations weep in vain for those gone before?

---

Another poem on Mount Wutai:
Step upon step, tier upon tier,
Its towering form pierces the azure sky.
A cough or sigh would not reach the realm of Zhuanxu—
Might this be the dragon that brings the sweet rain?

Descending Mount Wutai, I set my raft adrift among the stars.
Tracing the Yellow River to its source, I met my teacher upon a boat.
He said, “Why not return?”
Taking my hand, he led me to wander the Three Bees, Fangzhang, and Yingzhou,
And sat with me atop Dongying Mountain.
Turning to me, he said, “In seeking you, I grew parched upon the road.
East of the Eastern Sea grows a peach—fetch it, that I may eat.”
Then, to the willows, he said, “Stay here.”
I accepted his command and went.

The eastern mountain rose ten thousand *ren* high, with no peach tree in sight.
Walking ever eastward, I came upon a tree a *li* in girth,
Its branches thick, a hundred spans around—yet no peaches.
Only on the easternmost bough, slender as a finger, hung a peach the size of a melon,
Dangling above the roiling torrents and towering waves.
Heedless of my life, I strained upward with all my strength,
Broke the branch, plucked the peach, and offered it to my teacher.
Delighted, he said, “How fine this peach! How could I bear to eat it alone?”
He divided it into three, and we shared it.

When we had finished, my teacher asked,
“When you broke the peach, did you see anything?”
I replied, “No.”
“Come.”

We then went together to see it.” Arriving at the peach tree, we looked into the current and saw a corpse afloat in the heart of the waves. Upon close examination, it was none other than my own form. My teacher pointed and said, “Is this not your flesh-and-blood body? Now that it has departed, you may face the Celestial Emperor.” That very day, we stood together before the Jade Emperor, who issued a decree naming me the Perfected One of Pure Yang. The date was recorded as the fourteenth day of the fourth month in the *gengshen* year of the Kaiyuan era of the Tang.

By imperial edict, Willow was made an immortal; Willow received the decree. After paying respects and taking leave of the Emperor, my teacher and I returned to our original abode in the Paradise of Lang. There, Masters Li and Zhang met with great joy. After some time, the Queen Mother of the West arrived, and later still, the Star of Jade Balance, Ma Gu, the Supreme Perfected of the Celestial Pole, Cloud Maiden, Moon Slave, and the immortals of Mount Shang, Yixian, and other realms gathered like clouds, all coming to offer their congratulations. When the celebrations concluded, they departed.

I dwelt in the Paradise of Lang, wishing to universally deliver all beings. My teacher assented. I took leave of my teacher and, accompanied by Willow, set forth on our travels. I was born as a scholar in Changli to propagate the Daoist arts and deliver Master Han; I became a Daoist in Wuyuan to manifest divine powers and deliver the Lady He. I assisted Master Zhong by concealing my traces and aided Master Zhang by transforming my body, thereby delivering the Imperial Uncle and the Divine Elder. As for the Three Drunken Revels of Yueyang, where I painted a yellow crane to repay the host—such illusions began in my youth. In Luoyang, I wandered often, tossing gold to tease the Bodhisattva, for in my youth I was not swayed by wayward intentions. I dispensed medicine in Lu, revealed my form in Han, ever practicing benevolence and spreading kindness. I traversed rivers, lakes, and seas without exception; I journeyed through the capital’s outskirts and distant frontiers without fail.

Then came the affairs following my transcendental molting. Once the Eight Perfected had assembled, my virtue and conduct surpassed all bounds, and the Emperor elevated my rank, bestowing the title *Hongren Puji Fuyou Dijun*, enthroning me in the Eastern Palace as Chief Minister of the Highest Order.

From that time onward, Heaven and Earth remained unshattered, and my divine transformations knew no end—how could mere brush and paper ever capture them in full? I do not delight in flattering the world to gratify the hearts of men, nor do I relish the tedium of verbose discourse. Yet the disciples pressed earnestly, and I reluctantly complied, gathering these rough outlines to recount for them.

Is it vulgar or refined? Commonplace or strange? Let the world’s judgments decide. What reckoning do I have? What reckoning indeed.

**Later Traditions**
**○ Divine Transformations and Marvels**
**◇ Selling Ink in Wuchang**
Lü Dongbin wandered to Wuchang, disguising himself as an ink merchant. Each inkstick, no longer than a finger’s width, bore a price of three thousand coins. None sold. The crowd jeered and mocked.

A strongman named Wang declared, “The ink is small, yet the price is steep—could there be some hidden purpose?” He offered three thousand coins for a single stick. Lü then drank heavily with the man until, drunk, he stumbled home and fell into a stupor.

At midnight, a knock came at the door. The guest had returned the money and departed. By dawn, the inkstick had transformed into a bar of refined gold, bearing the character *Lü*. The merchant was sought everywhere, yet nowhere to be found.

**◇ Selling Combs in Wuchang**
Lü Dongbin roamed to Tianxin Bridge in Wuchang, concealing his name and hawking worn wooden combs for a thousand coins each. Days passed with no sales.

Then an old beggar woman appeared—over eighty, frail and hunched, her hair white as snow. The Patriarch sighed, “People cling to what lies before their eyes. They see me selling shabby goods at a high price and suspect some deeper meaning, yet among thousands, none possess the insight to perceive it. How then can they be spoken to of the Dao?”

The Patriarch then took the comb and combed the old woman’s hair. With each stroke, her hair grew longer, turning black as it cascaded to the ground, while her withered form grew youthful. The onlookers, struck with awe, clamored to buy his combs. Lü Dongbin smiled and said, “They see yet do not recognize; they recognize yet do not see.” With that, he cast the comb into the river below. It transformed into a blue-green dragon and soared away.

**◇ Water Transformed into Wine**
Ma Shan, a native of the Eastern Capital, failed the imperial examinations in the early Xining era and turned to the study of the Dao. One day, while traveling along the Bian River with a Daoist named Hou, they encountered a feathered immortal—clad in a blue-green headscarf and plain robe, his bearing refined, his bones extraordinary, his eyes bright as limpid water, his face untouched by dust.

Ma invited him to share tea and a meal. Hou, by nature quick to anger, rebuked him. The immortal said, “I possess the art of immortality.” Hou pressed him for details. The immortal asked, “What arts do *you* know?” Hou replied, “I can summon spirits with flying talismans, transmute stone into gold, and return money or jade to their owners.” The immortal said, “What you do is not the true Way.” Hou demanded, “Then what can *you* do?” The immortal answered, “I can strengthen my *qi* and purify my spirit.” Hou asked, “What does it mean to strengthen one’s *qi*?” The immortal said, “Simply observe.” He exhaled toward a wineshop, extinguishing its candles from dozens of yards away. Then he exhaled upon Hou’s face, sending a gust like a gale that left him trembling and unable to stand. Both men rose and bowed, saying, “Master, you are no ordinary man. We humbly seek your instruction.”

The immortal said, “To study immortality, one must establish merit and conduct. Merit lies in diligence and arduous cultivation; conduct lies in aiding others and benefiting all things.” Hou said, “Throughout my life, I have healed people with medicine—is this not merit and conduct?” The immortal replied, “You take lives to save lives, killing one to give life to another. It would be better to cure illness with talismanic water alone.” As dawn broke, the immortal took his leave, saying, “I shall return to the banks of the Xiang River. Let us part with a drink beneath the willow’s shade.” He gave Hou a hundred taels of silver to buy wine. Finding no wine available, the immortal produced a bottle and instructed Hou to fetch a bottle of Bian River water. He dropped in a single pill, and at once it turned into fine wine. The three drank deeply and became thoroughly intoxicated. The immortal left behind a poem:

*Three mouths share one chamber,*
*By its side, waters run clear.*
*Born to wander the world,*
*This is both surname and name.*

After parting, the two pondered the poem and realized he was Dongbin.

◇ **Square Holes in Paper**
Zhao Yingdao of the Directorate for the Advancement of Literature suffered from scrofula and was near death. Weeping, he bid farewell to his family and friends, saying, “I am dying. I can part with all the treasures in my chambers, but my white-haired parents have no one to rely on—what is to be done?” Before he could finish speaking, a Daoist knocked at the door and said to Zhao, “Your illness is not difficult to cure.” He took two sheets of paper, pinched the center of each to form two square holes roughly two *chi* in size, and handed them to Zhao, saying, “At night, burn one sheet to extinguish the disease, then apply a paste of frankincense to the sores. Keep the other sheet for those who come after.” With that, the Daoist vanished. Zhao then understood that the two square holes formed the character *lü* (吕).

◇ **Square Holes in Stone**
Lou Daoming of Zitong was wealthy and skilled in the arts of *xuansu*. He kept ten young maidens, dismissing each as soon as she became pregnant and replacing her with a new one. He maintained no fewer than ten at all times, taking them day and night without rest. Yet his spirit remained clear, his body robust, and his complexion as rosy as a peach blossom. He could go days without eating. At ninety-seven, he appeared no older than thirty. He was especially fond of boasting and exaggeration. When drinking with guests, he would claim that the Mysterious Woman had sent wine, or the Plain Girl had sent fruit, or that Peng Zu and Rong Cheng had bequeathed him texts, insisting he was a true immortal.

One day, Dongbin disguised himself as a beggar and came to his door. Lou, not recognizing him, shouted at him to leave. Dongbin pressed his feet upon a stone, instantly carving two square holes three *cun* deep. Lou was astonished and invited him to sit at his side, saying, “You are no ordinary man.” He summoned his maidservants to sing *Songs of Roaming Immortals* and ordered wine to be served. Dongbin improvised a *Wang Jiangnan* lyric in response:

*At Jasper Pool,*
*Auspicious mists gather the immortals.*
*White-clad golden youths strike phoenix clappers,*
*Green-robed jade maidens play simurgh pipes—*
*One dwells in the Great Brahma Heaven.*
*In drunken reverie,*
*One floats to the Jade Capital Mountain.*
*The hymns of void-stepping end the feast,*
*Yet one knows not what year this evening is,*
*For the seas have turned to mulberry fields.*

The maidservants presented Shu paper, asking him to write. Dongbin wrote from the bottom of the page to the top, filling every space without leaving gaps. Lou was greatly astonished and delighted, and was about to ask his Daoist name when Dongbin said, “I have already transmitted it mouth to mouth.” Lou pressed further, and he repeated, “I have already transmitted it mouth to mouth.” Suddenly, he ascended to the top of a cypress tree outside the gate and vanished. Several days later...

Suddenly, Lou fell gravely ill, vomiting several pints of silvery phlegm before passing away. The "mouth-to-mouth transmission" and the two square cavities in the stone were both veiled references to the character *Lu* (吕).

◇ **Painting a Mountain at Luofu**
Dongbin traveled to the Vermilion Bright Abbey at Mount Luofu and arrived at a small hermitage. The Daoist master was away, leaving only a young novice behind. The boy bowed and asked, “Has the master come to visit?” Then he secretly took the Daoist’s wine and offered it to Dongbin. Dongbin drank sparingly, then urged the boy to finish the rest, but the boy refused. The novice had long suffered from cataracts in his eyes. Dongbin sprayed the remaining wine onto his eyes, and suddenly his vision cleared as if he had never been afflicted. Dongbin then took up a brush and painted a mountain on the wall, with three pools at its base. He told the boy, “If you drink my wine, you will attain immortality. If not, it is fate—yet you will still enjoy a long life.” With that, he flew into the stone wall and vanished. When the Daoist master returned and saw the painted mountain extending through the wall, he exclaimed in astonishment, “The three pools at the base form the character *yan* (嵓). Could this have been Master Lü?” Later, the boy indeed lived to the age of one hundred and fifty before passing away.

◇ **Tempering the Sword at Mount Lu**
Dongbin visited the True Silence Abbey on Mount Lu in Jiangzhou, where he tempered his sword by the steps. The Daoist Hou Yonghui asked him, “Master, what is this sword for?” Dongbin replied, “To eliminate all injustices in the world.” Hou found his words strange and invited him to drink wine and eat fruit. He remarked, “Master, your Daoist demeanor is lofty—you cannot be an ordinary man of this world.” Dongbin said, “Let us drink heartily; do not press me with questions.” Once drunk, he wrote a poem on the wall with the tip of his chopstick:

*Dare I shrink from toil to hone my blade?*
*At dawn, the jade dragon roars when the case is opened.*
*In my hand, its qi freezes three feet of ice;*
*On stone, its spirit coils like a serpent.*
*Tyrants’ blood silently follows the flowing water;*
*Villains’ stains now dissolve with the fading marks.*
*To cut down the world’s injustices,*
*I shall ascend with you to the Nine Heavens.*

When he finished, the words seemed invisible at first, yet the ink shone brilliantly through to the other side of the wall. Hou was astonished and bowed deeply, then asked about the sword’s method. Dongbin said, “There is the *Dao-sword* and the *Dharma-sword*. The *Dao-sword* moves in and out without form, while the *Dharma-sword* is wielded through technique—this is what common eyes perceive, and it can only dispel demons and banish malevolent spirits.” Hou asked, “If one were to slay evil men with the *Dao-sword* amidst a crowd, would it not shock the world?” Dongbin replied, “Man’s spirit is his mother, and qi his child. When the spirit remains, qi gathers; when the spirit departs, qi scatters. Strike the spirit, and the qi will leave—the man will vanish of his own accord, or borrow another’s form. All such cases follow this principle.” Hou sighed, “These are truly the words of an immortal. May I know your name?” Dongbin said, “I am Lü Yan.” With that, he cast his sword into the air and ascended with it.

◇ **Celestial Music at the Feast**
In Luoyang, Chen Zhizhong built a grand estate in the Eastern Capital, where he gathered friends and family for a feast. Suddenly, a ragged Daoist arrived—it was Dongbin. Chen asked, “What skills do you possess?” Dongbin replied, “I have a set of celestial music. I would like to perform it to grace your splendid gathering.” From his waist, he produced a scroll of paintings and hung it on a pillar. It depicted twelve celestial maidens, each holding a musical instrument. Dongbin called to them, and they descended like a string of pearls, lining up before him. Two maidens held banners to lead the procession, while the others played music. Their skin was like jade, their faces like flowers, their voices delicate and sweet. They wore seven-jeweled crowns, gossamer robes, golden pendants, and jade ornaments that chimed softly as they moved. Each had a grain of yellow jade on the bridge of her nose, and their bodies were ethereal, unlike mortal beings. The music soared clear and bright through the misty heavens, its melodies unlike anything of this world.

When the three suites concluded, Chen asked, “What manner of women are these?” The Daoist replied, “They are the Six Jia and Six Ding Jade Maidens. When one perfects the Dao, the three hun and seven po within the body, along with the spirits of the five viscera and six bowels, all transform into such beings. Would you, sir, also wish to cultivate this path?” Chen, deeming it mere illusion and deception, felt deeply displeased. The Daoist turned to the maidens and said, “You may depart.” They then ascended back into the scroll. The Daoist took the scroll, opened his mouth, and swallowed it whole. He then demanded paper and brush and wrote in large characters:

*Through three thousand kalpas in the heavens I’ve passed,*
*Five hundred years among men I’ve spent at last.*
*At my waist, a sword-blade flashes purple lightning bright,*
*In the crucible, elixir flames rise in smoky might.*
*Once I rode a white deer o’er the boundless sea,*
*Now astride a blue ox, I enter grottoes free.*
*Such small arts are but trifles, a fleeting play—*
*None know I am a true immortal today.*

At the end, he signed: “Written by the Valley Guest.” He then stepped out the door and vanished in an instant. Chen exclaimed, “The Valley Guest must have been Dongbin!” Overcome with remorse, he wished to gouge out his own eyes, and not long after, he passed away.

◇ **A Brush Shaft Floats on the Waves**
Lu Dongbin’s calligrapher friend, Master Zhai, delighted in hosting knights-errant. Dongbin went to visit him and was received with the utmost courtesy, lodging in Zhai’s home. From then on, he came and went for over a year. One day, he took Zhai to the riverside, snapped a brush shaft in two, and set the pieces afloat on the waves. Dongbin stepped onto one half, urging the calligrapher to do the same. Terrified, Zhai dared not move forward. Dongbin laughed and crossed the water, reaching the opposite shore before vanishing. Only then did Zhai realize he was an extraordinary being. Ten days later, Dongbin returned, bringing food for Zhai—but all of it was rotten and foul. Zhai covered his nose and declined to eat. Dongbin sighed deeply and said, “Since you cannot stomach coarse fare, I shall leave you two jars of meat sauce.” He then departed and was never seen again. When Zhai opened the jars, he found them filled with gold flakes. Were not “two jars” simply two large vats?

◇ **The Sliced Fish Revives**
While traveling through Lushan, Dongbin stopped at a wineshop where he saw a fish being butchered for sashimi. He declared, “I can make this fish live again.” The sashimi-maker did not believe him. Dongbin placed a single pill into the fish’s belly, and after a long while, the fish leapt and writhed as if alive. Astonished, the man released it into the river, where it swam away, vigorous and free. Dongbin had already disappeared.

○ **Renaming and Enlightenment**
◇ **Hui the Recluse**

◇ **The Ancient Iron Mirror**

The Ministerial Gentleman Jia Shixiong kept an ancient iron mirror, which he once wished to temper and polish. Dongbin, styling himself as Recluse Hui, paid him a visit and begged to test his skill. From his bamboo case, he took a small measure of powder and placed it upon the mirror. Then he took his leave, saying, “Wait while I fetch more elixir.” When pursued, he was nowhere to be found. Only at the gate of the Taiping Temple where he had lodged was a poem inscribed:

*In hand, a green serpent soars beneath the white sun,*
*Within the grotto, immortal fruits bloom with eternal spring.*
*Know that the mist-and-clouds wanderer dwells beyond the world—*
*Not one who grinds mirrors amid the dust.*

When they looked at the mirror, the powder had already vanished, leaving only a single spot of luminous brilliance like jade.

◇ **Daoist Hui**

While traveling in Changsha, Dongbin disguised himself as Daoist Hui, carrying a small clay jar to beg for coins. Though he received countless coins, the jar never filled, and people all marveled at him. One day, seated in the marketplace, he declared, “Whoever can fill my jar with coins shall be taught the Dao.” People vied to toss coins into the jar, yet it never filled. A monk, driving a cartload of coins, jested, “Can your jar hold all this?” The Daoist assented. As the cart was pushed into the jar, it clattered loudly—then suddenly vanished. The monk exclaimed, “Was that a divine immortal’s doing—or mere illusion?”

---

The Daoist then improvised a poem:
Neither god nor immortal am I,
Neither art nor illusion this.
Heaven and earth shall one day end,
Mulberry fields shift and change again.
The body was never truly mine—
Why cling to what cannot remain?
Why not follow me and roam,
Riding the whale through boundless space?

The monk grew even more astonished and doubtful, attempting to seize him. The Daoist said, “Do you regret these coins? I shall repay you now.” He took a slip of paper, tossed it into the jar, and prayed, “Quickly push the cart out.” After a long while, nothing emerged. He said, “I must retrieve it myself,” and leapt into the jar, vanishing without a sound. The monk struck the jar to pieces and found a slip of paper inscribed with a poem:

To seek the true, first know the true,
Yet seeing it, you fail to wake.
A laugh, and we meet once more—
Driving your cart on Dongping’s road.

The monk returned in dismay, and as he reached Dongping, he suddenly saw the Daoist, who said, “I have awaited you for some time.” He returned the cart, coins all intact, and said, “I am Master Lü. At first, I thought you could be taught. But now, so attached to money, you cannot.” The monk began to repent and apologize, but it was too late.

◇ **Turning the Heart**

In Anfeng County, the courtesan Cao Erxiang contracted a loathsome illness and was made to lodge travelers in an inn. Dongbin, disguised as a destitute scholar, sought shelter there. The servant, seeing his ragged clothes, refused him. Erxiang said, “Since I have taken up this profession, what does filth or cleanliness matter?” She welcomed him in and treated him with great courtesy. Soon after, Cao’s illness flared, and she groaned in agony. Dongbin took a pair of chopsticks and pricked her thigh, saying, “Turn your heart, turn your heart.”

Outside the gate stood a soapberry tree, long withered and dead. Dongbin cast medicine upon it and departed. The next day, the tree revived, its branches and leaves lush and thriving. Only then did Cao realize he was an immortal—and that “turning the heart” referred to Lü. She cast off her headdress and robes, washed away her powder and rouge, abandoned her home, and journeyed far away. The people built a shrine to Master Lü and offered him worship.

At the end of the Shaoxing era, Cao suddenly returned to her hometown, her appearance refined and radiant, unrecognized by anyone. She recounted her story from beginning to end, then left again, her fate unknown.

◇ **The Unattached Elder Chang**

On Mount Dayu of Hengpu, a wealthy young man, enamored of the Dao, built a hermitage to receive wandering scholars for many years. One day, as the assembly had just completed a grand *Gulu* vegetarian feast, a ragged Daoist arrived seeking alms. The crowd showed no compassion, some even insulting him. The Daoist inscribed a *ci* poem:

"A brief sojourn at Dayu—
Who speaks with the white crane that comes?
By the ridge’s homesteads,
How many winters has the plum blossom seen?

Spring arrives, spring departs,
One lingers where fallen flowers meet flowing waters.
Flowers fill the front stream,
Hiding all immortals from mortal sight."

At the end he inscribed: *"The Unattached Elder Chang has come."* The five characters were written in three distinct styles. When he finished, he stepped into the Cloud Terrace and did not reemerge. After a long while, they searched for him, but he had vanished. Upon closer inspection, his writing had penetrated through to the other side of the wall. Only then did they realize that the character *Chang* was written without its "heart" (无心). He was none other than Master Lü. The assembly sighed in awe and regret.

◇ **Venerable Bin**

At the Hermitage of the Venerable Elder on Mount Qingcheng, Huang Ruogu was a man of refined bearing, strict discipline, and spotless conduct. He often used the *Tianxin* talismanic water and the *Three Lights* true talismans to treat illnesses, and whatever money or silk he received, he immediately distributed to the poor.

Lü Dongbin, disguised as Venerable Bin of the Upper Retreat, stayed for over a month. The talismans and registers he crafted would often rise into the air, transforming into dragons, serpents, clouds, and mist before flying away. When he summoned generals to expel ghosts, their forms would manifest, and they spoke in human tongues. His footsteps shook the earth like thunder, his gaze flashed like lightning, his breath formed clouds, and his spittle became rain.

He was also skilled in painting, requiring neither brush nor ink. He would merely hold inked water in his mouth and spray it onto silk or paper, naturally forming landscapes, flowers, trees, palaces, birds, beasts, and human figures—only a light touch was needed to refine them. Whenever he earned money from his paintings, he would buy wine and drink heartily with Ruogu. Though Ruogu could hold his liquor without limit, he was always bested by Bin.

One day, Ruogu asked, "Master, your conduct is unlike that of ordinary men. You must have come from among the immortals. Can you speak to me of the Dao?"

Bin replied, "The seven stars of the Northern Dipper on your left foot lack one—how could you attain the Dao? Another lifetime will be needed."

Ruogu exclaimed in astonishment, "Master Bin must be a sage!" For beneath his left foot was a birthmark shaped like the Northern Dipper, missing one star—a fact no one had ever known.

Ruogu then asked, "How long is my lifespan?"

Dongbin wrote ninety-four characters in reverse on the wall, enclosing them within two interlocking circles, then took his leave. Only later did Ruogu realize that the two circles formed the character *Lü*, and *Bin* was his given name. At forty-nine, Ruogu passed away, fulfilling the prophecy of the reversed inscription.

◇ **Huang the Auspicious Elder**

Zhong Jiang of Zhongshan in Changsha, having resigned from office in Jiankang during the Ji-Si year of the Jiading era, was returning home by boat. He stopped at Nanjin in Baling...

As dusk approached, a boat suddenly passed by. On board stood an elder in a yellow robe, his bearing and countenance extraordinarily refined. He stood motionless, gazing intently at Zhongshan for a long while. Zhongshan peered into the boat’s canopy and saw no other belongings—only ten black jars lined up at the bow, and two young attendants in blue-green robes standing unevenly before the canopy. Zhongshan assumed he was merely crossing the ferry. Yet after the boat had traveled no more than two *zhang*, it turned its oars and returned, the yellow-robed elder once again seated upright behind the canopy. He fixed his gaze on Zhongshan for another long while, then suddenly the boat vanished without a trace. Zhongshan had initially taken him for a wealthy merchant and had not spoken to him, but now he was struck with astonishment, realizing this was no ordinary man.

The next day, Zhongshan went to Lüxian Temple to pay homage before the true image of the immortal. There, he saw the figure clad in a yellow robe, with two young attendants in blue-green standing beside him—his appearance and even their faces were an exact match to what he had witnessed the day before. Zhongshan lamented his mortal eyes for failing to recognize a true immortal and sighed endlessly. Later, Zhou Xing composed a *shuidiao getou* lyric, which included the lines: *"As if at Nanjin Port, / Once more I meet Lord Lü’s boat."*

The following year, Zhongshan passed away. His grandson later showed me a portrait of the yellow-robed elder painted by his grandfather, its refinement and transcendent elegance truly unmatched.

◇ **Seeking Audience at the Confucian Gate**
◇ **An Audience with Prefect Wang of Yuezhou**

The Erudite of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, Ren Lun, was serving as Prefect of Yuezhou when a Daoist monk named Hui sought an audience. His appearance was striking, with his shins exposed, and his voice clear and resonant. Lun inquired about his lineage. Hui replied, "My lineage is of no consequence. What I seek to discuss is the game of *weiqi*." They played, and though Lun was renowned as a master of the board, he suffered repeated defeats. As dusk fell, Lun offered him wine and asked, "Where are you from?" Hui wrote a poem in response:

*Lineage and name are plainly recorded,*
*Yet I, a weary traveler from Penglai, am Master Lü.*
*How much can mortal eyes truly perceive?*
*They fail to recognize the old tree spirit south of the city.*

Before Lun could recover from his astonishment, Hui had vanished. A mist of swirling clouds lingered in the courtyard for some time before dissipating.

◇ **An Audience with Secretary Shi**

Secretary Shi, Wang Xiu, was avoiding the summer heat when a ragged woodcutter approached, axe in hand. His features were refined, his speech sharp and lucid. Shi asked about his hometown and lineage. The woodcutter replied, "I was born in Henan but later moved to Mount Zhongnan. I am a descendant of Lü Wei. My studies are confined to the *Zhuangzi* and the *Daodejing*; beyond these, I have no other pursuits." Shi asked, "What does Zhongnan Mountain possess?" The woodcutter answered with a verse:

*What does Zhongnan hold?*
*It holds but white clouds.*
*These bring joy to me alone—*
*They cannot be gifted to you.*

Shi found him extraordinary and detained him for two days, discussing methods to transcend life and death. As they parted, the woodcutter said, "I am bound for Yueyang." He left Shi with a single elixir pellet to consume. Shi lived to over ninety, yet his vitality remained like that of an infant.

◇ **Defying Authority in Baling**

Lü Dongbin was walking through the Baling market when the Prefect emerged.

**Defying Authority in Baling (continued)**

The Prefect’s vanguard seized him. The Prefect imprisoned him and ordered him to write a confession by sundown, yet by dusk, not a single word had been set down. The jailer pressed him. Lü Dongbin said, “Wait until I sober up.” The jailer said, “Do you not fear punishment? And you still seek solace in wine?” Before he finished speaking, Lü vanished without a trace, leaving only a sheet of paper inscribed with a poem:

*For a time I roamed the seas of Penglai,*
*By chance I met the Prefect, who asked my origin.*
*I dwell beneath the Dipper’s handle,*
*My sword hangs at the moon’s horn in the Southern Palace.*
*When I say I’m drunk, I am truly drunk—*
*How could I know what sorrow is?*
*Why did we meet yet not recognize each other?*
*I ride the white clouds back and depart.*

The Prefect, startled, exclaimed, “This is Lord Lü!” At dawn, he burned incense and begged forgiveness. One day, Lü appeared in a basin of water. The Prefect hastily summoned a painter to capture his likeness, which bore a striking resemblance to the portrait of Teng Zijing.

◇ **Visiting Daoist Abbeys**

**A Visit to Taiping Abbey**
At Taiping Abbey in Jiangzhou, a Daoist of lofty aspirations resided. Lü Dongbin visited him and presented a poem:

*Fallen and carefree, Scholar Xue,*
*Advanced in years yet beardless still.*
*In clouds he idly lies on stone,*
*In mountains he coldly seeks old steles.*
*He boasts of drinking great wine,*
*Scorns those who recite petty verse.*
*What manner of man is he?*
*Let the crowd mock as they will.*

At the end, he inscribed in small script: *“Written by Daoist Hui when visiting Master Xue the Alchemist with three companions.”* Only then was it known that *Hui* was Lü’s sobriquet.

**A Visit to Tianqing Abbey**
At Tianqing Abbey in Fulixian, Suzhou, a Daoist named Ning, though young, discoursed on the *Laozi* and *Zhuangzi* with extraordinary insight. One morning, a traveling medicine-seller arrived—it was Lü Dongbin, his bearing majestic. He stayed for over a month, then imparted the essentials of the *Laozi* and *Zhuangzi* to the Daoist: *“I observe that all Chan teachings derive from Laozi and Zhuangzi. Though there be thousands of sutras and myriad volumes, with endless debates and distinctions, they all establish their own schools, trace their lineages, and transmit their doctrines—yet in truth, they all stem from the principles of Laozi and Zhuangzi.”* Before departing, he inscribed two quatrains on the door in large seal script, the brushwork bold and dynamic. They read:

Amidst autumn’s desolate scene, leaves swirl in chaotic flight,
Beneath the pine’s shadow in the courtyard, time slips by as I sit.
Clouds obscure the crane-drawn chariot—where has it gone?
The immortal grotto’s morning audience has passed me by.

Second quatrain:
From age to age, the script of immortals transmits its thousand-year arts,
Mouth reciting the two-scroll *Huangting Jing*.
Within the crane’s abode, by the ancient altar amid pine shadows,
All is hushed—no footprints, the door ever barred.

After his departure, people scraped the characters to treat illnesses, and many were cured. The strokes penetrated over an inch into the wood, the ink traces indelible.

---

At Tianqing Abbey in Qinzhou, when Lü Dongbin visited, all the Daoist clerics had gone to the capital’s jiao rites, leaving only a young novice behind. Dongbin asked for a brush to inscribe the wall. The novice declined, saying, *“The abbey halls are newly renovated; the master forbade defacing the walls.”* Dongbin replied, *“Then trouble yourself to tend the incense burner in the Three Clarities’ hall—I wish to pay my respects.”*

Once there, he saw the limpid water of a pond behind the hall and, with his fingernail, etched into the wall:
*This stone pond’s clear water mirrors my heart,*
*Yet peach blossoms’ shadows plunge it into turmoil.*
*Once within the palace halls of Guishan,*
*I dissipate idle thoughts and still my mind with the seven-stringed zither.*

At the end, he inscribed: *“Written by Hui after returning.”* The wall was exceedingly high, beyond the reach of human hands. The crowd marveled, then realized *Hui* was Lü’s courtesy name, and *“after returning”* referred to the master’s return. Later, the master opposed this.

---

At a mountain temple, playing among the monks’ quarters:
A transformation in a monastic temple.

Lu Dongbin once wandered into a mountain temple, where he transformed his sword into a ravishing woman who entered the monastery. The monks’ eyes followed her in rapture, their spirits scattered and wills undone. As she passed the meditation hall, one monk sat cross-legged apart, paying her no heed, and walked straight out the gate as though untouched by desire. “This one is teachable,” thought Lu.

Once outside, he awaited the monk in a secluded spot, intending to waylay and provoke him. Beauty ensnares the senses—how hard it is to uproot the seeds of karmic entanglement. This was the first chapter of their Daoist affinity.

---

Lu Dongbin arrived at Jin’e Temple in Siming, where the abbot’s quarters stood stark and empty. Presently, a novice emerged. Lu asked, “Why so desolate?” The boy replied, “Do not call it desolate—it is emptiness itself.” Pleased by his words, Lu inscribed a poem on the wall:

*The abbot’s door stands ever ajar,*
*Yet here a mountain boy bares his feet.*
*I ask why the abbot’s quarters lie so still—*
*He says, “Emptiness, yet not even that.”*

Hearing this, how could one not rejoice?
Was the master no ordinary man?
I came to pay my respects but found him gone—
My seeking heart burns bright with dust and longing.

I return now, crossing the boundless expanse,
My path leading to Penglai’s distant peaks.
When next I climb the stone tower,
Snow will gleam, the sea stretch wide, and a thousand summits dawn.

---

At a temple on Mount Lu, the monk Fazhen of Kaiyuan Monastery had sat in meditation for twenty years, his precepts and conduct beyond reproach. One day, as he sat in samadhi, a Daoist appeared and asked, “Master, do you believe the Dao is found only in sitting?” Fazhen replied, “Yes.” The Daoist said, “The Buddha strictly forbids greed, anger, lust, and killing. While sitting, you may feel free of such thoughts, but when confronted with sights and objects, you cannot master yourself—these impulses scatter like wild horses. Can the Dao truly reside in sitting alone?”

He led Fazhen to the meditation hall, where a monk lay fast asleep. “Let us sit here awhile and observe him,” the Daoist said. After some time, a small snake, over three inches long, emerged from the sleeping monk’s crown, slithered down the bedpost to the floor, and devoured his spittle. It then drank from his chamber pot before gliding outside. Crossing a small ditch, it coiled around a flower terrace as if lingering in delight. When it attempted to cross another ditch, the water had risen, forcing it to retreat. The Daoist blocked its path with a small blade planted in the earth. The snake recoiled, found a side route to the bed’s right post, and ascended back into the monk’s crown.

The sleeping monk suddenly awoke and greeted the Daoist and Fazhen, saying, “I just had a dream. Let me tell you both: I dreamed I left through the left gate and came upon an exquisite feast, which I ate. Then I found fine wine and drank my fill. Lifting my robes, I crossed a small river outside the gate, where I met dozens of beautiful women and indulged in pleasure. I tried to cross another river, but the water had surged, blocking my way.
