A bandit appeared, intent on killing me, so I fled by a shortcut, re-entered through the right gate, and awoke.”

The Daoist and Fazhen laughed heartily. The Daoist said, “The bedpost was your gate, the spittle your feast, the urine your wine, the ditch your river, the flowers and trees your women, and the blade your bandit. Such are the illusions of human dreams!” Fazhen asked, “What of the snake?” The Daoist replied, “This monk harbors deep-seated anger and venom. His habitual tendencies have transformed him, assuming the form of a snake. When he closes his eyes in death, he will be reborn as one. How can he not be cautious?”

“I am Lü,” he said.

“Seeing your sincere devotion, I have come to teach you the Way.” Fazhen then followed him, and their destination remained unknown.

◇ *The Gift of the Kaiyuan Era*

At the bathhouse of Kaiyuan Temple in Yuanzhou stood a large well, its spring water sweet and cold. Dongbin grew fond of it and lingered for ten days, befriending the temple monks. The bathhouse monk treated him with utmost respect, unaware that he was Dongbin. Upon departing, Dongbin presented him with a few ink tablets. The monk stored them away without further thought.

One day, Li Dalin, the Transport Commissioner of Jiangxi, arrived in Yuanzhou. He sought out the monk and asked, “Did Master Lü ever gift you gold?” The monk, startled, replied, “I did not recognize Master Lü. A Daoist once visited and gave me ink, but no gold.” He produced the ink to show Li, only to find it had turned to gold. Li, astonished, rubbed the tablets in amazement and offered to exchange them for other gold. The monk refused but presented him with one tablet, asking, “How did the Commissioner learn of this?”

Li recounted, “Yesterday, passing through Lingling, I met He Xiangu, who inquired about Master Lü’s recent activities. She said, ‘Lü recently mentioned, “I stayed long in Yichun and grew close to the monk of Kaiyuan Temple’s bathhouse. Delighted by his Daoist bearing and immortal bones, I left him gold.”’ I heard her words and came to verify them.”

A fortnight later, Lü Dongbin returned and asked the monk, “Where is the ink?” The monk recounted all that had transpired. Dongbin laughed and said, “That woman is too talkative.” Then, taking the monk by the hand, he departed, and their destination remained unknown.

◇ *Gathering for Meals at Dayun Temple*
Dongbin, disguising himself as the recluse Hui, wandered to Dayun Temple, where he joined the communal meals for over a month. He remarked to the monks, “Your meals are most refined, but they lack noodles.” With that, he left. Ten days later, he returned with a small portion of flour, prepared the noodles himself, and served them—enough to sate hundreds of monks. The monks invited the recluse to tea. Citing the poem by Minister Ding, they recited:

*“Petals scatter as the monk’s chopsticks break them,
Clouds follow the guest’s bowl, rounding its rim.”*

The recluse replied, “The lines are fine, but they do not fully capture the essence of tea.” He then inscribed a poem:

*“Two tender leaves and a bud—peerless in quality,
Monks’ craftsmanship refines it with utmost skill.
Hare’s fur, gull’s down: fragrant clouds of white,
Shrimp’s eyes, froth’s waves: delicate ripples unite.
It dispels sleep’s grip, banishing drowsiness from seat and mat,
And infuses the flesh with a pure, invigorating qi.
Born wild in the ravines beyond mortal reach,
It scorns transplantation to the imperial capital.”*

He left the monks with a single cinnabar pellet, saying, “Take this, and you shall never die.” Then he departed. Later, the monks too ascended as immortals.

**◇ Blending with the Marketplace**

**◇ The Wine Shop of Shaozhou**
Outside the city of Shaozhou, an old woman ran a wine shop. One day, a Daoist named Lü came to ask for a drink. Finding no wine left, she gave him a *sheng* of lees-laden dregs. The Daoist asked the price, and she replied, “Twenty coins per *sheng*.” He traced twenty characters onto a purple stone by the door with his finger—when he left, the script had seeped through to the stone’s underside, leaving almost no margin. From then on, crowds gathered like the sea, and the wine shop thrived. Later, a temple named *Jixian Guan* was built on the site.

**◇ The Wine Vessel of Yongkang**
In Yongkang Prefecture, a man named Ni had just opened a winehouse when a Daoist arrived, demanding drink. From dawn till dusk, he consumed over a *dan* of fine wine. The onlookers marveled, and Ni pressed him for payment. The Daoist glared in silence, then collapsed in a drunken stupor. Ni kept watch over him. At the first drum of dawn, the Daoist suddenly rose, seized a brush, and inscribed a poem on the wall:

*“A whale’s gulp, a turtle’s swallow—hundreds of cups,
Which jade mountain rises, which crumbles?
Sober, the sleeves chill with heaven’s wind,
A crimson cloud drifts in from the sea.”*

At the end, he wrote: *“Composed by Yang Chun, Daoist of the Three Mountains.”* He tossed a clod of earth at Ni and strode out. Gazing northeast, he saw a crimson cloud approaching, clapped his hands in laughter, and vanished. When they scraped the wall, the ink had soaked through to the other side; the clod of earth was pure gold. From then on, the winehouse flourished, and they realized Yang Chun was none other than *Chunyang*.

**◇ The Teahouse of Bianjing**
At the end of the Later Zhou, a family named Shi ran a teahouse in Bianjing. Their young daughter, still wearing her hair in childhood braids, served the tea. Lü Dongbin disguised himself as a beggar, coming daily to claim the best seat and demand tea. His clothes were ragged, his flesh caked with filth—scarcely bearable to approach. Yet the girl showed no revulsion, bringing him the finest leaves each time. Her parents grew furious and beat her, but she continued to serve him without complaint for over a month.

One day, Dongbin said to her, *“Can you drink the dregs of the tea I’ve sipped?”* She found it too foul to swallow and poured it onto the ground. Suddenly, an extraordinary fragrance arose. She hastily licked it, and her spirit felt refreshed.

Dongbin said, *“I am Master Lü, no beggar. I regret you cannot finish my leavings, yet I can grant your wishes. Do you desire wealth? Or nobility?”*

“Or long life?” The girl replied, *“I am but a humble girl and know not what nobility entails. To be wealthy and long-lived would suffice.”* Dongbin departed and did not return.

In later years, she indeed rose to prominence. She lived to the age of one hundred and thirty-five before passing.

---

**Yanzhou Brothel**
In Yanzhou, a courtesan named Hou (her surname lost to time) ran an inn to lodge travelers. Dongbin, disguised in rags, sought lodging. He would leave at dawn and return at dusk, invariably drunk, and after a month had yet to pay a single coin. Hou summoned him for tea.

Dongbin said, *“I met Master Zhongli, who told me you are ready to hear of the Dao.”* Hou did not understand and offered him wine instead. Dongbin drank without cease. Hou grew increasingly displeased.

Dongbin stretched out his arm, revealing a hidden golden hairpin. He removed one and had her trade it for wine. Greedy for the gold, Hou asked, *“Will you stay the night after drinking?”* He replied, *“Very well.”* He climbed onto the bed, snoring loudly.

At midnight, Hou approached the bed, but Dongbin pushed her away with his hand. She retreated at once. By dawn, Dongbin had vanished. Examining her body, she found where his hand had touched her—his surname, *Lü*, etched deep into her skin.

Hou awoke to the truth: *“This was Lord Lü! Could it be our bond from a past life? A moment’s hesitation cost me dearly. Has he come to deliver me?”* She cut her hair, donned coarse robes, and set out to find Dongbin, but his whereabouts remained unknown.

---

**Guangling Brothel**
In Guangling, a courtesan named Huang Ying possessed great beauty, and wealthy patrons filled her door. One day, a scholar named Lü sought lodging. Huang rejected him for his ragged, filthy appearance. The scholar inscribed two poems on her screen. The first read:

"Moyu and Xishi share this same body—
pity the young and old are sundered by spring.
One day, a crone with crane-white hair and chicken-skin wrinkles,
today, a maiden of jade countenance and flower-like beauty."

The second read:
"Flowers bloom and flowers fall—both bring sorrow and joy,
as flowers change with time, so too does the person.
Blooming on the branch, they fear the guest’s plucking;
fallen to the ground, who will deign to look?"

The poems inscribed, the scholar vanished in an instant.

◇Eastern Capital Brothel
In the Eastern Capital, a courtesan named Yang Liu was a peerless beauty. A Daoist priest frequented her house, repeatedly offering gold and silk, yet he never once engaged with Yang. One evening, Yang, drunk, pressed herself upon him. The Daoist said: *"Within my body, the precelestial Kan and Li unite—husband and wife join internally, the sacred embryo already formed, the infant soon to be born. How could I still crave external beauty? The joy of internal union far surpasses that of external union."*

Yang found his words strange and astonishing. At the time, the prime minister Zhang Tianjue’s guest, Su, had long been intimate with Yang. She told Su, and Su told Zhang, who hastened to question the Daoist. The Daoist cried out and fled, rushing straight to Qiyun Hermitage, where he entered the hall and did not emerge. After a long while, they forced open the door to search for him, but he had already disappeared. Only a poem remained on the wall:

*"With one breath, the phoenix flute rends the Great Clarity,
a green-robed youth treads the void with celestial song.
The jade tower awakens a thousand-year dream—
on the branch of the peach of immortality, the golden rooster crows."*

Later, the hermitage burned to the ground without a trace, yet the wall bearing the poem stood firm and untouched—another marvel.

◇Crane Assembly at Qingcheng

In the final years of the Shaoxing era, Lü Dongbin attended the Crane Assembly at Mount Qingcheng. He rested at the home of a pastry vendor, who did not recognize him and found him rather peculiar. Dongbin took thick ink and boldly inscribed a poem upon the door’s great wooden panel:

*"Only fear the needle’s heart departs,
true bronze transmutes to gold in flowing water.
At the temples, no white hair appears—
the sturdy steed’s path is hard to follow."*

The four lines concealed the characters for *"Lü Dongbin has come."* The brushwork was mighty and vigorous, its brilliance extraordinary; when carved away, the strokes penetrated through to the wood’s reverse. By then, Dongbin had vanished without a trace. A local named Guan Yunxiang witnessed this and promptly painted his likeness—a gaunt Daoist. Thereafter, the pastry vendor’s business thrived.

◇Hanging One’s Robe at Jiangzhou
At the ritual hall of Prefect Pan in Ruichang County, Jiangzhou, a Daoist once arrived seeking to hang his robe. He carried neither bundle nor umbrella, only a tattered bamboo hat, his appearance coarse and rustic. The hall keeper scorned him, saying, *"With no umbrella or bundle, how can you hang your robe?"* The Daoist replied, *"If you will not permit it, then grant me a cup of tea before I go."* The keeper relented and bade him sit. Upon returning, he found the Daoist seated in the host’s place. Enraged, the keeper demanded, *"Where is your sense of guest and host? What sort of Daoist are you?"* The Daoist departed without a bow, leaving behind his hat. The keeper could not lift it. The assembly then chanted sutras to offer apology, whereupon the hat was raised—beneath it, the character *"Lü"* was inscribed upon the ground. When the sick took earth from the spot and boiled it into a decoction, they recovered at once. Over the years, a well formed there, its bubbling waters shaping the character *"Lü"* upon the surface; when disturbed, the strokes would re-form. The well remains to this day.

◇Cinnabar Medicine for the People
◇Scarlet Silk-Wrapped Remedy

In the Eastern Capital, a plague ravaged the populace for a year. An elderly woman who sold tea at home saw her children and grandchildren all fall ill. One day, a Daoist arrived. The woman treated him kindly and pleaded for her family’s affliction. The Daoist said, *"Await me tomorrow at dawn."* The woman rose early to wait. The Daoist presented a scarlet silk-wrapped remedy and instructed, *"When the illness strikes, have the sufferer hold it, and they will recover. One pill can heal a hundred, but beyond that, it loses its efficacy."* The woman followed his words, and her children and grandchildren were cured. After healing a hundred people, the remedy indeed ceased to work. When she opened the silk pouch, the medicine had vanished—only the three characters *"Lü Dongbin"* remained. Then she knew she had encountered Master Lü.

◇Filial Piety Moves Heaven to Save a Mother
In Tonglu, there was a prefectural officer whose name is forgotten. His mother suffered from a carbuncle on her back, and despite countless remedies, she did not recover. He prayed fervently, and Lü Dongbin was moved by his filial devotion. That night, Lü appeared in his dream and said, *"Your filial piety has touched Heaven, and I am commanded to save her. If you delay by even a day, she cannot be cured."* He then bestowed the recipe for the *"Numinous Treasure Ointment"*: five *gualou* fruits, their seeds removed, and five pieces of frankincense, each the size of a jujube. Grind each ingredient finely, then simmer with one *jin* of white honey to form an ointment. Take three *qian* per dose, dissolved in warm wine. The officer procured the herbs, administered the remedy, and his mother recovered at once. Later, when he shared it with others, it proved instantly effective.

◇The Healing of a Lame Man in Zhaozhou
In Zhaozhou, a poor man named Liu had been lame for twenty years. Each evening, he lit incense and prayed to Heaven. One day, a Daoist carrying an iron ladle approached him and said, *"Follow me."* Liu followed him.

After walking about two *li*, the Daoist pointed to the ground and said, *"Beneath here, three *chi* deep, lies a five-colored stone. Try digging it up."* Sure enough, they unearthed a stone the size of a pellet, its five hues uncommonly radiant. The Daoist said, *"Take it home, expose it to the sun for nine days, then grind it into fine powder. Steep it in a decoction of *mugua* peel and drink. Once healed, come to the third chamber on the left of the eastern corridor of Zhuyun Hall, east of the city, and we shall meet again on its wall."* Liu’s ailment vanished at once. He went to seek the Daoist but saw only an image of Lü Dongbin holding an iron ladle upon the wall.

◇The Healing of Eyes in Jiangling
In Jiangling, a Daoist named Fu revered the image of Lü Dongbin with utmost devotion. On New Year’s Day of the Qiandao era, a guest in square cap and robe entered and conversed with him. After some time, the guest invited him to drink, and Fu accompanied him. Thereafter, the guest visited every ten days. At the time, Fu’s eyes were dim and teary. The guest instructed him to take equal parts of raw and prepared *dihuang*, sliced and baked, along with Sichuan peppercorns—removing stems, seeds, and closed pods—lightly stir-fried. These were to be blended with honey into pills, fifty taken on an empty stomach with salted water. After prolonged use, Fu’s vision cleared. Reflecting on the guest’s appearance, he bore a striking resemblance to the Lü Dongbin image he venerated.

◇Selling Medicine in Yueyang
Lü Dongbin traveled to Yueyang, where he assumed a false name to peddle medicine, pricing each pill at a thousand gold pieces. For three days, none sold. He then ascended Yueyang Tower, consumed his own medicine, and suddenly rose into the air, standing suspended. The crowd, astonished and envious, clamored to buy his pills. Dongbin laughed and said, *"The Dao lies before your eyes, Penglai but a half-step away; yet you grasp the trigger but do not fire, missing what is right before you."* He then recited a poem:

At dawn he roams the Northern Sea, by dusk the verdant Wu;
In his sleeve, a green snake—his qi bold and unrefined.
Thrice he entered Yueyang, yet none knew his face;
Reciting verses, he soared across Dongting Lake.

◇Holding the Elixir in Chengdu
At the medicine market in Chengdu, a Daoist appeared daily—his face grimed, his robes tattered—holding a single elixir pill. He cried out in the streets: *"I am Lü Dongbin. Whoever bows to me twice shall receive this pill."* The crowd deemed him mad, gathering to mock and follow him. The Daoist repeated his offer several times, yet none bowed. He then sat by the fire pit before the Five Manifestations Temple, where children pelted him with tiles and stones. The Daoist laughed and said: *"The world longs to see me. Yet having seen me, they do not recognize me—such is fate."* Lü then consumed the golden elixir himself. Soon, five-colored clouds enveloped him, and in an instant, he vanished. The crowd was filled with remorse.

◇Jue Neng Receives the Elixir
Huang Jue Neng was renowned for his poetry. One day, while seeing a guest off outside the Eastern Capital’s gate at an inn, he encountered a feathered immortal. Huang offered wine and delicacies, and after sharing them, the immortal raised his cup, traced the character *"Lü"* in water, and said: *"Next year, we shall meet in Jiangnan."* The following year, Huang was indeed transferred to an official post in Jiangnan, where he again met Dongbin. The immortal gave him seven large coins, ten medium ones, and three small coins, saying: *"This number must not be increased. I entrust you with this measure of medicine. On New Year’s Day, grind it with wine and consume it. Thus, you may remain free of illness for the year."* Huang followed his instructions, and by seventy-three, his medicine nearly exhausted, he passed away in the Eastern Capital.

◇De Cheng Receives the Elixir
Li De Cheng was skilled in medicine. On a bitterly cold day, he encountered a destitute Daoist, clad in thin robes yet showing no sign of chill. The Daoist entered a wineshop with Li and his companions, seating himself at the head of the table. Li found this strange.

The tavern keeper said, *"Pay for the wine first."* The Daoist went inside and retrieved three wine flasks, saying, *"Each contains a measure of wine money."* The keeper checked and found it so, then poured three measures of wine for him. The Daoist poured and drank, while Li took only one flask—the other two emptied themselves. The Daoist said to Li, *"This is but a minor art. I am Lü Dongbin."* Li was astonished and overjoyed. The Daoist inscribed a quatrain:

*"The Son of Heaven within the ninefold palace is esteemed,
The five ranks of lords beyond its gates are honored.
Yet how can they compare to a ragged, drunken wanderer,
Who does not surrender his life and fate to Heaven and Earth?"*

He then left Li with a single pill of medicine, saying, *"Take this, and you shall enjoy a long life."* They parted at once. Li consumed the pill, and his hair never whitened, his teeth never fell out; he lived to one hundred and seven before passing away.

◇The Healing of Fistula in Jinling
Wan Yushi of Jinling once suffered from a fistula, leaving the left half of his body—hand and foot—paralyzed. For years, he walked with difficulty, and despite countless treatments, the condition remained incurable. One day, while venturing beyond the city’s southern gate, he encountered a Daoist arriving from Pude Mountain, who asked, *"What ails you so?"* Wan told him of his affliction.

The Daoist pressed his hand upon the afflicted area, and suddenly Wan felt a sharp, aching pain. The Daoist said, *"How could this be called a one-sided affliction? It shall soon heal of itself."* When asked his name and style, he replied, *"My surname is Qian, my style Xitun. I lodge at the Qingyuan Abbey."* He then spoke to Wan of the principles of Qian and Kun, Tun and Meng—of the positions of Heaven and Earth, ruler and teacher—all paths that mortals traverse.

Wan returned home, and his ailment vanished at once; he walked as before. He recounted the matter to his friends, who all said, *"Qian is yang; Xitun signifies purity. Could he not be the Pure Yang Immortal?"* Wan then went out again to seek him, but the Daoist was nowhere to be found. Only the image of the Pure Yang Immortal remained.

Recorded in the eleventh month of the *gengwu* year of the Longqing reign.

◇Poetic Inscriptions on Scenery

◇Poem on Yellow Crane Tower
One day, the prefect and his deputy of Wuchang were playing chess when a Daoist, who gave no name, stepped forward and said, *"I am a master of the game."* The prefect invited him to play, and after only eight moves, the Daoist declared, *"The prefect has lost."* The prefect said, *"The game is not yet finished—how do you know I have lost?"* The Daoist replied, *"My pieces have already seized the strategic points; thus I know."* And so it proved. After several games, the prefect lost every one.

Suddenly, he brushed his sleeves and departed, vanishing without a trace. The prefect ordered men to search the city for him and heard that he was playing the flute before the prefectural yamen. As soon as they arrived, the flute’s sound had shifted to the East Gate. When they reached the East Gate, it was heard at the West Gate; at the West Gate, it moved to the South Gate; at the South Gate, to the North Gate. Finally, at the North Gate, the sound came from before Yellow Crane Tower. The Daoist had fled into Stone Reflection Pavilion and disappeared, leaving only a poem in the pavilion that read:

*When the flute played before Yellow Crane Tower,*
*White duckweed and red smartweed filled the river isles.*
*To whom could I pour out my hidden feelings?*
*Only the clear wind and bright moon would know.*

At the end was written a single character: *Lü*.

◇ Karmic Encounters: Met Yet Unmet

◇ He Xiangu Meets a Daoist
He Xiangu was a Daoist maiden from Lingling Market. At thirteen, she followed her companions into the mountains to pick tea when she lost them and wandered, unable to find her way home. Below the eastern peak, she saw a man with a long beard and dark blue eyes, wearing a tall cap and a six-layered robe—it was Lü Dongbin. The maiden prostrated herself repeatedly in haste to bow. Dongbin took out a peach and said, *"You are young and must love fruit. Eat this whole, and one day you will ascend in flight; if not, you will remain earthbound."* Xiangu could only eat half. The bearded man pointed out her way home. When she returned, she thought only a day had passed, but in truth, a month had gone by. From then on, she neither hungered nor suffered bodily leaks, and she could foresee the fortunes and misfortunes of others. Later, she departed through *shijie*.

Dongbin once told Xiangu, *"I once wandered Hua Yin Market selling medicine, placing a single elixir among ten thousand other pills. When someone sought medicine, I would reach into the gourd and hand them whatever I grasped, observing their karmic affinity. For days, the ten thousand other pills were taken, but this elixir would fall from my hand the moment I touched it."* He sighed that in the world, those with the bone of immortality were so rare.

◇ A Daoist Friend Lectures on Scriptures
Chen Danran was a wealthy Confucian scholar who longed for the Dao. For years, he invited wandering Daoists to stay, yet never met one who could teach him. Dongbin disguised himself as a laborer, tending Chen’s garden for over a year. His strength in work surpassed that of ordinary men. Chen grew fond of him but treated him only as a hired hand.

One day, Chen and a Daoist friend were discussing the *Yinfu Jing*, reaching the line *"When Heaven unleashes its killing mechanism, Heaven and Earth reverse."* They did not understand the meaning of the "killing mechanism." Dongbin, standing nearby, spoke out boldly: *"The living do not live; the dead do not die. To kill the living after it has been born, or to learn death before it has come—this is the way to long life."* Chen was astonished and asked, *"Are you not a laborer? Who taught you such words?"* Then, muttering incoherently, he spoke in riddles that could not be deciphered.

The Daoist friend said, *"Where would a rustic field hand have stolen such words? He does not truly understand them."* Soon after, Dongbin suddenly took his leave, saying, *"I will travel far. I shall return at noon on the fifth day of the fifth month next year."* After he left, all was silent. Later, a villager traveling in Baling encountered him and said, *"Give this message to Master Chen: I am Lü Dongbin. At first, I thought he could be taught the Dao, but upon closer observation, he did not awaken. I will not return."* With that, he walked into the bamboo grove of Lüxian Pavilion and vanished.

On the noon of the fifth day of the fifth month the following year, Chen died suddenly.

◇ The Shaoxing Daoist Assembly
At the Shaoxing *guichou* Daoist assembly on Mount Kuaiji, a Daoist arrived carrying a bamboo hat. When the gathering dispersed, he hung the hat on the wall—though there was nothing to hang it on, it did not fall. He left a poem:

*By chance I rode the Azure Emperor from Penglai,*
*My sword and halberd gleamed across the nine heavens.*
*I stand before you now, yet none recognize me—*
*I leave this hat behind; do not let it sink into obscurity.*

◇ The Patriarch’s Admonition to the World
A speck of goodness—offer others convenience.
A speck of evil—refrain from doing it.
Clothing and food follow fate; joy comes naturally.
Why calculate destiny?
Why consult divination?
Deceiving others brings misfortune; forgiving others brings blessing.
The Heavenly Eye watches clearly; retribution comes swiftly.
Listen well to my words—gods bow, ghosts submit.

*The Peach Blossom Maiden*, also known by its full titles *The Peach Blossom Maiden’s Contest of Yin and Yang* and *The Illustrated Wondrous Book of the Peach Blossom Maiden’s Duel of Dao*, comprises four volumes and sixteen chapters.

Form cannot depart from shadow, nor shadow from form; yang cannot turn from yin, nor yin from yang. Yet if form severs from shadow, it must perish; if yang turns from yin, discord must arise. Thus, this book is titled *Contest of Yin and Yang*—for yang has turned from yin. When yin and yang fall into discord, how can they not contend?

Nevertheless, it is said that form never truly parts from shadow, nor yang from yin. Therefore, though yin and yang may contend at first, in the end they neither turn from nor oppose each other. When they neither turn from nor oppose, their contention ceases. When yin and yang no longer contend, they harmonize as one—entering the realm beyond the ordinary, ascending to sagehood.

Once yin and yang transcend the mundane and attain sagehood, they remain unstained by worldly dust. Unstained by worldly dust, all illusory realms dissolve. This is the grand resolution of *Contest of Yin and Yang*.

Thus, this preface is written.

Newly carved in the first winter month of the wushen year of the Daoguang reign.
Volume One.

**Chapter One**
*The Demon-Quelling Mountain’s Precept Blade Takes Form*
*The Recluse of Zhaoge Offers Divination*

In the age of the Three Sovereigns, there ruled in the northern realm of Julu a king named Jing Le, whose consort, Lady Shan Sheng, carried a child for fourteen months before giving birth to a prince—an incarnation of the Azure Emperor.

In time, the prince grew to manhood, renounced his kingdom, and cultivated the Dao, attaining true fruition. In heaven, he became the Jade Pivot, Celestial Worthy of the Northern Culmen, Master of Teachings; in the middle realm, the Supreme Sage Who Quells Demons; and below, the Perfected Martial and Mysterious Heavenly Thearch.

Once, while refining his Dao upon Snow Mountain, he used a precept blade to slit open his belly and wash his intestines. Overcome by the ordeal, he fell into a stupor and cast the blade aside. When immortals later revived him, he forgot to retrieve it. Later, while cultivating truth in Yuanxuan Cave, he found the blade missing and left its sheath behind as a treasure to guard the realm.

Both blade and sheath were gifts from the Azure Emperor to the Thearch—wondrous treasures of free will. For a full hundred years, they absorbed the essence of sun and moon before finally transforming into form. The precept blade refined into a yang body, the sheath into a yin body. The blade concealed itself within Demon-Quelling Mountain to cultivate truth, while the sheath nurtured its nature in Yuanxuan Cave.

After several centuries, the Queen Mother of the Western Pool summoned the sheath to heaven, entrusting it with the governance of the Peach Orchard and bestowing upon it the name Peach Blossom Immortal. The blade, however, had not yet attained true fruition. Harboring resentment, it stirred up mischief in Demon-Quelling Mountain—sometimes spewing flames to rival the sun and moon, other times conjuring clouds and rain without cause. This provoked the wrath of heaven, and celestial soldiers were dispatched to seize the blade and bring it to the Golden Palace, where it was sentenced to execution upon the Demon-Slaying Platform.

Fortunately, the founder of the Daoist teachings, the Most High Lord Lao, took pity on its millennia of arduous cultivation. He interceded at the Golden Palace and took the blade to the Tushita Heaven, where it became a youth attendant tasked with tending the divination box. There, it stole a glimpse of *The True Canon of Heavenly Stems* and secretly descended to the mortal realm.

Its true spirit unobscured, it incarnated into the household of a nobleman of the Shang dynasty, surnamed Zhou and named Qing, who held the office of Grand Minister. He took a wife of the Feng clan, and at the age of fifty, she bore him a son. Before the child’s birth, Lady Feng dreamed of a room ablaze with radiant light. Upon waking, she gave birth to a prince, whom they named Zhou Qian.

His face was dark as a cauldron’s bottom, his brows sharp as swords, and from infancy, his eyes shone with divine light. At seven, while playing in the garden, a celestial being descended from the sky and bestowed upon him a book of heavenly scripture. Possessing deep karmic roots, he comprehended it at a glance and gained mastery over past and future, the summoning of spirits, and the arts of cloud-riding and mist-traversing.

By thirty, both his parents had passed, and Zhou Qian inherited his father’s office. The world called him the Duke of Zhou, and his integrity won the respect of all officials. Yet seeing the Shang king’s waywardness, he repeatedly submitted memorials of remonstrance, only for the king to ignore them. Disheartened, he grew despondent.

One day after court, sitting alone in his estate, he mused: *“If I cannot guide the king to righteousness nor sacrifice myself for the realm, how can I remain among the common herd? Why not resign my post, withdraw to Zhaoge, and find a secluded place to open a divination stall, guiding the people? Let me become a leader in expounding the primordial, a master in elucidating the Eight Trigrams. Though I may not serve the nation or its people, my name shall endure for ten thousand generations—is this not good?”*

His resolve firm, he composed his resignation memorial by lamplight. At the fifth watch, he attended court, stepped forward from the ranks, and presented it to the throne. The Shang king, weary of his forthright admonitions, was overjoyed at his departure and approved his request.

Zhou Qian hastened home, gathered his valuables, locked his estate, and departed with his family for a quiet and refined dwelling in Zhaoge. There, he found himself carefree and unbound, at perfect ease.

A poem bears witness:
*“Men deem officialdom the world’s great marvel,*
*Yet I know reclusion holds heaven’s design.”*

With mist-wreathed mountains as his companions, he knew no fear—no longer did he endure the ceaseless toil of mind and heart by day and night.

After some ten days of leisure, Zhou Gong summoned an elderly steward. This man, named Jian Pengjian, had once served as a retainer to a senior minister. Honest and without deceit, he had followed Zhou Gong into reclusion, content to draw water and tend vegetables. Hearing his master’s call, he hastened to the great hall, bowed, and asked, “My lord, what task do you have for Pengjian?”

Zhou Gong replied, “Since I abandoned office to dwell in seclusion here, I have failed to serve the nation or its people, forsaking the legacy of my ancestors. Now, I wish to forge a new path and become a man of uncommon purpose. I intend to establish an enterprise here. You shall open a side gate on the left of the main entrance, seal off three adjoining chambers, and prepare them with utmost cleanliness. Place a seat within—make haste, for this must be done swiftly!”

Pengjian laughed and said, “My lord, I have never seen a noble lord or high minister take up trade or craft.”

Zhou Gong smiled in return. “I do not mean to engage in trade or craft. I shall open a divination stall to guide the foolish, that they may refrain from wickedness. Yet I fear crowds may disturb my peace, so I have devised a method: each divination shall cost one tael of silver. First, the seeker must give you the silver, and only then may they enter to see me and have their fortune told. No more than ten consultations a day—any beyond that shall not be entertained. If someone comes, they must first give you three *fen* of silver as a fee for your service. What say you to this?”

Upon hearing this, Pengjian stood by in silence, only lowering his head with a faint smile. Zhou Gong asked, “Why do you not speak a word?”

Pengjian replied with a laugh, “It is not that I refuse to answer, but how could Your Lordship, a man second only to the sovereign and above ten thousand others, stoop to such a base endeavor? First, it would diminish your noble stature; second, it might sow confusion among the people; and third, no one would dare approach for a divination. Moreover, the fee is far too steep—why go through this charade at all?”

Zhou Gong said, “You do not understand my intent. How does expounding the primordial Dao diminish my stature? How does guiding the foolish confuse the masses? I fear only that my divinations may lack efficacy—if they prove true, the seekers will trample one another to reach my door. Do not concern yourself; go and see to the preparations!”

With no choice, Pengjian had no option but to summon craftsmen to begin the work. Within days, all was ready, and he returned to report to Zhou Gong, “The divination stall is complete.” He then smiled and added, “Though Your Lordship demands a tael of silver per divination, if the readings are accurate, the fee will be justified. But if they are not, will you not be ridiculed? They will say Your Lordship is no good, extorting silver from the people with some trick to deceive them.”

Zhou Gong laughed and said, “I have my reasons—if a divination proves false, I shall repay tenfold!”

Pengjian exclaimed, “That will not do! Your Lordship may afford to repay tenfold, but the three *fen* I receive will be difficult to repay at such a rate. Please do not make sport of me!”

Zhou Gong smiled again. “You do not yet know the divine power of my Eight Trigrams—they are never the slightest bit in error. Even your share shall be repaid by me. What say you now?”

Upon hearing this, Pengjian was overjoyed. He promptly kowtowed, then rose to his feet. Zhou Gong ordered a large bamboo tablet to be brought, took up the brush himself, and inscribed in bold characters: **"The Principles of the Trigrams Communicate with the Divine."** On the left, he wrote in smaller script: *"Foreknowing life and death, fortune and misfortune,"* and on the right: *"Divination fee: one tael of silver; messenger’s commission: three fen."*

He then took another large bamboo tablet and wrote: *"Those seeking divination must arrive in the morning for guidance on fortune or misfortune. Only ten divinations are permitted each day; none after noon. Should the reading prove inaccurate, a penalty of ten taels and three mace of silver shall be paid!"* Having finished, he instructed Pengjian to sit at the crossroads of the main street, awaiting those who came to consult the oracle.

This spectacle caused a sensation among the people of Chaoge. Whispers spread from one to ten, ten to a hundred, until the entire city buzzed with talk: *"How extraordinary! Never has a nobleman of such high standing abandoned his rank to take up divination as a trade. And for a tael of silver at that! Who knows if it’s even accurate?"* Some wished to test it, but the fee was too steep, and so they all stood by, hands tucked in their sleeves.

Zhou Gong remained there all day, impeccably dressed in full court attire, seated in the center of the divination stall. He required no attendants, only a censer of fine incense burning before him as he sat in quiet dignity. Pengjian, naturally, sat alone inside the entrance.

For two or three days, not a single person came to consult the oracle. Only crowds of idlers gathered, chattering endlessly. Among them, a local bully declared, *"This nobleman has a sense of humor—I’ll play along. I’ll part with one tael and three fen to test him."* Another, a soldier, said, *"Yesterday, I came into some wealth but forgot something important and can’t decide what to do. I’ll go have it divined!"* And so, the two entered one after the other.

Only because the Duke held a noble rank, who dared sit idly chatting with him? Without waiting to be told, the local bully handed over the one tael and three fen to Pengjian. Pengjian took the silver, chuckling inwardly, *"How amusing—today brings good fortune!"* He set the one tael before Zhou Gong’s seat and reported to him. Zhou Gong then instructed him to bring the first man in. The bully followed him inside.

Zhou Gong said, *"You need not bow, nor explain your matter. Simply stand to the side and pray silently."* The bully did as told, whispering his plea. After a moment, Zhou Gong said, *"I understand your heart. You desire the wife of one of your servants for her beauty. You wish to break them apart, forcing the husband to remarry, but he refuses. Now you intend to have him killed—is this not so? Yet I fear you will fail to kill him and instead bring death upon yourself!"*

Hearing his secret exposed, the bully was struck dumb, his face ashen. He fell to his knees, pleading, *"My lord! It is true. I beg you to show me the way—how may I avoid disaster?"* Zhou Gong nodded. *"Since you repent, there is still hope. Had you not met me, you would surely die tomorrow!"* He took up a sheet of paper, wrote several lines, and handed it to the bully. *"This concerns your heart’s desire—"*

The bully took it and read:

*Lusting to seize a youthful wife’s embrace,*
*Little did you know the trap was laid.*
*Tomorrow at the third watch, in your disgrace,*
*Two corpses shall lie in four parts displayed.*

Zhou Gong continued, *"The woman’s husband has already stolen from you and bribed men. Tomorrow, when she meets you, they will seize and kill you both. If you seek my salvation, you must renounce your desire for her, turn to virtue, and Heaven will protect you, turning misfortune to blessing. Here is your proof: tonight at the third watch, go east thirty li. You will see a lantern hanging at a door—knock and enter. There, you will meet your adversary. Invite him home for wine, and with mediation, all will be well by dawn."*

The bully kowtowed in gratitude, then rushed out. The crowd, seeing him drenched in sweat and fleeing, swarmed forward, blocking his path. *"Was the divination accurate?"* they demanded. Without stopping, he shoved them aside and declared, *"Truly, truly—it is most divine!"* Then he vanished in a flash.

The soldier had already approached the table. Following instructions, he too prayed silently. Zhou Gong rendered his judgment in a few lines and passed it to him. It read:

*Ten strings of cash you took in greed,*
*To catch adulterers in their deed.*
*Ill-gotten wealth—let it go,*
*Lest tangled passions bring you woe.*

Zhou Gong then called out, *"Soldier, yesterday someone gave you several dozen strings of cash, promising ten more if you helped catch adulterers at the third watch tomorrow—is this your concern?"* The soldier, trembling, prostrated himself. *"My lord, you are a living immortal!"*

The soldier replied, *"This humble one came precisely for this matter."* Zhou Gong smiled and said, *"Do not take this sum of money. If you help that man catch the adulterer and succeed, his resentment will be dispelled—why would he then give you the money? If you fail to catch the adulterer, how could he willingly part with his wealth? I will now show you a path: go meet that man as planned, and after you have met, present this divination slip of mine to them. Naturally, someone will give you ten strings of cash. From this day forward, abandon such reckless thoughts, and you will avoid disaster."*

The soldier, upon hearing this, hastily kowtowed and said, *"With my lord’s clear guidance, this humble one will never again entertain such improper desires!"* He then took his leave, refusing to disclose the matter to anyone, only exclaiming, *"Truly divine, a living immortal! If any of you doubt it, go and test it yourselves."* Hurriedly departing the divination market, he rushed away.

Unbeknownst to them, both the local tyrant and the soldier had come to believe in the divine accuracy of Master Zhou’s words. When they met, they discovered they had taken the same path: the one the soldier had been ordered to catch in the act was none other than the tyrant’s own servant. That night, their encounter left both astounded and overjoyed, fully convinced of Master Zhou’s oracular prowess. The tyrant invited everyone back to his home, where the soldier helped mediate between the two, producing Master Zhou’s divination slip for all to see. Only then was the knot of grievance untangled. The tyrant also gave the soldier ten strings of cash.

Word of these two cases spread through Zhaoge, and soon the entire city was abuzz. Those with unresolved disputes flocked to seek his judgments, crowding the divination market beyond capacity. Every ruling he issued proved accurate—each single verdict correct, every pair of rulings flawless. Day after day, he would settle exactly ten cases before closing his gate, paying no heed to those still waiting outside.

Peng Jian was overjoyed—earning three qian of silver daily, rain or shine. Childless and alone, he would rise each morning to resolve his ten cases. Once the sign was taken down and stored away, he would head straight to the taverns along the streets, determined to spend every last qian of his three. If any silver remained, he would give it to the poor who passed by. Months passed in this manner, and over half a year went by.

Yet even in such prosperity, a flaw lurked—trouble was about to unfold before his eyes.

Hear me now, good listeners, as I tell the tale in full.

In the city of Chaoge there lived a widow named Shi, whose husband had died years before. She had but one son, named Shi Zongfu, and though their household was poor, she had scraped together a few taels of silver. She sent her son to Mengjin to try his hand at trade, and as they parted, mother and son agreed he would return within three months. Yet half a year passed, and no word came. The widow Shi thought of her son day and night, standing at her gate in hope, only to watch the days slip by with no sign of him. She sought the counsel of gods and diviners, but all proved empty words, and her heart grew heavy with sorrow.

One day, as she stood at her door, she overheard passersby say: *"In Qiyun Lane, there is a Master Zhou who tells fortunes—his divinations are wondrously accurate. Only his fee is steep: one tael and three fen of silver!"* These words stirred her heart. *"Why not go and ask?"* she thought. She borrowed the sum from her neighbors, rose before dawn, washed and dressed, ate a light meal, bound her hair with black silk, locked her door, and set off for Master Zhou’s divination stall.

She arrived just as dawn broke. By chance, Peng Jian was opening his gate, hanging his sign, and sweeping the ground. The widow Shi recognized him and called out: *"Master Peng! Is the master in?"* Peng Jian looked up, saw that it was the widow Shi—once his neighbor in the same ward—and replied: *"Sister, you’ve come so early—you must have a matter at hand. Do you seek a divination?"* At his words, the widow Shi’s eyes brimmed with tears. *"Indeed."*

"My son, Shi Zongfu, left home to pursue trade abroad. Before departing, he promised to return within three months. Now half a year has passed, yet no word has come. I cannot set my heart at ease, so I borrowed one tael and three *fen* of silver to ask the master for a divination—whether he fares well, whether he lives or has perished. Then, at least, I may cease this endless fretting." As she spoke, she handed the silver to Peng Jian.

Peng Jian took it and said, "Rest assured, good sister! Heaven watches over the virtuous—your son is safe abroad. Perhaps he has merely been delayed. Since you, as his mother, cannot set your mind at ease without a divination, I shall take you inside." With that, he led the widow into the inner hall.

The widow Shi looked up and saw a table set in the center, upon which lay the Four Treasures of the Study, a divination tube, an incense altar, and the like. Seated in the middle was the master himself, his bearing unlike that of ordinary men—what an imposing presence! Behold:

A three-beamed crown adorned his head, its eight treasures clustered close; he wore a black silk robe, upon which coiled embroidered dragons. His face was dark as a cauldron’s bottom, yet gleamed; his eyes like stars, their pupils radiating light. Seated upright, he arrayed the Eight Trigrams before him—no less than a transcendent spirit in mortal guise.

At the sight of the Duke of Zhou’s majestic bearing, the widow Shi could not help but kneel at once. The Duke, seated above, saw an elderly woman enter—her face etched with sorrow, hurrying forward until she knelt in the center of the hall. His expression soured. Why? That very morning, upon taking his seat, he had cast a divination and found yin malevolence (*yin sha*) overwhelmingly strong. He had just been about to summon Peng Jian to instruct him: *No divination fees from women today.* Yet here, the first visitor brought before him was a woman. His displeasure showed as he said, "Rise."

Then he turned to Peng Jian: "Why did you not announce her before bringing someone in for divination?" Peng Jian replied, "This is the wife of Shi Du, Jia-shi. Her late husband and I were acquainted. Today, she comes to inquire about her son’s return—hence my failure to announce her first."

The widow Shi, hearing this, spoke through tears: "This old woman’s only son, Shi Zongfu, went abroad on business and has not returned for half a year. With no word of his life or death, my heart is consumed with worry. I know I have broken protocol and beg only for your mercy and compassion, Master!"

The Duke of Zhou nodded and said, "Very well. Are you asking about a traveler, then?"

"Let me divine a hexagram for you." He then took up the divination tube, shook it twice, and cast a hexagram. After calculating the branches—zi, wu, mao, and you—he sighed as he looked at the widow Shi: "If I did not speak plainly, would I not leave you vainly hoping? Your son’s life will end at the third watch tonight!"

Hearing this, the widow Shi started in shock and cried, "My lord! I asked when he would return—how can you say he is about to die?" The Duke of Zhou replied, "My divination aligns with the primordial yin and yang and the later heaven’s eight trigrams. Every detail, down to the finest hair, is revealed therein—how much more so the matter of your son’s life? Your son has indeed set out on his journey, but you and he may never meet again—even a ten-thousandth chance is impossible!"

The widow Shi burst into sobs: "If my son dies tonight, where will he die? What illness will claim him?" The Duke of Zhou answered, "I foresee your son crushed to death in a ruined kiln at the third watch tonight."

Seeing that the Duke’s words were as clear as if he had witnessed it himself, the widow’s heart grew even more desolate. She kowtowed repeatedly, begging the lord to save her son. The Duke of Zhou sighed, "Very well. Tell me your son’s Eight Characters and birth hour, and I will examine his annual fate."

The widow Shi hastily recited her son’s details—born on the eighteenth day of the twelfth month at the chou hour, now fourteen years old. The Duke of Zhou put away the divination box, arranged Shi Zongfu’s Eight Characters, and upon seeing them, cried out, "Alas! A baleful star looms over him, the White Tiger guards his fate—even an immortal could not pass through this gate! There is not a single saving grace in his destiny. What can be done?"

Stone Widow, compose your heart now and cease longing for him." Truly:
When King Yama decrees death at the third watch,
Who dares detain a soul till the fifth?

Seeing the Duke of Zhou declare her son beyond saving, the widow Shi could only wail aloud, her grief piercing as she left the divination market and made her way home. What fate befell her son? The answer lies in the next chapter.

Chapter Three: Soft Creature Born in Heaven’s Wrath | A Wise Maiden Perceives the Dao’s Mystery

We leave the widow Shi weeping her way home. Meanwhile, in the Tushita Palace of the Thirty-Third Heaven, the Most High Lord Lao sat in meditation upon his rush cushion when suddenly the Golden Youth came to report: "The boy who tends the divination box has vanished—gone no one knows where, and still he has not returned." Upon hearing this, Lord Lao extended his divine vision and soon understood the cause. He nodded and said, "Ah, wretched obstacle! Instead of nurturing stillness to cultivate the Dao and attain true fruition, you stir recklessly today, bringing suffering upon yourself."

"Not that I lack the compassion to save you!" With these words, he went to memorialize the August Heavenly Emperor. The Emperor then commanded the Peach Garden Immortal to descend to the mortal realm. The immortal, bearing the Jade Edict, followed a wisp of numinous light to the household of Minister Ren in the city of Zhaoge, for they shared a karmic bond. There, she was reborn as the minister’s daughter.

Now grown to sixteen, her face bloomed like peach blossoms, her frame swayed like tender willow—beyond compare in beauty. How so? A poem bears witness:

Her lips like cherries, teeth of jade,
She alone among mortals speaks the language of flowers.
Only by predestined bond was such a seed sown—
How rare for a celestial maiden to grace a mortal home!

When Minister Ren and his wife first beheld her, they dreamed of a sky ablaze with iridescent clouds, from which descended an immortal bearing a radiant peach blossom in hand. She placed it in the lady’s grasp, and with its fragrance still lingering, the dream dissolved. Three days later, the child was born, and so they named her Peach Blossom.

The elderly couple had no other daughter, cherishing her as a pearl cupped in their palms, raising her with every tenderness. But of this, no more need be said.

One day, as the couple sat together in their hall, they heard a clamor in the streets—wailing so mournful it chilled the heart. Minister Ren hurried out to see and recognized the widow Shi from next door. Tears streamed down her face as she sobbed uncontrollably, while the crowd around her offered words of comfort, though their murmurs were too tangled to make out. Perplexed, he stepped forward and called through the throng, “Good sister, what grieves you so? Come, take shelter in our home and share your sorrow. Perhaps this old man can offer some solace.”

With that, he ushered Widow Shi inside. The onlookers, seeing her led into the Ren residence, dispersed in a flurry. Minister Ren guided her through the main gate, where his wife came out to meet them. Seeing the widow’s distress, she rushed forward to support her, and together they entered the central hall and sat down. “Good sister,” Lady Ren asked gently, “who has wronged you? What quarrel has left you so aggrieved?”

Wiping her tears, the widow replied, “My lady, it is no quarrel—my son died at the third watch tonight. Who now will sustain this old life of mine?” At this, the couple gasped in shock. “Has word of misfortune reached you?” they asked.

The widow waved her hands frantically. “No, no. My boy left home saying he’d return within three months, but now half a year has passed with no news. Unable to bear the worry, I consulted the oracle this morning, and the hexagrams foretold great misfortune—tonight at the third watch, he would be crushed to death in an abandoned kiln. How could I not grieve?”

Minister Ren burst into laughter. “Good sister, I thought some calamity had befallen him! But this is only an ill-omened divination. Why torment yourself so? The fortune-teller is no living immortal—how could he know such things with certainty?”

The widow replied, “Had it been anyone else, I wouldn’t believe it. But this was divined by Lord Zhou. His judgments of yin and yang are infallible, his predictions of fortune and misfortune never err. What he says comes to pass. I pleaded with him, and he declared, *‘Unless you beg the King of Hell himself, I fear there is no hope of his survival.’*”

Minister Ren paused, taken aback. “I’ve heard Lord Zhou’s divinations are truly divine. If he says so, perhaps there is no saving your son. But sister, if he knows your boy will die in an abandoned kiln, why not ask him *which* kiln? Send someone at once to stop him from entering—then all may yet be well.”

Lady Ren interjected, “You’re growing old and forget how the world works! Lord Zhou is no living immortal—he merely interprets the hexagrams. How could he pinpoint *which* kiln? It seems there is no remedy after all.”

The stone-matron, hearing Lady Ren’s words, grew even more grieved and could no longer contain herself, bursting into loud sobs. Lord and Lady Ren, seeing her so distraught, reflected that she had but one son, and her household was poor and struggling—if he truly died, upon whom would these aged bones rely for care and compassion? At this thought, they too could not help but shed tears.

The narrative now turns elsewhere. As for Miss Peach Blossom, ever since the age of five, while playing outside with her maidservants, she had encountered a mendicant Daoist who gave her three volumes of celestial scripture and a pellet of elixir to consume. After eating it, she found herself able to recognize characters. Each night in her dreams, the Daoist would return to instruct her in their interpretation—truly teaching her for several months in her sleep, until at last she no longer dreamed of him. Having acquired immortal arts, she mastered the three volumes of celestial scripture within a year.

By the time she reached sixteen, she had still not told her parents, nor did she readily show herself to others. As Miss Peach Blossom had always loved peach blossoms, Lord Ren had planted several hundred peach trees in the rear garden for her to admire morning and evening. Each day, she tended to the peach trees in the garden or did some needlework. That morning, after finishing breakfast and putting away her sewing, she was about to go to the peach garden when she suddenly heard ceaseless wailing from the main hall, the voice sounding like that of a woman. She thought to herself, *“Why is someone weeping in the hall today? Let me go and see.”* With delicate steps, she made her way to the main hall.

There, she saw her parents sitting with the stone-matron from next door, all of them in tears. Startled, she approached and offered a deep bow. Seeing Miss Peach Blossom, the stone-matron suppressed her sorrow and said, *“Miss, how rare it is to see you appear before others! It’s been years since I last saw your countenance—now you’ve grown even more lovely.”* Lord and Lady Ren, seeing their daughter approach, wiped their tears and said, *“Daughter, come sit beside us.”*

Miss Peach Blossom then asked, *“Father, Mother, why are you weeping here with Auntie Stone?”* Lady Ren quickly replied, *“Daughter, you wouldn’t know. Auntie Stone’s son went away on business, and after half a year with no word, she went to Lord Zhou to divine when he might return. Unexpectedly, Lord Zhou, interpreting the hexagrams, declared that her son would surely die at the third watch tonight—with no hope of salvation. That’s why she is so heartbroken. Your father and I were trying to console her, but it stirred our own sorrow over having no son, and so we wept as well.”*

Miss Peach Blossom, upon hearing this, sighed and said, *"What great matter is this, to cause such distress? It’s only this!"* Turning to Auntie Stone, she continued, *"Do not grieve so, Auntie. If your brother is fated to die, no amount of weeping will bring him back to life. That Lord Zhou is not necessarily so divinely accurate in his calculations. Tell me your brother’s Eight Characters, and I’ll divine for you. Let me see whether his fate truly allows for salvation or not."*

Lord and Lady Ren, hearing this, immediately said, *"Daughter, do not tease Auntie. When have you ever learned to cast divinations?"* Miss Peach Blossom replied, *"Father and Mother, you do not know—I’ve only just learned. Auntie, go ahead and tell me. What harm is there in letting me try?"* With no other choice, Auntie Stone recited her son’s Eight Characters to Miss Peach Blossom.

Miss Peach Blossom extended her delicate fingers, counting and calculating for a moment. Soon, life and death, fortune and misfortune became perfectly clear to her. Nodding repeatedly, she said, *"What a remarkable Lord Zhou! His divination is entirely accurate—no wonder all of Chaoge City holds him in such reverence. Indeed, at the third watch tonight, your brother will surely be crushed to death beneath a ruined kiln. The White Tiger star dominates his fate, the Gate of Death guards his life, and both the yearly and monthly influences are inauspicious. There is no doubt he will die. Judging by the direction, it will be fifteen *li* south of the city, where a collapsed kiln stands. Tomorrow, his body will be found there."*

Auntie Stone, upon hearing this, began weeping again. Lord Ren quickly consoled her, laughing, *"Sister, do not take the words of a child so seriously. If we know the direction, I’ll send a servant at once to save your son. Why distress yourself so? But my daughter’s words cannot be trusted."* Miss Peach Blossom smiled and said, *"Can human effort defy Heaven? If you and Auntie cannot believe me, so be it. But the hour has just entered *wei*, and by the beginning of *shen*, a great storm will arrive. If there is no wind or rain, then my words are empty nonsense. If the storm comes, Auntie, hurry back, and I will teach you a method to save him."*

With that, she bid them farewell and returned straight to the peach garden. Lord Ren, hearing his daughter’s words, could not help but laugh heartily. *"Wife,"* he said, *"look at this clear sky—where is the rain? Sister, do not grieve so. Even if your son were to die, weeping would not bring him back. In my opinion, return home and put your mind at ease. Tomorrow, I’ll send someone to inquire after him."*

With no other recourse, Auntie Stone took her leave and returned home, lost in anxious thoughts. As the *shen* hour arrived, the weather suddenly changed: in an instant, a fierce storm erupted, with torrential rain and howling winds. Auntie Stone, seeing the heavens unleash such a tempest, was startled. *"Indeed, the rain has come! This proves Miss Peach Blossom’s divination is accurate. She said there was a way to save my son—why not go and beg her for it? Perhaps she truly has a method to rescue my child."*

Braving the downpour, she hurried to Squire Ren’s gate and knocked to be let in. At that moment, Squire Ren and his wife were in the main hall, marveling at their daughter’s divination—how uncannily accurate it had proven—and wondering how she had acquired such mystical arts. As they spoke, they saw Auntie Stone arrive through the rain and immediately understood she had come on account of her son. How she might secure his rescue would be revealed in the next chapter.

**Chapter Four**
*Auntie Stone Pleads for Her Orphaned Son*
*Miss Peach Blossom Teaches a Strange Art*

Auntie Stone entered the central hall, where Squire Ren and his wife rose to greet her, and they all took their seats. Serving girls attended to her needs. Without delay, Auntie Stone adjusted her robes, dropped to her knees in haste, and implored, *"Master, Mistress, you must save my son! My gratitude will know no bounds!"*

Squire Ren and his wife quickly helped her to her feet, saying, *"Old sister, have you seen the rain outside? This was merely our daughter’s idle guess—please, rise."* Once she stood, Auntie Stone pressed on: *"Master, Mistress, if your daughter’s words were mere nonsense, how could they have come to pass so precisely? I beg you both to summon her at once. Any further delay, and I fear it will be too late to save him."* With that, tears streamed down her face.

Unable to refuse, Squire Ren called for a servant girl to fetch Miss Peach Blossom. The moment Auntie Stone saw her, she cried, *"Miss, have mercy! Save my son’s life!"* and knelt once more. Mistress Ren stepped forward to lift her up, saying, *"How can this be?"* Then, turning to her daughter, she urged, *"Child, if there is any way you can save him, do so."*

Miss Peach Blossom motioned for her parents and Auntie Stone to sit, then said, *"There is indeed a method to save him, but you must never reveal my name to anyone. You must not say I saved your son—if Duke Zhou learns of it, he will never let the matter rest. He will come for me, and our families will become entangled in bitter enmity. Would that not turn your kindness into vengeance?"*

Auntie Stone replied, *"Miss, rest assured! How could I be so ungrateful? I would never breathe a word of your name."*

Peach Blossom said, *"Since that is so, Auntie, listen to what I say: according to the divination of the Eight Trigrams, your son is fated to die without hope of life. Yet I possess a kind of immortal art that can revive the dead and reverse his fate by breaking the Eight Trigrams. If you do not trust in this spiritual power, there will be no way to save him!"*

Auntie Stone, overjoyed upon hearing this, said, *"Miss, how do you perform this method? Tell me quickly so I may act at once!"*

Peach Blossom replied, *"There is no need for anything else. You must go and purchase a paper horse for the Star Lord of the Local Earth God and another for the Fire Virtue Foundation Lord. Place them in the room as offerings, with two candles lit beside them. You need only set out a bowl of pure water and an egg beneath the table. Then, invert a sieve and place a lamp beneath it—this is called the 'Lamp of Added Lifespan,' and you must never blow it out. If it goes out, your son will be beyond saving. Observe whether the wind and rain continue or cease tonight. If they cease and the sky clears as before, at that time, find an old shoe and an old garment with a lining. Place a mirror atop them and set them in the bowl of water. You must then take the old shoe in hand and sit in the room. Be sure to step outside the main gate, strike the threshold with the shoe, and call your son’s name once before returning. Do this once for every watch of the night. If you call past the third watch, you may rest easy and go to sleep. Tomorrow morning, I assure you, your son will return home, and mother and child will be reunited."*

Just so:

The maiden’s wondrous method none could know,
Yet from primordial Heaven’s secret scrolls it came.

That day, Old Woman Shi carefully took her leave of Squire Ren and his wife and hurried home. The storm still raged, and though the couple had heard their daughter describe countless techniques, they remained unconvinced. Together they asked, “Dear child, life and death are decreed by Heaven above. If Shi Zongfu is fated to perish tonight at the third watch in the ruined kiln south of the city, how can you have his mother weep through half the night and promise his return by dawn, reuniting mother and son? We find these words hard to believe.”

Seeing her parents’ doubt, the young lady dared not reveal the truth beforehand, lest she leak Heaven’s design. She merely replied, “It is not yet time to explain. Wait until tomorrow, and I will tell you both.” Satisfied with this answer, the elder Rens pressed no further. The young lady took leave of her parents and retired to the Peach Garden to rest.

As for Shi Zongfu—when he left home in the ninth month of the previous year, he had promised to return within three months. Yet on the road he met a cloth merchant and joined him in a venture to Mengjin. The journey proved so profitable that he lost track of time. With two ledgers of capital, he had earned threefold in interest. By the end of the second month, he longed to return, knowing his mother awaited him. He bid his companion farewell, packed his belongings, and set off posthaste for Chaoge. Along the way, he ate when hungry, drank when thirsty, traveled by moonlight and starlight, and wished he could fly home in a single step to embrace his mother. After several days of travel, on the fifteenth day of the third month, Shi Zongfu left his inn. Calculating the distance, he found himself no more than a hundred and fifty or sixty li from Chaoge. “If I push on today,” he thought, “I can reach the city.” With this in mind, he quickened his pace, head bowed in thought, striding swiftly forward without a glance at the passing scenery.

Just as he reached the hour of *shen*, dark clouds suddenly gathered on all sides. Shi Zongfu groaned inwardly, "The rain is coming—how can I possibly cover these last few dozen *li* and reach the city in time?" His thoughts grew tangled, his anxiety mounting with each step, yet his pace slowed all the same. After hurrying several *li*, sweat drenched his body. A fierce wind lashed his face, and in an instant, the downpour struck like lightning, pouring down in torrents. Shi Zongfu knew there were no villages ahead nor dwellings behind—nowhere to take shelter. Though he had rain gear, the gale and deluge rendered it useless, leaving him no choice but to press onward through the storm. Each step became a struggle. As he walked, he scanned his surroundings, desperate for some refuge to weather the rain, if only for a moment.

Ahead, he spotted a crumbling kiln. Though dilapidated, it would suffice for shelter. He set down his belongings, peeled off his sodden clothes, wrung them out, and—finding nowhere to hang them—draped them back over his body. Sinking to the ground, he sighed repeatedly: *"How infuriating! The more I rush, the more fate thwarts me. Truly, the heavens rain without regard for man."* Glancing at the darkening sky, the rain still unrelenting, he realized he would not reach the city that day. With no other recourse, he resigned himself to spending the night in the ruined kiln, alone and forlorn, waiting for dawn to make his way into the city.

He consoled himself: *"Must I absolutely enter the city today? After all these days endured, can I not bear one more night?"* Turning the thought over, his mind gradually settled. Leaning against the wall, he closed his eyes to rest. *(The rest is left untold.)*

Meanwhile, the Stone Crone, half-believing and half-doubting Peach Blossom Girl’s words, braved the rain to purchase two paper horses bearing the Star Lords’ insignia and returned home. A live chicken was at hand, and she took one. As dusk deepened, the rain gradually cleared, and her heart settled somewhat. Seeing Peach Blossom Girl’s words prove true, she thought, *"Surely my child has hope now!"* Another moment passed, and the sky indeed brightened—startled, she murmured in awe, *"Peach Blossom is truly a divine being! She must not be underestimated."*

Judging the hour ripe for mourning her son, she covered her face and began to wail, her grief mounting until she wept and cried out in earnest. Only at the first watch did she fall silent. Then, taking Shi Zongfu’s shoe, she stepped outside the main gate and struck it once in the center of the threshold. With each blow, she called out, *"Shi Zongfu, my child! Come back quickly—I long for you so! When parents live, a son should not wander far, lest I stand at the gate in vain. I beg you!"*

That day, the author of this tale composed a song to share with you all. Its lyrics read:

At the first watch, the moon hangs low,
In the widow’s chamber, sobs overflow.
She cries, she calls for her son, Shi Zongfu:
*"Oh, my child! My heart’s own marrow!
Where are you now?
I sent you off to trade, yet parted from this wretched mother so.
Here I count the days, my fingers worn with longing,
Never dreaming you’d vanish past the year’s last moon’s fading glow.
Now it’s the third day of the third month—
Oh, my child, how my heart burns with woe!"*

At the second watch, the moon climbs high,
In the widow’s chamber, her wails rise to the sky.
She calls for her son, Shi Zongfu:
*"Why did your fate turn so cruel?
Any death but this—
Never should you have perished in some ruined kiln so cold!
What sin did you commit?
Condemned to wander far, your homeland left behind.
Since Master Zhou foretold your doom, my heart has been carved by a thousand blades.
If you falter now, your mother—half a soul—will be left with no end in sight!"*

At the third watch, the moon stands at its peak,
In the widow’s chamber, her sorrow runs deep.
She calls for her son, Shi Zongfu:
*"What misfortune did you invite?
Now I’ve followed Miss Ren’s method—
But will this way truly work? I cannot know!
If your life is spared and you return,
I’ll burn silver candles high to thank the gods’ grace!"*

Old Woman Shi, heeding Peach Blossom Girl’s instructions, wept for a while, then called out a line. She wailed until the fourth watch before falling silent, ceasing her cries. She returned to the inner hall, preparing to rest her head upon the pillow—yet for now, let us set this aside.

Meanwhile, Shi Zongfu, alone in the ruined kiln, saw the rain gradually clear at the first watch. With nothing to occupy him in the desolate shelter, he hastened his journey. From the fifth watch until the hour of *shen*, the rain poured heavily again. Leaning against the kiln’s crumbling wall, he shut his eyes in exhaustion. Soon, he drifted into a deep slumber, his snores like thunder. That night, the perilous wall teetered on collapse—what would become of his life? We shall see in the next chapter.

**Book Two**

**Chapter Five**
*The Filial Son Escapes Disaster Through Divine Dispensation*
*The Compassionate Mother Reunites with Her Son Through Wondrous Art*

*Poem:*
White clouds still drift o’er autumn’s Han palace halls,
Beacon fires fade, their smoke on lofty towers palls.
Where now the general’s steed and war-worn might?
Wild grasses, idle blooms—all earth is steeped in sorrow’s blight.

Now, Shi Zongfu, still in the ruined kiln, had nearly reached the end of the first watch. Leaning against the wall, his eyes heavy with weariness, he was just about to drift asleep when suddenly he heard someone outside call out: “Shi Zongfu, my child! Come back at once!” Startled, he opened his eyes wide—only to find himself alone in the broken kiln. He listened again, but the voice was gone. Muttering to himself, he thought: *How strange! Just now, someone clearly called my name. Could my mind be addled, and I misheard?*

By then, the rain had ceased. He stepped outside and looked up to see a sky full of bright stars, a slender crescent moon casting faint light, the grass slick as oil. Though he wished to press onward, he knew the road ahead offered no shelter. Reluctantly, he returned to the kiln and sat where he had before, his heart full of doubt: *Could this be my imagination playing tricks? Perhaps my longing for my mother is so intense that my soul, borne on the wind, has conjured this voice by chance. But how could my mother’s call reach me from so far away?*

Lost in thought, he drifted back into a hazy slumber. In his dream, he heard the voice again. “How bizarre!” he thought. “Could I be mistaken once more?” He bolted upright, crying out, “Mother!” Tears streamed down his face. After a moment’s bewilderment, he gave a bitter laugh: *Of course—I must be muddled! If I hadn’t heard my mother’s call in a dream, how could I have ventured into these wilds by night? But here I am, utterly alone. Who knows the hardship of my solitary refuge? Mother… you cannot know your son now lingers in this desolate place.*

That night, his restless thoughts stirred up demonic delusions, leaving his heart even more agitated. "I cannot sleep in peace," he muttered, "and my heart aches for my mother. Why not sit until the fifth watch? When dawn breaks, I can enter the city and see if anyone has truly called for me again." Resolved, he roused his spirits and sat upright for a while, the faint moonlight still lingering.

Just as he sat through the third watch, his eyes grew heavy once more. Then, clear and unmistakable, he heard a voice at his ear: *"Shi Zongfu, my child! Come back at once!"* Overcome, he burst into sobs and cried out, *"Mother! Your son is here!"* *This is surely my mother’s voice—could she truly have come near these outskirts?* With that thought, he rushed outside to search.

No sooner had he stepped beyond the cave’s entrance than a deafening crash erupted behind him, as though heaven and earth were collapsing.

The sight sent Shi Zongfu’s soul scattering beyond the heavens. He whipped around to look—only to see the crumbling cave dwelling collapse in upon itself. With a cry of *"Aiya!"*, his body gave way, and he slumped to the ground. After steadying his spirit for some time, he at last sighed deeply and murmured, *"This old cave, worn by years of wind and rain, must have been soaked through by this downpour. How could its walls have held?"* Truly, it was fated in a past life that Shi Zongfu should have been crushed to death in that ruined shelter tonight—yet by some twist, Peach Blossom Girl had taught Shi’s widowed mother a way to avert the disaster, allowing him to escape this calamity. Though Peach Blossom Girl’s Dao arts reached the divine, it was also by virtue of Shi Zongfu’s filial piety that this karmic cause and effect came to pass.

That night, as Shi Zongfu gathered his wits, he intoned, *"Great Compassionate Savior, Most High Heavenly Worthy!"* Then, struck by the thought, he broke into sobs once more: *"Had I been but a step slower, I would have been buried alive inside! Who knows how many years it would have taken to drag my corpse from the rubble?"*

"How could my mother at home have known? She would have waited in vain for my death—would that not have cost one life and doomed another? And who would have cared for her in her old age?" As he spoke through his sobs, a gust of wind suddenly rushed past, and the watchman’s drum on the city wall struck the fourth watch. Shi Zongfu started, then laughed aloud: "Fool that I am! Heaven has favored me, sparing me from this great calamity—what greater fortune could there be? Tomorrow morning, I will enter the city and see my mother. Then mother and son will talk and laugh together—is that not a joyous thing?" His sorrow turned to joy, and he sat down upon a roadside stone. After a while, the muffled clang of the fifth watch’s gong drifted from the city, filling him with delight. "One more watch," he thought, "and dawn will break. But my belongings are buried in the ruins, and I cannot dig them out now. Fortunately, I have twenty taels of silver on me. Staying here by this ruined shelter is useless—better to hurry to the city gates and wait. When dawn comes and the gates open, I can enter and see my mother." With that, he rose at once, straightened his clothes, and strode swiftly toward Chaoge City, arriving with nothing but his life and empty hands.

Shi Zongfu hastened to the city gate and stood alone, waiting. Soon, the sky brightened, and the gates swung open. He stepped inside and hurried home, his feet moving as swiftly as wings. As he raised his hand to knock, a voice answered from within. Though Shi’s mother had lain down that night, she could not sleep. Fearful that the words of the Duke of Zhou might prove true and Peach Blossom Girl’s false, she tossed and turned until the last watch, when she finally dozed off. Suddenly, the sound of knocking startled her awake. She answered at once, her heart leaping with joy—she knew her son had returned alive. She scrambled up, rushed to the door, and cried out, "My son Shi Zongfu! Have you come home?"

"Your mother has longed for you so desperately!" She hurriedly unlatched the door. Where was this a mere mother and son reunion? It was as if they had been reborn and met again—boundless joy, yet endless sorrow.

Without a word of other matters, Old Woman Shi rushed forward, wrapped her son in her arms, and wept aloud, "My child! Have you truly returned alive?" Seeing his mother speak so strangely, Shi Zongfu could not help but shed tears as well. "Mother, I truly escaped death and returned. Let us go inside, and I will tell you everything slowly." Hearing this, Old Woman Shi pulled her son by the hand, and together they entered the main hall and sat down.

Shi Zongfu then recounted how he had taken shelter from the rain on the road, hidden in the ruined kiln until a certain hour, and how "I heard you call for me three times. When I rushed out of the kiln, it collapsed and nearly crushed me inside. Now all my luggage is buried in the kiln, though fortunately, I had the silver on me." As he spoke, he broke into sobs. Upon hearing this, Old Woman Shi could not help but weep as well. After the two had cried together for some time, she said, "Let us not grieve any longer. The one who saved your life lives right next door. Quickly, come with me to thank Miss Ren!"

Shi Zongfu asked in haste, "Mother, whom are we going to thank for saving my life?" Old Woman Shi then told him how, "Fearing you would not return, I went to Master Zhou to divine your fate. He said that last night at the third watch, you would be crushed to death in the ruined kiln, with no way to escape. I wept bitterly on my way home, but the daughter of our neighbor, Old Man Ren, taught me a way to break the curse—just as I have done—and so saved your life." She pointed to the table, "See the paper horses there? And the eggs, the sieve, the lamp, the robe, the mirror, and the shoes?" Only then, hearing his mother’s words and recalling what had happened in the kiln, did Shi Zongfu realize the truth. "Mother, what you say is entirely correct. It was truly Miss Ren who saved my life. But how can we simply go to her home like this? Wouldn’t others gossip about it?"

"Now your son has twenty taels of silver on him. Take out two or three taels to buy a sheep and a jar of wine to send over—only then will it show our wholehearted gratitude."
